Ruling on Stealing Drums and Musical Instruments
Explains why amputation is not applied for stealing drums and musical instruments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on amputation for stealing musical instruments and distinguish the proofs of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
No amputation for stealing musical instruments because they are valueless (Sahibayn) or due to ta'wil (Abu Hanifa).

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses the hadd of theft (sariqa) when the stolen item is a musical instrument. The Hanafi school agrees that amputation (qat') is not applied, but they differ on the legal cause. The Sahibayn hold that these instruments have no value in Shari'ah, while Abu Hanifa argues that the thief may have a valid excuse (ta'wil) of intending to break the instrument to prevent evil.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
What is being ruled upon? Stealing a musical instrument (duff, drum, barbat, flute). The ruling is that no amputation is applied.
2
Trace the first proof (Sahibayn)
According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, these instruments have no value (la qeema laha) because they are used for prohibited entertainment. Therefore, the theft does not meet the nisab requirement for hadd, and breaking them incurs no liability.
Note: 'Wa alayhi al-fatwa' indicates this is the relied-upon view for fatwa.
3
Trace the second proof (Abu Hanifa)
According to Abu Hanifa, the thief may have a valid excuse (ta'wil) of intending to break the instrument to prevent evil. This creates a shubha (doubt) that prevents the application of hadd.
Shubha in hadd cases lead to dropping the punishment.
4
Understand the practical consequence
Both views agree that no amputation applies. The practical difference is in the reasoning: Sahibayn deny the value, Abu Hanifa affirms value but uses shubha. The text also notes that breaking the instrument incurs no liability (daman) because it has no value.
5
Note the tarjih
The fatwa is on the view of the two (Sahibayn) but the text also says 'al-sahh' (the correct view) is no amputation, indicating the agreed outcome.
The intra-madhhab difference does not change the ruling in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (و دف) وطبل وبربط ومزمار ) لأنها عندهما لا قيمة لها وعليه الفتوى فلا ضمان على من كسرها، وعند أبي حنيفة آخذها يتأول الكسر فيها والدف - بالضم والفتح - الذي يلعب به وهو نوعان: مدور ومربع ؛ كذا في المغرب. والبَربَط - بفتح الباءين الموحدتين ـ وهو العود؛ كذا في الترغيب والترهيب أطلقه فشمل الدف والطبل للغزاة وفيه اختلاف المشايخ والأصح عدم القطع لأن صلاحيته للهو صارت شبهة؛ كذا في غاية البيان
His saying: (And a duff, a drum, a barbat, and a flute) because according to them (the two, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) they have no value, and the fatwa is upon that, so no liability on the one who breaks them. According to Abu Hanifa, the taker of them can be interpreted as intending to break them. The duff (with damm and fath) is that which is played with, and it is of two types: round and square; as in al-Mughrib. Al-barbat (with fatha on both letters) is the oud (lute); as in al-Targhib wa al-Tarhib. This is general, including the duff and drum for warriors, and there is a difference of opinion among the mashayikh, and the correct view is no amputation, because its suitability for amusement creates a shubha; as in Ghayat al-Bayan.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the matn stating that no amputation is applied for stealing a duff (tambourine), drum (tabl), barbat (lute), or flute (mizmar). The commentary presents two views: According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), these instruments have no value (la qeema laha) because they are used for forbidden amusement, so breaking them incurs no liability. Therefore, stealing them does not meet the nisab requirement for hadd. According to Abu Hanifa, the thief may have an excuse (ta'wil) of intending to break the instrument, creating a shubha. The text defines the terms: duff is a frame drum played with, of two shapes; barbat is the oud. The ruling includes even drums used by warriors (for encouragement), and the mashayikh have differed. The correct view (al-sahh) is no amputation, as the instrument's use for idle amusement creates a shubha that the thief might have intended to break it or prevent evil.

Key Terms
دف (duff): Tambourine or frame drum, used for entertainment.
بربط (barbat): A type of lute (oud).
مزمار (mizmar): Flute or reed instrument.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the reason given by the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) for not applying amputation in stealing a duff?
A) Because it is made of wood, not precious metal.
B) Because it has no value (la qeema laha).
C) Because the thief intended to break it.
D) Because it is used for worship.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school does not apply amputation for theft of musical instruments, either due to their lack of value or due to the shubha of the thief's intention.

No Hadd for Betrayal, Plunder, and Snatching
Explains why these acts do not incur hadd amputation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define khiyanah, nahb, and ikhtilas, identify the missing element (secret taking) that excludes them from hadd amputation, and cite the hadith proof.

Core Idea
Amputation requires secret taking from hirz; betrayal, plunder, snatching lack this essential element.

In this lesson, we examine three categories of wrongful taking that, despite being prohibited, do not trigger the hadd punishment of hand amputation. The Hanafi school grounds this ruling on the absence of the rukn (essential element) of theft: secret taking from a secure place (hirz). The primary evidence is an explicit hadith. We will also address an apparent counterexample from the life of the Prophet (peace be upon him).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling on Each Act
The passage states there is no amputation (qat') for khiyanah, nahb, and ikhtilas. Note that these acts are still sinful and punishable by ta'zir.
2
Understand the Definitions
Khiyanah: taking what is in one's possession as a trust (amanah). Nahb: open forcible taking in a public place. Ikhtilas: quick snatching, not necessarily secret.
3
Isolate the Missing Rukn
The rukn of theft (sariqah) is al-akhdh khufyah (secret taking). These three acts lack secrecy: the betrayer already had access; the plunderer takes openly; the snatcher takes quickly but not from a secure hiding place.
4
Cite the Primary Dalil
The hadith in Tirmidhi: 'There is no amputation for a traitor, a plunderer, or a snatcher.' This is a clear (qat'i al-dalalah) text excluding these from hadd.
5
Resolve the Apparent Contradiction
The hadith of 'Aisha (a woman borrowed and denied) is explained by the majority: the cutting was for an actual theft she committed, not for the betrayal itself. Full discussion in Fath al-Qadir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وبخيانة ونهب واختلاس) لانتفاء ركن السرقة وهي الأخذ خفية والخيانة هي الأخذ مما في يده على وجه الأمانة، والنهب هو الأخذ على وجه العلانية والقهر في بلد أو قرية، والاختلاس أن يأخذ الشيء بسرعة والاسم الخلسة. وفي السنن والجامع للترمذي مرفوعاً «ليس على خائن ولا منتهب ولا مختلس قطع (۱) وأما ما في الصحيحين عن عائشة عنها أن امرأة كانت تستعير المتاع وتجحده فأمر النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم بقطعها فأجاب عنه الجماهير بأن القطع كان لسرقة صدرت منها، وتمامه في فتح القدير
His saying: (And [no amputation] for betrayal, plunder, and snatching) because the essential element of theft (sariqah) is absent, which is taking secretly. Betrayal (khiyanah) is taking from what is in one's hand on the basis of trust. Plunder (nahb) is taking openly and by force in a town or village. Snatching (ikhtilas) is taking something quickly, also called khalas. In the Sunan and Jami' of al-Tirmidhi, it is raised: 'There is no amputation for a traitor, a plunderer, or a snatcher.' As for what is in the two Sahihs from 'A'ishah that a woman used to borrow goods and deny them, and the Prophet ordered her hand to be cut, the majority answered that the cutting was for theft that occurred from her. The full discussion is in Fath al-Qadir.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage explains three categories of wrongful taking that do not incur the hadd of amputation: khiyanah (betrayal of trust), nahb (plunder, open robbery), and ikhtilas (snatching). The reason is that the rukn (essential element) of sariqah (theft) is secret taking (akhdh khufyah). In khiyanah, the property is already in the person's possession as a trust (amanah), so there is no taking from a secure place (hirz). Nahb is open and forcible taking, not secret. Ikhtilas is quick but not necessarily secret; it is also not from hirz. The primary proof is a hadith in Tirmidhi: 'There is no amputation for a traitor, plunderer, or snatcher.' Some objected using the hadith of the woman who borrowed and denied (which might seem like khiyanah), but scholars explained that her punishment was for actual theft that occurred from her, not for the betrayal itself.

Key Terms
خيانة (khiyanah): Betrayal of trust; taking property entrusted to one's care for oneself.
نهب (nahb): Plunder; open and forcible taking in a public place.
اختلاس (ikhtilas): Snatching; taking quickly in a manner that is not secret from hirz.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is amputation not applied for betrayal (khiyanah)?
A) Because the value is less than nisab.
B) Because the property was already in the person's possession as a trust.
C) Because it is not a crime.
D) Because the person is a family member.

Key Takeaway
The hadd of theft requires secret taking from hirz; betrayal, plunder, and snatching are excluded by the explicit hadith of the Prophet.

The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Grave Robbing (Nabsh) and the Hadd Punishment
Intra-Hanafi dispute on grave robbing hadd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether grave robbing incurs amputation, explain the proof of Abu Yusuf, and evaluate the counter-evidence of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad using the concept of shubha.

Core Idea
Grave robbing avoids hadd due to shubha in ownership.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: does the grave robber (nabbash) suffer amputation? The matn states no, aligning with Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf dissents, citing a hadith and property protection. We will examine both sides and the reasoning that leads to the mufta bihi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
First, note that Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold no hadd, while Abu Yusuf holds that amputation applies. The matn explicitly states the majority view.
This is an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf, not inter-madhhab.
2
Examine Abu Yusuf's evidence
Abu Yusuf relies on a hadith: 'Whoever robs a grave, we cut his hand' and the principle that the shroud is valuable property protected in a secure place (hirz).
The hadith is not widely accepted as marfu'.
3
Examine the counter-evidence of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad
They cite a hadith: 'No amputation on al-mukhtafi' (the grave robber) and argue shubha in ownership—the deceased's need for the shroud takes precedence over the heir's ownership.
The Medinan term 'mukhtafi' is key.
4
Understand the shubha reasoning
Ownership of the shroud is doubtful because the dead person's need is prioritized, and the heir's ownership is incomplete pending the deceased's needs. This shubha blocks the hadd.
Shubha in ownership is a classic hadd-dropping factor.
5
Note the siyasah alternative
The text concludes that if a hadith supporting amputation is accepted, it applies as a discretionary punishment (siyasah) by the Imam, not as a fixed hadd.
This preserves the ruler's authority without contradicting the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ونبش) أي لا قطع على النابش وهو الذي يسرق أكفان الموتى بعد الدفن، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد. وقال أبو يوسف : عليه القطع لقوله عليه السلام من نبش قطعناه ولأنه مال متقوم محرز مثله فيقطع . ولهما قوله عليه السلام لا قطع على «المختفي وهو النباش بلغة أهل المدينة ولأن الشبهة تمكنت في الملك لأنه لا ملك للميت حقيقة ولا للوارث لتقدم حاجة الميت وقد تمكن الخلل في المقصود وهو الانزجار لأن الجناية في نفسها نادرة الوجود، وما رواه غير مرفوع أو هو محمول على السياسة لمن اعتاده فيقطعه الإمام سياسة لا حداً أطلقه فشمل ما كان القبر في بيت مقفل على الصحيح وما إذا سرق من تابوت في القافلة وفيه الميت لما بينا وما إذا سرق من القبر ثوباً غير الكفن لعدم الحرز . وأشار إلى أنه لو سرق من البيت الذي فيه قبر الميت مالاً آخر غير الكفن أنه لا يقطع لتأوله بالدخول إلى زيارة القبر، وكذا لو سرق من بيت فيه الميت لتأوله بتجهيزه وهو أظهر من الكل لوجود الإذن بالدخول فيه عادة
His saying: (And grave robbing) i.e., no amputation on the grave robber (nabbash) who steals the shrouds of the dead after burial. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf said: Amputation is on him, because of the hadith 'The one who robs a grave, we cut his hand', and because it is valuable property preserved in a secure place, so amputation applies. The proof for the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is the hadith 'No amputation on al-mukhtafi' (which is the grave robber in the language of the people of Medina), and because the doubt (shubha) is established in ownership, since neither the dead nor the heir truly owns it, due to the priority of the deceased's need. Also, the objective (zajr) is impaired because the crime itself is rare in occurrence. The narrated hadith is either not raised (mawquf) or is interpreted as for political discretion (siyasah); the Imam may cut as a political punishment, not as a hadd. This is general, including if the grave is in a locked house according to the correct view, and if the shroud is stolen from a coffin in a caravan containing the dead, and if a cloth other than the shroud is stolen from the grave because it is not a secure place. It is indicated that if one steals property other than the shroud from the house containing the grave, he is not cut due to the interpretation of entering to visit the grave. Similarly, if one steals from a house containing the dead, due to the interpretation of preparing him, and this is the most apparent of all, because permission to enter is customary.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage presents the intra-Hanafi dispute on grave robbing (nabsh). The matn states that no amputation applies to the grave robber (nabbash). Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold this view. Abu Yusuf opposes, arguing that amputation should apply based on a hadith: 'Whoever robs a grave, we cut his hand', and because the shroud is valuable property and the grave is considered a secure place (hirz). The majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) respond with another hadith: 'No amputation on al-mukhtafi' (the grave robber in Medinan dialect). They also argue that there is a shubha in ownership: the deceased's need for the shroud takes priority over the heir's ownership, so the shroud is not fully owned. Additionally, the deterrence objective is weak because the crime is rare. The reported hadith from Abu Yusuf is either not prophetic (mawquf) or is to be applied as a discretionary punishment by the ruler (siyasah), not as a fixed hadd. The commentary extends the ruling to various scenarios: even if the grave is in a locked house, no hadd; if the shroud is stolen from a coffin in a caravan, no hadd; if a cloth other than the shroud is stolen from the grave, no hadd because it is not hirz. If someone steals other property from a house containing a grave, he is not cut because he may have entered to visit the grave. Similarly for a house containing a deceased person before burial.

Key Terms
نباش (nabbash): Grave robber; one who steals shrouds from graves after burial.
المختفي (al-mukhtafi): Grave robber in Medinan dialect.
سياسة (siyasah): Discretionary punishment by the ruler, not a fixed hadd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary argument of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad against applying amputation for grave robbing?
A) The shroud is not valuable.
B) The dead person owns it, not the thief.
C) There is a hadith saying no amputation for al-mukhtafi.
D) The grave is not a secure place.

Key Takeaway
Grave robbing does not trigger the hadd of amputation because a shubha in ownership exists, and the evidence for amputation is either weak or interpreted as discretionary punishment.

Why No Hadd for Stealing Public or Shared Property?
Explains why amputation is not applied for stealing public or shared property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state why the Hanafi school does not apply the hadd of amputation for theft from public or shared property, and distinguish between real partnership and semblance of partnership.

Core Idea
A shared right in property, even a semblance, repels the hadd of theft.

The hadd of amputation is one of the most severe hudud punishments in Islamic law. However, not every act of taking property without permission triggers this punishment. The Hanafi school applies a key principle: when there is any semblance of ownership or partnership (shubhat al-shirkah), the hadd is dropped. This lesson explains this rule using the case of public and shared property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Categories
The passage mentions two types of property: mal al-'ammah (public property, e.g., endowments, mosque funds) and mal mushtarak (shared property between individuals).
2
Understand the Reason for Repelling Hadd
For shared property, the thief has a real share (shirkah haqiqiyyah). For public property, he has a semblance of a share (shubhat shirkah). Both create a shubha (doubt) in ownership, which repels the hadd.
This is based on the principle that hudud are repelled by shubuhat.
3
Consider the Location of Theft
The text adds that if the property is taken from a musalla (the public prayer area), it is like a mosque where entry is permitted, so no hadd. But if taken from a private house, the ruling may differ.
This distinction shows the importance of the place of safekeeping (hirz).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ومال عامة أو مشترك لأن له فيه شركة حقيقة في الثاني أو شبهة شركة في الأول . من لذاته لكن إذا أخذه من مصلاهم لا يقطع لكونه في حكم المسجد يؤذن في دخوله بخلاف أخذه بيت.
His saying: (And the property of the public or shared) because he has a real partnership in the second (shared) or a semblance of partnership in the first (public). From itself, but if he takes it from their prayer place, he is not cut because it is in the ruling of a mosque where entry is permitted, unlike taking it from a house.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage states the ruling that no amputation is applied for stealing public property (mal al-'ammah) or shared property (mal mushtarak). The reason is that the thief has either a real share (in shared property) or a semblance of a share (in public property). This creates a shubha in ownership, like the thief taking what he partially owns. The text specifically mentions that if the property is taken from the place where the public prays (musalla), it is like a mosque where entry is permitted, so no hadd. But if taken from a house, the ruling is different (implies amputation may apply). The full discussion likely continues on the next page but is not present.

Key Terms
مال العامة (mal al-'ammah): Public property, e.g., endowments, mosque funds.
مال مشترك (mal mushtarak): Jointly owned property between two or more individuals.
شبهة شركة (shubhat shirkah): Semblance of partnership; the thief has an indirect right in the property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is amputation not applied when someone steals from public property (mal al-'ammah)?
A) Because the public is not a legal entity.
B) Because the thief has a semblance of partnership (shubhat shirkah).
C) Because the value is distributed among many.
D) Because public property is not considered property.

Key Takeaway
The hadd of amputation for theft is repelled when the thief has a real or semblance of a share in the property, such as in public or jointly owned property.

No Amputation for Theft of Musical Instruments
Ruling that no hand amputation for theft of musical instruments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on theft of musical instruments and explain why shubhah averts hadd amputation.

Core Idea
Hadd amputation requires the stolen item to have legal value; musical instruments lack value, so no cut.

In Hanafi criminal law, the punishment for theft (hadd) is only applied under strict conditions. One such condition is that the stolen property must have legal value (qimah). This lesson examines a specific application: musical instruments like the duff (tambourine), tabl (drum), barbat (lute), and mizmar (flute). According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, these items are considered valueless for the purpose of hadd, so no amputation is prescribed. We will explore the reasoning behind this ruling, including the role of legal doubt (shubhah) and the disagreement between the founding imams.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is theft of a musical instrument. The ruling is that the hadd amputation is not applied.
2
Locate the condition for hadd
The key condition is that the stolen item must have legal value. The passage states these instruments have no value according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.
Value (qimah) is a condition for amputation.
3
Understand the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa held that the thief may be liable for damages but still no amputation. The fatwa follows the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) who say no damages either.
Intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.
4
Recognize the role of shubhah
The possibility that these instruments could be used for permissible purposes (like warrior's drum) creates a doubt that averts the hadd.
Shubhah is a broader principle in Hanafi fiqh for averting hudud.
5
Apply the fatwa hierarchy
The mufta bihi is no amputation and no damages, as stated in the text.
Authority: Zahir al-Riwayah of Sahibayn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ودف وطبل وبربط ومزمار) لأنها عندهما لا قيمة لها وعليه الفتوى فلا ضمان على من كسرها، وعند أبي حنيفة آخذها يتأول الكسر فيها والدف - بالضم والفتح - الذي يلعب به وهو نوعان: مدور ومربع ؛ كذا في المغرب. والبَربَط - بفتح الباءين الموحدتين ـ وهو العود؛ كذا في الترغيب والترهيب أطلقه فشمل الدف والطبل للغزاة وفيه اختلاف المشايخ والأصح عدم القطع لأن صلاحيته للهو صارت شبهة؛ كذا في غاية البيان
His saying: (and a tambourine, a drum, a lute, and a flute) because according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) they have no value, and the fatwa is upon that, so there is no liability on the one who breaks them. According to Abu Hanifa, the taker may interpret breaking them. The tambourine (duff) – with damm and fath – is what is played, and it is of two types: round and square. Thus in al-Mughrib. Al-barbaṭ – with fatha on both baa's – is the lute. Thus in al-Targhib wa al-Tarhib. He made it general to include the tambourine and drum for warriors, and there is disagreement among the shuyukh. The most correct is no amputation because its suitability for amusement creates a doubt. Thus in Ghayat al-Bayan.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement clarifying that no hand amputation is required for theft of musical instruments such as duff (tambourine), tabl (drum), barbat (lute), and mizmar (flute). The basis is that according to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), these items have no legal value (qimah), so no amputation applies. The fatwa follows their view. Even Abu Hanifa, who considered the taker liable for breaking them, does not impose amputation but rather a tort (damages). The passage also mentions that the duff and drum used by warriors are included, but the correct position is no amputation due to the doubt (shubhah) arising from their potential for permissible amusement. This is a typical Hanafi application of the principle that hadd punishments are averted by doubts.

Key Terms
الدف (al-duff): A musical instrument played by hand, round or square, used for entertainment.
البربط (al-barbat): A lute, a stringed musical instrument.
شبهة (shubhah): A legal doubt or ambiguity that averts the application of hadd punishments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on stealing a lute (barbat)?
A) The thief's hand is amputated because it is valuable property.
B) The thief is not amputated but must pay damages.
C) The thief is not amputated and does not pay damages because it has no legal value.
D) The thief is executed for destroying musical instruments.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school does not amputate for theft of musical instruments because they lack legal value and are subject to legal doubt.

No Amputation for Grave Robbery – Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on grave robbery and hadd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on grave robbery and evaluate the intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf, identifying the proofs and the relied-upon ruling.

Core Idea
Grave robbery does not incur hadd amputation due to ownership doubt and a specific hadith.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses a specific intra-Hanafi dispute: whether the grave robber (al-nabbash) who steals shrouds should have his hand amputated. The majority of the Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani) holds that no hadd applies, while Abu Yusuf argues for amputation. Understanding this ikhtilaf reveals how the Hanafi school applies principles of doubt (shubhah) and proof (dalil).

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: no amputation for grave robbery.
2
Identify the opposing view
Abu Yusuf's view: amputation applies, based on a hadith and analogy.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Abu Yusuf's dalil: hadith 'man nabasha qata'nahu' and general principle of theft of protected property.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The majority responds with a counter-hadith ('la qat'a ala al-mukhtafi'), shubhah in ownership, weakness in Abu Yusuf's hadith (not marfu'), and the crime's rarity.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: no hadd amputation, but the ruler may impose ta'zir or siyasah for repeat offenders.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ونبش) أي لا قطع على النابش وهو الذي يسرق أكفان الموتى بعد الدفن، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد. وقال أبو يوسف : عليه القطع لقوله عليه السلام من نبش قطعناه ولأنه مال متقوم محرز مثله فيقطع . ولهما قوله عليه السلام لا قطع على «المختفي وهو النباش بلغة أهل المدينة ولأن الشبهة تمكنت في الملك لأنه لا ملك للميت حقيقة ولا للوارث لتقدم حاجة الميت وقد تمكن الخلل في المقصود وهو الانزجار لأن الجناية في نفسها نادرة الوجود، وما رواه غير مرفوع أو هو محمول على السياسة لمن اعتاده فيقطعه الإمام سياسة لا حداً
His saying: (and grave robbery) i.e., no amputation on the grave robber who steals the shrouds of the dead after burial. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf said: Amputation is required upon him, due to his (peace be upon him) saying: 'Whoever robs a grave, we will amputate him,' and because it is valuable property protected in a safe (muhraz) like other property, so he should be amputated. Their proof (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is his (peace be upon him) saying: 'No amputation for al-mukhtafi' – and he is the grave robber in the dialect of the people of Medina – and because a doubt (shubhah) regarding ownership has taken hold, since the deceased does not truly own it, nor does the heir due to the precedence of the deceased's need, and there is weakness in the objective (deterrence) because this crime is rare in occurrence. What he (Abu Yusuf) narrated is not marfu' or is interpreted as a matter of policy (siyasah); for one who habitually does it, the Imam may amputate him as a policy, not as a fixed punishment (hadd).
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on grave robbery (nabsh). The majority position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad is that no hadd amputation applies for stealing shrouds from a grave. Abu Yusuf disagrees and argues for amputation based on a hadith and the general principles of theft. The response to Abu Yusuf includes: (1) a counter-hadith using the term 'mukhtafi', (2) a doubt (shubhah) in ownership – the deceased does not own the shroud after burial, and the heir's right is subordinate to the deceased's need, (3) the rarity of the crime reduces the need for deterrence. The hadith cited by Abu Yusuf is considered either not marfu' (i.e., not authentically attributed to the Prophet) or is interpreted as a discretionary punishment (siyasah) that the ruler may apply but not as a fixed hadd. Therefore, the relied-upon position is no amputation.

Key Terms
النباش (al-nabbash): One who steals from graves, specifically the shrouds.
المختفي (al-mukhtafi): In Medinese dialect, it means the grave robber.
السياسة (al-siyasah): Discretionary punishment by the ruler, not a fixed hadd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the relied-upon Hanafi position regarding amputation for a person who steals a shroud from a grave?
A) His hand is amputated because it is theft from a locked grave.
B) His hand is not amputated because of doubt in ownership and a specific hadith.
C) He is executed for desecration.
D) He must pay the value of the shroud but no amputation.

Key Takeaway
Grave robbery (nabsh) does not incur the fixed punishment of amputation in Hanafi fiqh due to the presence of doubt (shubhah) regarding ownership and a specific Prophetic exemption.

No Amputation for Theft from Public or Common Property
Ruling on non-amputation for theft from public/common/waqf property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the types of property that avert the hadd of amputation and explain the reasoning based on shubhah (doubt).

Core Idea
Hudud are averted by doubts: public and common property create a shubhah that blocks amputation.

In Hanafi law, the severe punishment of hand amputation for theft is not applied when the stolen property belongs to the public treasury (bayt al-mal), is common property (mushtarak), or is waqf. This lesson explores the rationale: the thief may have a real or constructive share, creating a doubt (shubhah) that blocks the hadd. Understanding this principle is essential for distinguishing cases where the hadd punishment applies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of property
The text lists three types: public property (mal 'ammah, i.e., bayt al-mal), common property (mushtarak), and waqf property. Note the differing reasons for each.
Public and common are explicitly named; waqf is added by extension.
2
Understand the reasoning for each category
For common property, the thief has a real share (shirkah haqiqiyyah). For public property, there is a semblance of partnership (shubhat shirkah) because the thief is a Muslim entitled to support. For waqf, there is no specific owner (la malik).
These reasons all create a doubt (shubhah) that averts the hadd.
3
Apply the principle 'hudud are averted by doubts'
The principle (al-hudud tudra' bi al-shubuhat) is cited as the overarching rule. When any doubt exists about ownership or the thief's right, amputation is not applied.
This is a fundamental Hanafi maxim.
4
Distinguish from cases where amputation does apply
The hadd applies only when the stolen property is privately owned (mal al-ghayr) with clear ownership, no right of the thief, and taken from proper custody (hirz).
Think of a private home or shop.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ومال عامة أو مشترك) لأن له فيه شركة حقيقة في الثاني أو شبهة شركة في الأول وهو مال بيت المال فإنه مال المسلمين وهو منهم، وإذا احتاج ثبت الحق له فيه بقدر حاجته فأورث شبهة والحدود تدرأ بها. وأما مال الوقف فلم أر من صرح به ولا يخفى أنه لا يقطع به لعدم المالك كما صرحوا أنه لو سرق حصر المسجد ونحوها من حرز فإنه لا يقطع معللين بعدم المالك
His saying: (and property of the public or common) because he has a real share in the second (common property) or a semblance of share in the first (public property), which is the property of the treasury (bayt al-mal). It is the property of the Muslims and he is among them. If he is in need, a right is established for him in it to the extent of his need, thus it creates a doubt (shubhah), and legal punishments (hudud) are averted by such doubts. As for waqf property, I have not seen anyone explicitly state it, but it is not hidden that amputation does not apply due to the absence of an owner, just as they stated that if someone steals the mat of a mosque or similar items from a safe place, there is no amputation, because there is no owner.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement explaining that amputation is not applied when the stolen property belongs to the public treasury (bayt al-mal) or is common property (mushtarak). The reason is that the thief has a real or constructive share in such property, creating a doubt (shubhah) that averts the hadd. For bayt al-mal, since the thief is a Muslim and potentially entitled to a share if in need, there is a semblance of ownership. For common property, there is an actual share. The passage also mentions waqf property, stating that amputation does not apply because there is no specific owner (malik) – the mat of a mosque is given as an example. This aligns with the Hanafi principle that hudud are averted by doubts.

Key Terms
مال عامة (mal 'ammah): Public property, specifically the treasury of the Muslims (bayt al-mal).
شبهة شركة (shubhat shirkah): A semblance of partnership in property, sufficient to avert hadd.
بيت المال (bayt al-mal): The public treasury of the Islamic state.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is amputation not applied for stealing a mat from a mosque?
A) Because the mat is not valuable enough.
B) Because there is no specific owner of the mat.
C) Because the thief is poor.
D) Because the mosque is not a secure place.

Key Takeaway
Hudud punishments are averted by any genuine doubt; theft from public, common, or waqf property does not incur amputation due to uncertainty in ownership.

No Amputation for Theft Equivalent to One's Debt
Ruling with conditions and proofs for the hadd exemption.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi conditions for exemption from amputation when a thief steals property equal to a debt, and distinguish between recovery (istifa') and substitution (istibdal).

Core Idea
No amputation if the stolen money matches the debt in type; goods require mutual consent.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains a specific shubhah (legal doubt) that averts the hadd of theft: when a person takes property equivalent to a debt owed to him. The ruling depends on whether the act is considered istifa' (recovery of a right) or istibdal (substitution requiring consent). We'll unpack the Hanafi position and its conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is stealing property equal to a debt. The ruling: no amputation under specific conditions because it is considered recovery (istifa').
This exemption is based on istihsan, not strict qiyas.
2
Isolate the rationale (ta'lil)
The text states it is istifa' (recovery) of his right. This creates a shubhah (doubt) that averts the hadd.
The shubhah is that the thief believed he was taking his own property.
3
Extract the conditions for exemption
The stolen property must be of the same type (jins) as the debt, specifically money (nuqud). Similarity in quantity or quality is not required; even excess creates partnership doubt.
Debt may be deferred; still no amputation due to istihsan.
4
Distinguish excepted cases
If the thief steals goods ('urud) or jewelry, it is substitution (istibdal) requiring mutual consent, so amputation applies.
The passage explicitly excludes goods from the exemption.
5
Note the internal ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf held that no amputation applies even for goods, but the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) restrict the exemption to money. The mufta bihi is the majority view.
The Shafi'i school is more lenient, allowing taking different types based on financial equivalence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ومثل دينه) لأنه استيفاء لحقه. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان الدين مؤجلاً وهو استحسان لأن التأجيل لتأخير المطالبة . والمراد بالمماثلة المثل من حيث الجنس بأن كان من النقود، سواء كان من جنسه حقيقة كأن يكون دينه دراهم فسرق دراهم، أو من جنسه حكماً كأن سرق دنانير في الصحيح ولهذا كان للقاضي أن يقضي بها دينه من غير رضا المطلوب ويضم أحدهما إلى الآخر في الزكاة فخرج ما إذا سرق عروضاً ومنها الحلي فإنه يقطع لأنه ليس باستيفاء وإنما هو استبدال فلا يتم إلا بالتراضي ولم يوجد. وعن أبي يوسف أنه لا يقطع لأن له أن يأخذه عند بعض العلماء قضاء من حقه أو رهناً بحقه. قلنا : هذا قول يستند إلى دليل ظاهر فلا يعتبر بدون اتصال الدعوى به حتى لو ادعى ذلك درئ عنه الحد لأنه ظن في موضع الخلاف وأما المماثلة من حيث القدر فليست بشرط لأنه لو سرق زيادة على حقه لا يقطع لأنه بمقدار حقه يصير شريكاً فيه فيصير شبهة، وكذا المماثلة الوصف حتى لو سرق من جنس حقه أجود أو أردأ ألا يقطع ؛ كذا في المجتبى. وفيه أن ابن أبي ليلى والشافعي يطلقان أخذ خلاف جنس حقه للمجانسة في المالية، وما قالا الأوسع
His saying: (and like his debt) because it is recovery of his right. He made it general to include even if the debt is deferred, and it is istihsan because deferment is for postponing demand. By similarity (mumathalah) is meant similarity in type (jins), i.e., money (nuqud), whether it is of the same type literally, e.g., his debt is dirhams and he steals dirhams, or of the same type by rule, e.g., he steals dinars as is correct (sahih). For this reason, the judge can discharge his debt with them without the consent of the debtor, and one is added to the other in zakat. So excluded is if he steals goods (‘urud) including jewelry; then he is amputated because it is not recovery (istifa’) but substitution, which requires mutual consent and this is absent. From Abu Yusuf that he is not amputated because he can take it, according to some scholars, as settlement of his right or as collateral for his right. We say: This statement is based on a clear evidence but is not considered without being connected to a claim; if he claims that, the hadd is averted from him because it is a misunderstanding in a place of disagreement. As for similarity in quantity, it is not a condition because if he steals more than his right, he is not amputated because to the extent of his right he becomes a partner, thus creating a doubt. Similarly, similarity in description, even if he steals a superior or inferior quality of the same type of his right, he is not amputated. Thus in al-Mujtaba. In it, Ibn Abi Layla and al-Shafi’i allow taking a different type of his right due to financial equivalence, but what they said is broader.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling on the principle that if a person steals property equal to a debt owed to him, he is not amputated because he is recovering his own right. The condition is that the stolen property must be of the same type (jins) as the debt, specifically money (nuqud). If the debt is in dirhams, stealing dirhams is fine; stealing dinars is also considered same type by rule (hukman) because they are both money. If the debt is deferred, still no amputation due to istihsan. However, if he steals goods or jewelry, he is amputated because that is not recovery but substitution requiring consent. Abu Yusuf reportedly said no amputation even for goods because he could take them as settlement or collateral. But the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) reject this unless there is a prior claim. The quantity or quality of the stolen item does not matter: even if he steals more than his debt, no amputation for the excess because to the extent of his debt he is a partner, creating a doubt. The passage also mentions that Ibn Abi Layla and al-Shafi’i are more lenient, allowing taking different types of property due to financial equivalence, but the Hanafi view is narrower.

Key Terms
الاستيفاء (al-istifa'): Recovery or collection of one's due right from the debtor's property.
المماثلة (al-mumathalah): Similarity in type (jins), specifically money, not quantity or quality.
الاستبدال (al-istibdal): Substitution of the debt with a different type of property, which requires mutual consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person is owed 100 dirhams. He steals 100 dirhams from the debtor. According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling?
A) He is amputated because theft is theft.
B) He is not amputated because he is recovering his right.
C) He is not amputated only if he claims it in court first.
D) He is amputated only if the debt is deferred.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the hadd of amputation for theft is averted when a person takes property equal to a debt, but only if the property is of the same type as the debt (money), not goods.

Theft from Public Treasury and Waqf: No Amputation
First exception to amputation: Bayt al-Mal and waqf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on theft from Bayt al-Mal and waqf property, explain the role of shubha (doubt) in averting the hadd punishment, and apply the principle 'al-hudud tudra'u bi al-shubuhat' to similar cases.

Core Idea
Theft from public funds or waqf does not incur amputation because the thief's potential share creates a legal doubt.

The chapter on theft begins with an exception: stealing from the public treasury (Bayt al-Mal) is not subject to amputation. Why? Because the treasury belongs to all Muslims, including the thief. If he is in need, he has a right to a portion, which casts doubt on his criminal intent. This doubt (shubha) triggers the well-known maxim: 'Hudud are averted by doubts.' The commentator extends the same reasoning to endowment (waqf) property, where there is no specific owner. In this lesson, we will unpack the reasoning and see how this ruling exemplifies a key Hanafi principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first exception to amputation
The text begins 'الأول وهو مال بيت المال' — the first exception is property of the public treasury. Identify what is being ruled upon: theft from Bayt al-Mal.
This is the opening of the chapter; the author lists exceptions before stating the general rule.
2
Understand the reasoning: shubha from potential right
The rationale: 'فإنه مال المسلمين وهو منهم، وإذا احتاج ثبت الحق له فيه بقدر حاجته فأورث شبهة'. Because the thief is a Muslim, he has a potential right to the funds if in need. This creates a shubha (doubt) that prevents the hadd.
The principle 'الحدود تدرأ بالشبهات' is invoked.
3
Extend to waqf property
The commentator extends: 'وأما مال الوقف فلم أر من صرح به ولا يخفى أنه لا يقطع به لعدم المالك'. Although not explicitly stated earlier, it is clear that theft from waqf also does not incur amputation due to the absence of a specific owner.
The example of a mosque mat (حصر المسجد) is given: no owner, no hadd.
4
Recognize the underlying principle
Both cases rely on the principle that hadd punishments are averted by doubts. The thief's potential ownership (Bayt al-Mal) or the lack of a private owner (waqf) each create a shubha.
This is a consistent Hanafi position: strict application of hadd requires certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب السرقة الأول وهو مال بيت المال فإنه مال المسلمين وهو منهم، وإذا احتاج ثبت الحق له فيه بقدر حاجته فأورث شبهة والحدود تدرأ بها . وأما مال الوقف فلم أر من صرح به ولا يخفى أنه لا يقطع به لعدم المالك كما صرحوا أنه لو سرق حصر المسجد ونحوها من حرز فإنه لا يقطع معللين بعدم المالك
Book of Theft. The first: it is the property of the public treasury, for it is the property of the Muslims and he is among them. If he is in need, the right to it is established for him to the extent of his need, thus creating a doubt (shubha), and legal punishments are repelled by doubts. As for endowment property (waqf), I have not seen anyone explicitly [state the ruling], but it is not hidden that amputation is not applied for it due to the absence of an owner, as they have explicitly stated: if one steals a mosque's mat or similar items from a secure place, then amputation is not applied, justified by the absence of an owner.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on theft with a ruling statement. It identifies the first exception to the amputation penalty: theft from the public treasury (Bayt al-Mal). The reasoning is that the public treasury is collectively owned by all Muslims, including the thief himself. If the thief is in need, he has a right to a portion of those funds. This creates a legal doubt (shubha) about his criminal intent, and the principle is that punishments are averted by doubts. The commentary then extends the same ruling to endowment (waqf) property, noting that amputation is not applied because there is no specific individual owner. The example of stealing a mosque's mat is given: since the mat belongs to the mosque (waqf), there is no private owner, so the hadd punishment is not carried out.

Key Terms
شبهة (shubha): A legal doubt or uncertainty that prevents the application of a hadd punishment.
بيت المال (bayt al-mal): The public treasury of the Islamic state, collectively owned by all Muslims.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is amputation not applied for theft from the public treasury (Bayt al-Mal)?
A) Because the thief must be poor.
B) Because the property is not considered valuable enough.
C) Because the thief has a potential right to it, creating a doubt (shubha).
D) Because it is not kept in a secure place (hirz).

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school exempts theft from Bayt al-Mal and waqf from amputation because the thief's potential share or the absence of an owner creates a legal doubt that averts the hadd punishment.

Theft by Debtor: The Exception of Same Genus
Explaining the exception for a debtor stealing property of the same genus as his debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on amputation for a debtor who steals property of the same genus as his debt and distinguish it from stealing goods of a different genus.

Core Idea
A debtor who steals property of the same genus as his debt is not amputated; it is recovery, not barter.

This lesson examines a key exception in Hanafi hudud law: a debtor who steals property of the same genus (e.g., currency) as his debt does not face amputation, because he is effectively recovering what is owed. The source passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on this principle, including conditions like deferred debts and the exclusion of goods, and contrasts the Hanafi view with that of Ibn Abi Layla and al-Shafi'i. By studying this, you will understand how the concept of shubha (legal doubt) averts the hadd punishment, and why the Hanafi school insists on genus similarity rather than mere monetary equivalence.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Begin by identifying the core ruling: no amputation (hadd) for a debtor who steals property of the same genus as his debt. This is the exception to the general rule of amputation for theft.
The ruling applies even if the debt is deferred, due to istihsan.
2
Identify the conditions for the exception
Determine what conditions must be met: similarity in genus (both must be currency types, e.g., dirhams or dinars), but not necessarily exact type, amount, or quality.
3
Identify the opposing view
Note the positions of Ibn Abi Layla and al-Shafi'i: they equate different genera (e.g., goods) based on common monetary value, thus no amputation.
4
Analyze the opponent's proof
Their proof is al-mujanasah fi al-maliyyah (commonality in monetary value), making the stolen property similar to the debt.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school rejects this, arguing that taking goods is barter (istibdal), which requires mutual consent, not recovery (istifa'). The text notes the opposing view is 'broader' but maintains the narrower, more cautious approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ومثل دينه) لأنه استيفاء لحقه. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان الدين مؤجلاً وهو استحسان لأن التأجيل لتأخير المطالبة . والمراد بالمماثلة المثل من حيث الجنس بأن كان من النقود، سواء كان من جنسه حقيقة كأن يكون دينه دراهم فسرق دراهم، أو من جنسه حكماً كأن سرق دنانير في الصحيح ولهذا كان للقاضي أن يقضي بها دينه من غير رضا المطلوب ويضم أحدهما إلى الآخر في الزكاة فخرج ما إذا سرق عروضاً ومنها الحلي فإنه يقطع لأنه ليس باستيفاء وإنما هو استبدال فلا يتم إلا بالتراضي ولم يوجد. وعن أبي يوسف أنه لا يقطع لأن له أن يأخذه عند بعض العلماء قضاء من حقه أو رهناً بحقه. قلنا : هذا قول يستند إلى دليل ظاهر فلا يعتبر بدون اتصال الدعوى به حتى لو ادعى ذلك درئ عنه الحد لأنه ظن في موضع الخلاف وأما المماثلة من حيث القدر فليست بشرط لأنه لو سرق زيادة على حقه لا يقطع لأنه بمقدار حقه يصير شريكاً فيه فيصير شبهة، وكذا المماثلة الوصف حتى لو سرق من جنس حقه أجود أو أردأ ألا يقطع ؛ كذا في المجتبى. وفيه أن ابن أبي ليلى والشافعي يطلقان أخذ خلاف جنس حقه للمجانسة في المالية، وما قالا الأوسع
He said: (And similar to his debt) because it is recovery of his right. He made it absolute, so it includes if the debt is deferred — this is istihsan, because deferment is for delaying the demand. What is meant by similarity is likeness in terms of genus, that it is from currencies, whether it is from its genus in reality, like his debt is dirhams and he steals dirhams, or from its genus virtually, like he steals dinars in the correct view. For this reason, the judge may use them to pay his debt without the consent of the debtor, and one is combined with the other in zakat. So excluded is if he steals goods ('urud), including jewelry; then he is amputated because it is not recovery, but rather barter, which is not complete except by mutual consent, and that did not occur. And from Abu Yusuf that he is not amputated because he may take it according to some scholars as payment of his right or as pawn for his right. We said: This is an opinion based on an apparent proof, so it is not considered without connection to the claim; even if he claims that, the punishment is averted from him because it is a presumption in a place of dispute. And similarity in amount is not a condition, because if he steals an excess over his right, he is not amputated because to the extent of his right he becomes a partner in it, creating a doubt. Similarly, similarity in description: even if he steals from the same genus but superior or inferior, he is not amputated. Thus in al-Mujtaba. And in it: Ibn Abi Layla and al-Shafi'i allow taking the opposite genus of his right due to commonality in monetary value, but what they said is broader.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and an inter-madhhab dispute. It clarifies the second exception: a debtor who steals property of the same genus as his debt does not incur amputation because he is effectively recovering what is owed to him. This applies even if the debt is deferred (istihsan, because deferment only delays demand). The similarity required is in genus (e.g., both are currency); exact type (dirham vs dinar) is not necessary. If he steals goods or jewelry, he is amputated because that is not recovery but barter, needing consent. Abu Yusuf reported a view of no amputation, but the text refutes it, noting that his argument relies on a claimant's right, but without actual claim it is insufficient. The condition of similarity is in genus and description (quality) but not amount: if he steals more than his due, he becomes a partner to the extent of his due, creating doubt and averting hadd. The passage then records a dispute: Ibn Abi Layla and al-Shafi'i allow taking a different genus than the debt, citing commonality in monetary value, but the Hanafi position (as held by Abu Hanifa and the majority) restricts it to the same genus. The text notes that the opponents' view is broader (awsa'), implying it may have some merit but the Hanafi view is narrower.

Key Terms
مماثلة (mumathalah): Likeness in genus (jins) between the stolen property and the debt, not necessarily in exact type or amount.
استيفاء (istifa'): Recovery of a right; taking what is owed to oneself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a debtor steals jewelry (a good) of the same value as his debt, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) No amputation because it is recovery of his due.
B) Amputation is applied because jewelry is not of the same genus as his debt (currency).
C) No amputation because the amounts are equal.
D) Amputation if the debt is deferred.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a debtor who steals property of the same genus as his debt is not amputated; the exception does not extend to goods or different genera.

Theft from Waqf Property and the Application of Hadd
Elaborating on the application of amputation for theft from waqf property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a trustee can demand amputation for theft from waqf property, and distinguish between theft of corpus and usufruct.

Core Idea
Amputation for theft from waqf hinges on ownership and beneficiary status, not on absence of an owner.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq resolves an ambiguity in earlier Hanafi texts regarding the application of the hadd of theft to waqf property. It distinguishes between the corpus (raqaba) and usufruct (ghalla), and between waqf for specific individuals and for the general public. The trustee (mutawalli) represents the waqf, and the key is whether an owner or right-holder exists to demand amputation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the stolen property type
Determine whether the stolen item is from the corpus (raqaba) or the usufruct (ghalla) of the waqf. This is the first distinction because the legal ownership differs.
Corpus is legally considered owned by the founder; usufruct is owned by the beneficiaries.
2
Identify the nature of the waqf
Is the waqf for specific individuals (e.g., family) or for the general public (e.g., poor, mosque)? Private waqf has identifiable owners; public waqf is like Bayt al-Mal.
This affects whether the thief may be a beneficiary.
3
Check the status of the thief
If the thief is a beneficiary of a public waqf, amputation is not applied due to shubha (doubt). For private waqf, even a beneficiary may be amputated if they steal from others' shares.
The text specifically mentions the poor person stealing from a public waqf.
4
Determine the trustee's authority
The trustee can demand amputation if he has a valid hand (yad sahiha) over the property. For corpus, this is clear. For usufruct, he holds it as a trust for beneficiaries, so he can demand amputation on their behalf.
The exception is when the waqf is for the general public and the thief is a beneficiary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ظاهر قوله : (وأما مال الوقف الخ) قال المقدسي في شرحه صرحوا بأن متولي الوقف يقطع بطلبه، ذكره في التبيين والفتح ونحوهما، وطلبه إنما هو في الوقف .اهـ. وقال الرملي : صرح ابن ملك في شرح المجمع من بحث الخاص بأنه لو سرق مال الوقف من المتولي يجب القطع وسيأتي في شرح قوله «ولو مودعاً والأصل فيه أن كل من كان له يد صحيحة يملك الخصومة إلى أن قال : فللمالك أن يخاصم السارق ثم قال : ومتولي المسجد ثم قال : فتعتبر خصومتهم في ثبوت ولاية لا عند . هو من حيث الاسترداد وفي حق القطع، فهو صريح فيه ويلوح الفرق بين نحو حصر المسجد وغيرها فتأمل اهـ. ونحوه في حواشي أبي السعود عن شيخه، ولعل الفرق هو أن الوقف باقي على ملك الواقف حكماً الإمام كما يأتي في محله لكن هذا يظهر في رقبة الوقف، أما غلته فلا وعلى هذا فعدم القطع في حصر المسجد لعدم المالك لكونها من غلة الوقف بخلاف رقبة الوقف كما لو وقف على أولاده مثلاً جرى به التعامل من المنقولات وقد صرحوا بأن غلة الوقف ملك المستحقين وأنها أمانة تحت يد الناظر، فعلى هذا يكون للمتولي يد صحيحة عليها فله القطع بها لكن ينبغي عدم القطع فيما لو كان وقفاً على العامة كالوقف على الفقراء فإنه مثل بيت المال إذا كان السارق فقيراً، وأما وقف المسجد فالظاهر أنه ليس كذلك لأنه ليس لأحد تناول شيء من غلته لأنها تصرف في منافع المسجد إلا أن يكون له وظيفة في المسجد.
The apparent meaning of his statement: (And as for waqf property...) Al-Maqdisi said in his commentary: They explicitly stated that the trustee of the waqf can require amputation by his demand, mentioned in al-Tabyin and al-Fath and similar works, and his demand is concerning the waqf. End. And al-Ramli said: Ibn Malik explicitly stated in his commentary on al-Majma', from the discussion of special cases, that if someone steals waqf property from the trustee, amputation is obligatory. And it will come in the commentary on his statement 'And if deposited...' and the principle is that whoever has a valid hand (yad sahiha) possesses the right of litigation... until he said: So the owner may litigate against the thief. Then he said: And the trustee of the mosque. Then he said: So their litigation is considered in establishing the authority... from the perspective of recovery and regarding amputation. So it is explicit therein, and the difference is apparent between something like a mosque mat and other things, so ponder. End. And similar is in the marginalia of Abu al-Su'ud from his teacher. Perhaps the difference is that the waqf remains in the ownership of the founder in a legal sense (hukman al-imam), as will come in its place, but this appears regarding the corpus (raqaba) of the waqf; as for its yield (ghalla), then not. Accordingly, the non-amputation for the mosque mat is due to the absence of an owner, because it is from the yield of the waqf, unlike the corpus. For example, if he made a waqf for his children, it is customary with movable property, and they have stated that the yield of the waqf is owned by the beneficiaries and is a trust under the administrator. On this basis, the trustee has a valid hand over it, so he can require amputation. However, amputation should not be carried out if the waqf is for the general public, like a waqf for the poor, because it is like the public treasury if the thief is poor. As for a mosque waqf, apparently it is not like that because no one may take anything from its yield, as it is spent on the mosque's utilities, unless he has a function (wazifa) in the mosque.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement that elaborates on the application of amputation for theft from waqf property. It resolves earlier ambiguities by distinguishing between the corpus (raqaba) and the usufruct (ghalla) of the waqf. The trustee (mutawalli) represents the waqf. For the corpus (e.g., land or building), the trustee can demand amputation because the waqf is legally considered as still owned by the founder in a sense, and the trustee has the right to litigate. For the usufruct (e.g., rent proceeds or produce), it is owned by the beneficiaries and is a trust in the trustee's hands; so theft from the trustee of usufruct can incur amputation if the waiver is for specific individuals (like the trustee's children). However, if the waqf is for the general public (e.g., for the poor), and the thief is poor, it is treated like Bayt al-Mal, and amputation is not applied because the thief is also a beneficiary. For a mosque waqf, its usufruct is not owned by anyone; it is spent on the mosque, so theft of items like mats (which are from the yield) does not incur amputation because there is no owner. The commentary notes a nuance: if the thief has a function (wazifa) in the mosque, his right might create a shubha.

Key Terms
رقبة الوقف (raqabat al-waqf): The corpus or principal asset of the endowment, distinct from its yield.
غلة الوقف (ghallat al-waqf): The income or produce generated by the waqf asset, owned by the beneficiaries.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when can the trustee of a waqf demand amputation for theft of waqf property?
A) Always, because the trustee represents the waqf.
B) Never, because waqf has no specific owner.
C) For theft of the corpus or usufruct of a private waqf, but not for usufruct of a public waqf if the thief is a beneficiary.
D) Only if the waqf is for the poor.

Key Takeaway
The application of hadd for theft from waqf depends on the type of property and the nature of the waqf, not on the absence of an owner.

Re-theft After Amputation: The Hanafi Position
Intra-Hanafi dispute on repeating hadd for unchanged item

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on whether amputation is repeated when the same unchanged item is stolen again, and contrast the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
If the same item is stolen again unchanged, the hadd is not repeated due to residual shubha; change of item or ownership triggers a fresh hadd.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on the application of the hadd of theft when the same property is stolen a second time after amputation. The relied-upon position (istihsan) holds that amputation is not repeated if the item has not changed, while Abu Yusuf, based on qiyas and a hadith, argues for amputation. We will explore the reasoning of both sides.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the point of dispute
The scenario: a thief steals an item, is amputated, the item is returned, then he steals it again. Does he get amputated again? The Hanafi school is split: Abu Hanifa says no (istihsan), Abu Yusuf says yes (qiyas).
Focus on the condition 'the item has not changed'.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and its basis
Abu Hanifa argues that the first amputation causes the inviolability (isma) of that specific property to fall. Even when returned, a doubt (shubha) remains about its inviolability, preventing a second hadd. This is based on istihsan, preferring to avoid repeating punishment due to the rarity of repeating such a severe offense and the diminished deterrent effect.
Key term: shubhat al-suqut (doubt of the falling of inviolability).
3
State Abu Yusuf's opposing view and its basis
Abu Yusuf applies qiyas: each theft is a separate crime, and the hadith 'If he returns, amputate him' (without qualification) supports amputation again. The second theft is even worse because the deterrent had no effect.
Abu Yusuf's view is a narration (riwayah) from him, not the relied-upon position.
4
Understand the exceptions where amputation is applied
The text clarifies that if the item has changed (physical change like yarn woven into cloth, or conceptual change like ownership transfer via sale and repurchase), then amputation is applied. Change breaks the unity of the property, removing the shubha.
Change can be material or legal (e.g., change of ownership).
5
Apply the ruling to a hypothetical case
Case: A thief steals a silk scarf, is amputated, the scarf is returned. Later, he steals the same scarf again (unchanged). Ruling: No amputation. If the scarf had been dyed or sold, then amputation would apply.
Consider the difference between physical and legal change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وبشيء قطع فيه ولم يتغير وهذا استحسان والقياس أن يقطع وهو رواية عن أبي يوسف لقوله عليه السلام فإن عاد فاقطعوه من غير فصل، ولأن الثانية متكاملة كالأولى بل أقبح لتقدم الزاجر وصار كما إذا باعه المالك من السارق ثم اشتراه منه ثم كانت السرقة. ولنا أن القطع أوجب سقوط عصمة المحل كما يعرف من بعد إن شاء الله تعالى وبالرد إلى المالك وإن عادت حقيقة العصمة بقيت شبهة السقوط نظراً إلى اتحاد الملك والمحل وقيام الموجب وهو القطع فيه بخلاف ما ذكر لأن الملك قد اختلف لاختلاف سببه، ولأن تكرار الجناية فيه نادر لتحمله مشقة الزاجر فتعرى الإقامة عن المقصود وهو تقليل الجناية فصار كما إذا قذف المحدود في القذف المقذوف الأول. قيد بقوله «ولم يتغير» لأنه لو تغير مثل ما لو كان غزلاً فسرقه فقطع فيه فرده ثم نسج فعاد فسرقه فإنه يقطع. وعلى هذا الصوف والقطن والكتان وكل عين أحدث المالك فيه صنعاً بعد القطع لو أحدثه الغاصب ينقطع به حق المالك. وأطلق في التغير فشمل المعنوي كما إذا باعه المسروق منه بعد القطع ثم اشتراه فسرقه لأن تبدل السبب كتبدل العين وذكر الشمني أنه لا يقطع عند مشايخ العراق، وينبغي أن يكون حكم ما إذا باعه المالك فسرقه من المشتري وجوب القطع بالأولى.
He said: (And regarding something for which amputation has been carried out and it has not changed - this is istihsan; by qiyas he would be amputated, and it is a narration from Abu Yusuf because of his statement, peace be upon him: 'If he returns, then amputate him' without separation, and because the second [offense] is complete like the first, rather worse due to the prior deterrent. And it becomes like when the owner sells it to the thief, then buys it back from him, and then the theft occurs. And for us: the amputation caused the inviolability of the property (isma) to fall, as will be known later, and with its return to the owner, although the actual inviolability returns, the doubt of its falling remains, considering the unity of ownership and property and the existence of the cause, which is the amputation, unlike what was mentioned because ownership differed due to different cause. And because repetition of the offense is rare due to enduring the hardship of the deterrent; thus imposing the punishment lacks the objective, which is reducing the offense. So it becomes like when one who has been flogged for slander (hadd al-qadhf) then slanders the same first victim. He restricted it with 'and it has not changed' because if it changed, such as if it was yarn and he stole it, was amputated for it, returned it, then he wove it and returned and stole it, then he is amputated. Similarly, wool, cotton, flax, and any property in which the owner made a new creation after amputation; if the usurper made it, the owner's right is cut off. And he made 'change' absolute, so it includes conceptual change, such as if the victim sold it after amputation, then bought it and stole it, because exchange of cause is like exchange of the object. Al-Shumnuni mentioned that he is not amputated according to the Iraqi shaykhs, and it should be that the ruling if the owner sells it and the thief steals it from the buyer is that amputation is obligatory a fortiori.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the repetition of amputation for stealing the same item after having already been amputated for it and the item returned. The relied-upon position (istihsan) is that the second amputation is not applied if the item has not undergone any change. Qiyas (analogy) would require amputation, and a narration from Abu Yusuf supports that view based on a hadith 'If he returns, amputate him'. However, Abu Hanifa argues that the first amputation causes the inviolability (isma) of that specific property to fall; even if it is returned to the owner, the doubt (shubha) of its inviolability remains, because the ownership and the object are the same, and the cause (theft) is identical. Therefore, the second theft does not warrant a new amputation. This is unlike cases where ownership changes (e.g., sale and repurchase). Furthermore, repeating the punishment is rare and misses the deterrent purpose. However, if the item has undergone physical change (e.g., yarn woven into cloth) or conceptual change (e.g., sold and repurchased), then amputation is applied because the item is considered different. The commentary also notes a nuanced opinion from Iraqi scholars that even conceptual change might not trigger amputation, but a fortiori if the owner sells directly to the thief, then the thief steals from the buyer, amputation is obligatory.

Key Terms
عصمة المحل (ismat al-mahal): The legal inviolability or sanctity of the property that protects it from being subject to hadd again.
شبهة السقوط (shubhat al-suqut): Doubt about the continued existence of inviolability, preventing a second hadd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, if a thief steals the same piece of cloth, is amputated, the cloth is returned, and he steals it again unchanged, what is the ruling?
A) He is amputated again because the hadd applies to each theft.
B) He is not amputated due to the doubt from the previous hadd.
C) He is amputated only if the cloth was woven into a garment.
D) He is not amputated regardless of change.

Key Takeaway
Amputation is not repeated for an unchanged item due to the residual shubha from the first hadd; change of the item or ownership triggers a fresh hadd.

Amputation for Theft of Valuable Non-Currency Items
Listing valuable items for amputation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the specific valuable items for which amputation applies and explain the Hanafi rationale based on value and security.

Core Idea
Amputation applies to any valuable secured property, not just gold and silver.

The text lists items like teak, bamboo, and precious stones, and explains that amputation applies because they are among the most valuable and are not freely available. This extends the principle beyond the traditional currency examples. Understanding this list helps you identify the scope of hudud in theft.

Guided Path
1
Enumerate the listed items
Read the source and note each item: teak (saj), bamboo (qana), ebony (abnus), sandalwood, green gemstones, ruby, peridot, pearls, aloeswood, musk, oils, safflower, saffron, ambergris.
These are all valuable and not freely available in nature.
2
Identify the condition for amputation
The passage states these items are 'muhrazah' (secured) and 'not existing as freely available in their original form'. This is the key condition: the stolen property must be genuinely valuable and not something that can be picked up freely.
Compare with the condition for gold and silver: they are also secured and valuable.
3
Recognize the a fortiori (awlā) reasoning for perfumes
The author says perfumes are included 'bi al-awlā' (a fortiori). This means if wood and stones are included, then perfumes, which are even more valuable, are certainly included.
This shows the principle is based on relative value, not an exhaustive list.
4
Note the exception for unworked ivory
Ivory (ʿāj) does not incur amputation until it is worked into something. Raw ivory is not considered valuable enough; once crafted, it becomes a valuable secured item.
This exception highlights that raw natural materials may be excluded.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ويقطع بسرقة الساج والقنا والأبنوس والصندل والفصوص الخضر والياقوت والزبرجد واللؤلؤ لأن هذه الأشياء من أعز الأموال وأنفسها وهي محرزة لا توجد مباحة الأصل بصورتها في دار الإسلام غير مرغوب فيها فصارت كالذهب والفضة. وفي شرح المختار: لا قطع في العاج ما ليعمل فإذا عمل منه شيء قطع فيه. وأشار المصنف إلى أنه يقطع في العود والمسك والأدهان والورس والزعفران والعنبر بالأولى وفي طلبة الطلبة. قال جار الله العلامة : الساج ضرب من الشجر يعلوه الحمرة وهو صلب كالحجر ولا يكون هذا الأبنوس إلا في بلاد الهند ودور سادات مكة هذا من الساج اهـ. والقنا خشب الرماح جمع قناة وألفها منقلبة عن الواو والأبنوس بفتح الباء معروف وهو معرب. ولم يذكر المصنف
He said: (And amputation is applied for theft of teak (saj), bamboo (qana), ebony (abnus), sandalwood (sandal), green gemstones (fusus al-khudr), ruby (yaqut), peridot (zabarjad), and pearls (lu'lu') because these items are among the most precious and valuable properties, and they are secured (muhrazah), not existing as freely available in their original form in the abode of Islam, so they become like gold and silver. And in Sharh al-Mukhtar: No amputation for ivory (aj) until it is worked, then if something is made from it, amputation applies. And the author indicates that amputation applies for aloeswood (ud), musk (misk), oils (adhhan), safflower (wars), saffron (za'fran), and ambergris ('ambar) a fortiori. And in Tulbat al-Tulbah, Jar Allah al-Allamah said: Teak is a type of tree that has redness on top, hard like stone, and this ebony is only found in the land of India and the houses of the leaders of Makkah; this is from teak. End. Qana is the wood of spears, plural of qanah, its alif is from waw. Abnus is well-known and is Arabized. And the author did not mention
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement listing specific valuable items for which the hadd of amputation applies, even though they are not gold or silver. The ruling is based on their intrinsic high value and scarcity; they are considered akin to precious metals. Items include types of wood, gems, and perfumes. The commentary from Sharh al-Mukhtar notes that ivory does not incur amputation until it is worked, because unworked ivory is not considered valuable enough. Perfume items are included a fortiori (bi al-awla) because they are even more valuable. The passage also provides linguistic definitions: teak is a red hardwood, ebony is from India, qana is spear wood. The author (al-Mawsili or the like) explicitly mentioned these items, and the commentary notes what he did not mention (implied that other similar items follow the same ruling).

Key Terms
محرزة (muhrazah): Secured property; not freely available in nature.
أعز الأموال (a'azz al-amwal): The most precious types of property, justifying the hadd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, for which of the following items is amputation applied for theft?
A) Unworked ivory.
B) Raw cotton.
C) Teak wood.
D) Common soil.

Key Takeaway
Amputation applies to theft of any valuable, secured, non-freely-available property, as exemplified by the listed woods, gems, and perfumes.

The Ruling on Heavy Valuable Items and Hadd
Rejecting the objection that heaviness prevents a valuable item from being subject to hadd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that heaviness does not prevent the application of hadd for valuable items and explain why the objection based on weight is rejected using qiyas and authoritative Hanafi sources.

Core Idea
Heaviness does not negate value; amputation applies to valuable items regardless of weight.

This passage addresses an objection concerning heavy items: does their weight reduce desirability and thus exempt them from the hadd of theft? The Hanafi position is clear—value is not diminished by weight. Al-Hakim in al-Kafi unconditionally applies amputation. Let us examine the objection and its rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Some argue that heaviness reduces desire for an item among the majority, potentially excluding it from 'valuable' items subject to hadd.
The objection is based on subjective desirability, not objective value.
2
State the Hanafi position
Weight does not affect value; amputation applies if the item is otherwise valuable (mal mutaqawwim).
This is the mufta bihi view, supported by al-Hakim and al-Fath.
3
Analyze the rebuttal from al-Fath
Heaviness does not contradict value nor decrease it; the example of a heavy load of fabric shows that amputation would still apply.
This rebuttal uses qiyas: heavy and light items of equal value are treated the same.
4
Evaluate the counter-response
Some tried to limit the ruling by a qualifier in al-Hidayah ('the heavy of it'), but the commentator finds this weak (fihi nazar zahir).
The counter-response is dismissed because it would lead to arbitrary exemptions.
5
Confirm the mufta bihi
Amputation applies for valuable items regardless of weight.
This is the established Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فلو كان ثقيلاً الخ) قال في الفتح : ونظر فيه بأن ثقله لا ينافي ماليته ولا ينقصها وإنما ثقل فيه رغبة الواحد لا الجماعة، ولو صح هذا امتنع القطع في فردة حمل من قماش ونحوه وهو منتف ولذا أطلق الحاكم في الكافي القطع اهـ وأجاب بعضهم بأنه إنما يرد لو لم يقل في الهداية لأن الثقيل منه فمع التقييد بقوله منه لا يرد اهـ. وفيه نظر ظاهر
He said: (So if it is heavy...) He said in al-Fath: And he objected to it that its weight does not contradict its value nor decrease it, but rather the weight causes desire in one person not the group. And if this were accepted, then amputation would be prevented for a single load of fabric and the like, which is false. Therefore, al-Hakim in al-Kafi unconditionally applied amputation. End. And some responded that this objection only applies if it were not said in al-Hidayah 'because the heavy of it', so with the qualification 'of it', the objection does not apply. End. And there is apparent consideration in this.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement addressing an objection about heavy items. The objection claimed that an item's heaviness could reduce the desire for it (people may not want it because it's heavy), thus potentially excluding it from the category of valuable items subject to hadd. The commentator refutes this by noting that heaviness does not negate value; for example, a heavy load of fabric is still valuable. Al-Hakim in al-Kafi unconditionally applied amputation for such items. A responder attempted to reconcile the objection by pointing to a qualifier in al-Hidayah that says 'the heavy of it' meaning only some heavy items might be exempt, but the commentator finds this response weak (fihi nazar zahir). The relied-upon position is that amputation applies regardless of weight if the item is otherwise valuable.

Key Terms
ثقيل (thaqil): Heavy in weight, potentially affecting the item's desirability but not its value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, because heavy items are not desired by most people.
B) No, because weight does not negate value; amputation is applied.
C) Only if the item is very heavy.
D) Only if the item is made of metal.

Key Takeaway
Heaviness does not exclude a valuable item from hadd; amputation is applied solely based on value.
