كتاب السرقة

وزوج سيده ومكاتبه وأخته وصهره ومن مغنم وحمام وبيت أذن في دخوله لم يقطع) لوجود الشبهة في كل واحد منها، أما إذا سرق من قريبه المحرم فللدخول في الحرز مع البسوطة في المال في الأصول والفروع والمراد من السرقة منه السرقة من بيته . أطلقه فشمل ما إذا سرق ماله أو مال غيره لأن بيته ليس بحرز في حقه مطلقاً، واحترز به عما إذا سرق مال محرمه من بيت غيره فإنه يقطع لوجود الحرز ، وينبغي أن لا يقطع لما في القطع من القطيعة فيندرئ ؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وقد يقال ليس القطع حقه وإنما هو حق الشرع فلا يكون قطيعة وينبغي أن لا يقطع في الولاد لما ذكرنا من الشبهة في ماله فعدم القطع في الولاد للشبهة لا لعدم الحرز وفي المحارم لعدم الحرز واحترز بقوله «لا برضاع» عن المحرم الذي محرميته بالرضاع كابن العم الذي . هو . أخ من الرضاع فإنه رحم محرم لا من جهة القرابة وإنما محرميته من جهة الرضاع. فإذا سرق من بيته قطع كما إذا سرق من الرحم فقط، و به اندفع ما في التبيين من أنه لا حاجة إلى إخراجه لأنه لم يدخل في ذي الرحم المحرم اهـ. ظناً منه أنه متعلق بالرحم وليس كذلك بل متعلق بالمحرم كما علمت، وأما إذا سرق أحد الزوجين من الآخر أو العبد من سيده أو من امرأة سيده أو زوج سيدته فلوجود الإذن بالدخول عادة فانعدم الحرز أطلق في الزوجين فشمل الزوجية وقت السرقة فقط بأن سرق منها ثم أبانها وانقضت عدتها ثم ترافعا فلا قطع والزوجية بعدها كما إذا سرق من أجنبية ثم تزوجها ثم ترافعا فلا قطع ولو بعد القضاء، وكذا عكسه لوجود الشبهة قبل الإمضاء. وشمل الزوجية من وجه كما إذا سرق من مبتوته في العدة أو سرقت هي لوجود الخلطة بخلاف ما إذا سرق منها بعد الانقضاء فإنه يقطع . والحاصل أن في باب السرقة يكتفى بوجود الزوجية في حالة من الأحوال قبل القطع لسقوطه، وفي باب الرجوع في الهبة لا بد من قيام الزوجية وقت الهبة فلو حدثت بعدها فالرجوع ثابت، وفي الوصية الاعتبار لها حالة الموت لا غير . وشمل ما إذا سرق أحدهما من حرز لا يسكنان فيه لوجود البسوطة بينهما في الأموال عادة والعبد في هذا ملحق بمولاه حتى لا يقطع في سرقة لا يقطع فيها المولى كالسرقة من أقارب المولى وغيرهم لأنه مأذون له بالدخول عادة في بيت مولاها لإقامة المصالح.
وأطلقه فشمل القن والمكاتب لأنه قن ما بقي عليه درهم والمأذون له في التجارة، وأما إذا سرق من مكاتبه فإن له حقاً في إكسابه ولذا لا يجوز له أن يتزوج أمة مكاتبه. وأما إذا سرق من ختنه ومن صهره فالمذكور هنا قول الإمام، وعندهما يقطع لأنه لا شبهة في ملك الختن لأنها تكون بالقرابة ولا قرابة وله أن العادة قد جرت بالبسوطة في دخول بعضهم منازل بعض بلا استئذان فتمكنت الشبهة في الحرز والمحرمية بالمصاهرة كالمحرمية بالرضاع، وعلى هذا الخلاف إذا سرق من كل من يحرم عليه بالمصاهرة ومحل الاختلاف ما إذا لم يجمعهما منزل واحد، أما إذا جمعهما منزل واحد فلا قطع اتفافاً؛ كذا في شرح الطحاوي. وسيأتي في باب الوصية للأقارب وغيرهم أن الأصهار كل ذي رحم محرم من امرأته، والأختان زوج كل ذي رحم محرم منه . وأما إذا سرق من المغنم فإن له فيه نصيباً كما أفتى به الله عنه مع أن المصنف قد قدم أنه لا قطع في المال المشترك، فالظاهر من إعادته
علي رضي أنه لا قطع وإن لم يكن له حق في الغنيمة وبحث في غاية البيان بأنه ينبغي أن يكون المراد من السارق من الغنيمة من له نصيب في الغنيمة في الأربعة الأخماس أو في الخمس كالغانمين أو اليتامى والمساكين، أما غيرهم فلا نصيب له في الغنيمة فينبغي أن يقطع بخلاف السارق من بيت المال فإنه معد لمصالح عامة المسلمين وهو منهم إلا أن يقال إن مال الغنيمة مال مباح في الأصل فلا قطع بسرقته حيث كان على صورته ولم يتغير، وسواء كان السارق حراً أو عبداً. وأما إذا سرق من الحمام أو بيت أذن للناس في الدخول فيه فلاختلال الخرز بالإذن في الدخول. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا سرق من الحمام وصاحبه عنده أو المسروق تحته بخلاف ما إذا سرق من المسجد وصاحبه عنده فإنه يقطع والفرق على الظاهر أن الحمام بني للإحراز
.
(والمحرمية بالمصاهرة كالمحرمية بالرضاع انظر ما معنى هذا الكلام هنا فإن المحرم بالرضاع يقطع كما تقدم .
Book of Theft. His wife's husband and her master and her maktub and his sister and his father-in-law and from spoils and a bathhouse and a house permission was given to enter it, the hand is not cut. Due to the presence of shubha in each of them. As for if he steals from his close relative (mahram), due to entry into the hirz with familiarity in property in the ascendants and descendants, and what is meant by stealing from him is stealing from his house. He made it absolute, so it includes whether he steals his own property or someone else's property because his house is not a hirz regarding him absolutely. And he restricted by it to when he steals the property of his mahram from another's house, then he is cut due to existence of hirz. And it should be that he is not cut due to what is in the cutting from severance of ties, so it is repelled. Like this in Fath al-Qadir. And it may be said: the cut is not his right, rather it is the right of the Shari'ah, so there is no severance. And it should be that he is not cut in the case of a child due to what we mentioned of shubha in his property, so the absence of cutting in the case of a child is due to shubha, not due to absence of hirz. And in the case of mahrams, it is due to absence of hirz. And he restricted by his saying 'not by rada' to the mahram whose mahramiyyah is by rada' like a paternal cousin who is a brother by rada', for he is a mahram womb relative not by lineage, but his mahramiyyah is by rada'. So if he steals from his house, he is cut, just as if he steals from a womb relative only. And by this is repelled what is in al-Tabyin that there is no need to exclude him because he did not enter into 'dhi rahim mahram'. He thought that it is related to the womb, but it is not so; rather it is related to the mahram, as you know. And as for if one of the spouses steals from the other, or a slave from his master, or from his master's wife, or his master's husband, then due to the existence of permission to enter customarily, so hirz is absent. He made absolute in the spouses, so it includes the spousal relationship only at the time of theft: if he steals from her, then divorces her and her iddah expires, then they bring it to court, there is no cutting, and the spousal relationship after it, just as if he steals from a foreign woman then marries her then they bring it to court, there is no cutting even after judgment, and similarly the opposite, due to existence of shubha before execution. And it includes spousal relationship in one aspect: when he steals from his wife in iddah, or she steals, due to existence of mixing; unlike if he steals from her after expiration, then he is cut. The conclusion is that in the chapter of theft, the existence of spousal relationship at any state before the cutting suffices for its dropping, while in the chapter of return in gift, the spousal relationship must exist at the time of the gift; if it occurs after it, the return is established. And in bequest, consideration is only at the time of death. And it includes if one of them steals from a hirz in which they do not reside, due to existence of familiarity in property customarily. And the slave in this is attached to his master, such that he is not cut in a theft in which the master is not cut, like theft from the master's relatives and others, because he is permitted to enter customarily into his master's house to attend to needs. And he made it absolute, so it includes the qinn and the maktub because he is a qinn as long as a dirham remains on him, and the one permitted for trade. And as for if he steals from his maktub, he has a right in his earnings, and therefore he may not marry the slave-girl of his maktub. And as for if he steals from his son-in-law and from his father-in-law, what is mentioned here is the statement of Imam Abu Hanifah, and according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) he is cut because there is no shubha in ownership of the son-in-law because it is not by lineage, and he has that custom has prevailed of familiarity in entering one another's homes without permission, so shubha in hirz is established, and mahramiyyah by marriage is like mahramiyyah by rada'. And upon this is the difference if he steals from everyone who is mahram to him by marriage, and the point of difference is when they do not share a single house; if they share a single house, then there is no cutting unanimously. Like this in Sharh al-Tahawi. And it will come in the chapter of bequest for relatives and others that 'as-har' (in-laws) are every dhi rahim mahram from his wife, and 'akhtan' (sons-in-law) are the husbands of every dhi rahim mahram from him. And as for if he steals from the spoils, he has a share in it as was given by Allah, and with that the author has presented that there is no cutting in shared property. So the apparent from his repetition is that there is no cutting even if he has no right in the spoils. And it is discussed in Ghayat al-Bayan that it should be that what is meant by the thief from spoils is the one who has a share in the spoils in the four-fifths or in the one-fifth, like the ghazis or orphans and poor. As for others, they have no share in the spoils, so they should be cut, unlike the thief from the treasury (bayt al-mal) because it is prepared for the general interests of the Muslims, and he is one of them. Unless it is said that the property of spoils is originally mubah, so there is no cutting for its theft as long as it is in its original state and has not changed. And it is equal whether the thief is free or slave. And as for if he steals from a bathhouse or a house that people were given permission to enter, due to the violation of hirz by the permission to enter. He made it absolute, so it includes if he steals from the bathhouse and its owner is with him, or the stolen item is under him, unlike if he steals from a mosque and its owner is with him, then he is cut. And the difference apparently is that the bathhouse was built for (public) use, not for safeguarding. The mahramiyyah by marriage is like mahramiyyah by rada'; look at the meaning of this statement here, for the mahram by rada' is cut as has preceded.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explaining the line from Kanz al-Daqa'iq which lists categories of thieves whose hands are not amputated due to shubha (legal ambiguity). The matn states that the hand is not cut for: the wife's husband (i.e., the husband of one's wife's mother? Actually 'zawj sayyidih' means the husband of one's master? The text is complex — it refers to various relationships where there is either familiarity in the home or a right in the property, creating a shubha that prevents the hadd. The commentator explains each category in detail, following the order of the matn. The first category is stealing from a close relative (mahram), where it is said the hand is not cut because the thief has access to the property by virtue of kinship and familiarity. However, if the mahram steals the property of another from a house that is not his own, he is cut because the hirz (secure place) is intact. The commentator notes a difference of opinion regarding the rationale: some say it is to avoid severing family ties, while others say the cut is God's right, so no severance. The passage then discusses the case of a child (walad) stealing from parents due to shubha in property meaning the child has a claim in the parents' wealth. For mahrams by rada' (foster relationships), the ruling is different: if one steals from their foster relative's house, they are cut because the foster relationship does not grant the same access. The text then addresses stealing between spouses: due to mutual permission to enter each other's homes, the hirz is compromised, so no cutting. This applies even if they later divorce or if the marriage occurs after the theft but before judgment. The slave is treated like the master because the slave is permitted to enter the master's house. For the maktub (slave with a contract of manumission), the master has a right in his earnings, so stealing from him creates a shubha. For the khatan (son-in-law) and sihr (father-in-law), Imam Abu Hanifah holds the hand is not cut due to customary familiarity, while the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold it is cut because there is no shubha in ownership. They also differ on mahramiyyah by marriage being like mahramiyyah by rada' — but the commentator notes that for rada', the thief is cut, so the analogy is problematic. Stealing from spoils (ghanimah) is exempt because the property is shared (musharakah), and the thief may have a share. From a bathhouse or a house where entry is permitted publicly, the hirz is breached, so no cutting. The commentary ends with a note on the difference between a bathhouse and a mosque: in a mosque, if the owner is present, the hand is cut because the mosque is not built for storage. Overall, the passage demonstrates how shubha (legal ambiguity) in either the hirz or the property ownership prevents the application of the hadd punishment.

Key Terms
ختن (khatan): Son-in-law; the husband of one's daughter or sister.
صهر (sihr): Father-in-law or any in-law by marriage.
غنيمة (ghanimah): Spoils of war taken from the enemy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi school position, what is the ruling when a person steals from his son-in-law's house (separate residence)?
A) The hand is cut because there is no blood relation.
B) The hand is not cut due to customary familiarity.
C) The hand is cut unless they live together.
D) The hand is not cut only if the son-in-law gave permission.

Key Takeaway
Customary familiarity between in-laws creates shubha in hirz, exempting from amputation (Imam's view), but Sahibayn disagree; spoils and public places are exempt due to shared ownership or lack of hirz.

Theft and Family Ties: Shubha as a Barrier to Hadd
Ruling on theft between spouses and relatives, with shubha analysis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why shubha from marital or family ties prevents the hadd of amputation in Hanafi law, and distinguish the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding theft from in-laws.

Core Idea
Marriage or close family ties create a shubha that drops the hadd of amputation, even if the relationship exists only before execution.

Theft is normally punished by hand amputation, but the Hanafi school recognizes that certain relationships create a legal doubt (shubha) that suspends this hadd. This lesson analyzes how marital and familial bonds—whether by blood, marriage, or even the custom of mutual entry—can prevent the penalty. We will also examine an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether in-laws qualify for this protection, culminating in the operative position for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scope of shubha in marriage
Understand that the existence of a marital relationship at any time before execution drops the hadd, even if the theft occurred before the marriage. The key is that shubha exists at the time of execution, not necessarily at the time of theft.
This applies even if the marriage occurred after the theft but before the judgment is executed.
2
Recognize the role of 'basuta' (customary mutual entry)
The concept of basuta extends shubha to relatives who customarily enter each other's homes without permission, even if they do not reside together. This creates a doubt in the enclosure (hirz) and thus drops the hadd.
Basuta is a form of 'urf that the Hanafi school uses to establish shubha.
3
Apply the ruling to slaves and special cases
A slave's status mirrors his master: if the master would not be amputated for stealing from a relative, the slave also is not amputated. Similarly, stealing from a mukatab involves a shubha because the master has a right in the mukatab's earnings.
The master cannot marry the mukatab's female slave, indicating a right in property.
4
Analyze the intra-madhhab dispute on in-laws
Compare Imam Abu Hanifa's view (no amputation for theft from son-in-law or brother-in-law due to basuta/shubha) with the Sahibayn's view (amputation because no blood relation and no share in property). Identify the differing proofs: custom vs. absence of ownership shubha.
If they share a house, all agree on no amputation.
5
Distinguish between theft, gift revocation, and wills
In theft, the marital relationship at any time before execution suffices. In gift revocation, the relationship must exist at the time of the gift. In wills, the relationship at the time of death is the only consideration. This shows how the timing requirement varies by area of law.
This distinction is crucial for applying the correct rule in different legal contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أجنبية ثم تزوجها ثم ترافعا فلا قطع ولو بعد القضاء، وكذا عكسه لوجود الشبهة قبل الإمضاء. وشمل الزوجية من وجه كما إذا سرق من مبتوته في العدة أو سرقت هي لوجود الخلطة بخلاف ما إذا سرق منها بعد الانقضاء فإنه يقطع . والحاصل أن في باب السرقة يكتفى بوجود الزوجية في حالة . من الأحوال قبل القطع لسقوطه، وفي باب الرجوع في الهبة لا بد من قيام الزوجية وقت الهبة فلو حدثت بعدها فالرجوع ثابت، وفي الوصية الاعتبار لها حالة الموت لا غير . وشمل ما إذا سرق أحدهما من حرز لا يسكنان فيه لوجود البسوطة بينهما في الأموال عادة والعبد في هذا ملحق بمولاه حتى لا يقطع في سرقة لا يقطع فيها المولى كالسرقة من أقارب المولى وغيرهم لأنه مأذون له بالدخول عادة في بيت مولاها لإقامة المصالح. وأطلقه فشمل القن والمكاتب لأنه قن ما بقي عليه درهم والمأذون له في التجارة، وأما إذا سرق من مكاتبه فإن له حقاً في إكسابه ولذا لا يجوز له أن يتزوج أمة مكاتبه. وأما إذا سرق من ختنه ومن صهره فالمذكور هنا قول الإمام، وعندهما يقطع لأنه لا شبهة في ملك الختن لأنها تكون بالقرابة ولا قرابة وله أن العادة قد جرت بالبسوطة في دخول بعضهم منازل بعض بلا استئذان فتمكنت الشبهة في الحرز والمحرمية بالمصاهرة كالمحرمية بالرضاع، وعلى هذا الخلاف إذا سرق من كل من يحرم عليه بالمصاهرة ومحل الاختلاف ما إذا لم يجمعهما منزل واحد، أما إذا جمعهما منزل واحد فلا قطع اتفافاً؛ كذا في شرح الطحاوي. وسيأتي في باب الوصية للأقارب وغيرهم أن الأصهار كل ذي رحم محرم من امرأته، والأختان زوج كل ذي رحم محرم منه . وأما إذا سرق من المغنم فإن له فيه نصيباً كما أفتى به الله عنه مع أن المصنف قد قدم أنه لا قطع في المال المشترك، فالظاهر من إعادته علي رضي أنه لا قطع وإن لم يكن له حق في الغنيمة وبحث في غاية البيان بأنه ينبغي أن يكون المراد من السارق من الغنيمة من له نصيب في الغنيمة في الأربعة الأخماس أو في الخمس كالغانمين أو اليتامى والمساكين، أما غيرهم فلا نصيب له في الغنيمة فينبغي أن يقطع بخلاف السارق من بيت المال فإنه معد لمصالح عامة المسلمين وهو منهم إلا أن يقال إن مال الغنيمة مال مباح في الأصل فلا قطع بسرقته حيث كان على صورته ولم يتغير، وسواء كان السارق حراً أو عبداً. والمحرمية بالمصاهرة كالمحرمية بالرضاع انظر ما معنى هذا الكلام هنا فإن المحرم بالرضاع يقطع كما تقدم .
A [foreign] woman then he married her then they litigated: no amputation, even after judgment. Likewise its opposite due to existence of shubha before execution. And it includes partial marriage, like when he steals from his wife in her 'idda or she steals due to existing mixing; unlike if he steals from her after the 'idda expires, then he is amputated. The conclusion: in the chapter of theft, the existence of marriage at any state before amputation suffices for dropping it. In the chapter of revocation of gift, the existence of marriage at the time of gift is necessary; if it occurs after, revocation is established. In the will, consideration is for the state of death only. And it includes when one of them steals from an enclosure they do not reside in, due to the usual exchange between them in wealth. The slave in this is attached to his master, so he is not amputated for theft for which the master is not amputated, like theft from the master's relatives and others, because he is usually permitted to enter his master's house for fulfilling needs. And he made it absolute, so it includes the qinn and the mukatab because he is a qinn as long as a dirham remains [to be paid], and the one permitted to trade. As for when he steals from his mukatab, he has a right in his earnings, therefore he is not allowed to marry the female slave of his mukatab. As for when he steals from his son-in-law or from his brother-in-law: here is the statement of Imam [Abu Hanifa]; according to them [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] he is amputated because there is no shubha in the ownership of the son-in-law, as it is by kinship, and there is no kinship. And he [Abu Hanifa] says that custom has developed of exchange in entering each other's houses without permission, so the shubha is established in the enclosure. And the mahramiyya by marriage is like the mahramiyya by suckling. Upon this is the dispute if he steals from anyone who is mahram by marriage. The locus of difference is when they do not share a single house; if they share a single house, then definitely no amputation; as in Sharh al-Tahawi. And it will come in the chapter of wills for relatives and others that the 'ashār are every relative mahram from his wife, and the 'akhtān are the husband of every relative mahram from him. As for if he steals from the booty: he has a share in it, as Allah has given the verdict about it, even though the author has earlier presented that there is no amputation in jointly owned property. The apparent from his repetition is that there is no amputation even if he has no right in the booty. And a discussion in Ghayat al-Bayan that it should be understood from the thief from booty is the one who has a share in the booty in the four-fifths or in the fifth, like the participants or orphans and the poor; as for others, they have no share in the booty, so it is appropriate that he be amputated, unlike the thief from the public treasury, for it is prepared for the public interests of the Muslims, and he is among them, unless it is said that the booty wealth is originally permissible property, so there is no amputation for stealing it as long as it remains in its form unchanged. And it is the same whether the thief is free or a slave. And the mahramiyya by marriage is like the mahramiyya by suckling: see what the meaning of this statement is here, for the mahram by suckling is amputated, as has preceded.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling statement with legal reasoning on the effect of marital and family relationships on the hadd of theft. The author explains that the shubha (doubt) arising from marriage or close family ties prevents the amputation of the hand, even if the relationship exists only at some point before the execution of the penalty. The key principle: in theft, the existence of a marital relationship at any time before the actual cutting suffices to drop the hadd, whereas in revocation of gifts, the relationship must exist at the time of the gift, and in wills, only at the time of death. The text then discusses specific cases: theft from a wife during idda, theft between spouses when they do not live together but still have mutual access, and the slave's status is attached to his master's. A significant intra-Hanafi dispute is presented regarding theft from a son-in-law (khatan) or brother-in-law (sihr): Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the usual custom of mutual entry creates shubha, so no amputation; the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree, arguing no shubha in ownership because the relationship is not by blood. The author notes that if they share a house, all agree on no amputation. The passage also covers theft from booty (maghnam) and the public treasury (bayt al-mal), where the existence of a share in the property creates shubha even if the thief is not a direct participant. The final note questions the earlier equation of mahramiyya by marriage with that by suckling, since the latter does not prevent amputation as previously stated. This commentary guides the student to understand the role of shubha as a grounds for dropping the hadd, and the detailed application of this principle across different relationships.

Key Terms
شبهة (shubha): A doubt or ambiguity that creates a legal excuse, preventing the application of the hadd penalty.
بسوطة (basuta): The usual exchange and mutual entry between people, especially relatives and in-laws, without seeking permission.
ختن (khatan): Son-in-law; the husband of one's daughter or sister.
صهر (sihr): Brother-in-law; a relative by marriage, such as the wife's brother or the sister's husband.
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has made a contract of manumission (kitabah) and is paying for his freedom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) He is not amputated only if they live in the same house.

Key Takeaway
Marriage or close family ties create a legal doubt (shubha) that drops the hadd of amputation for theft, even if the relationship exists only before execution, and the Hanafi school applies this principle broadly, including through custom for in-laws.

Theft from Bathhouses and Public Places: The Concept of Hirz
Ruling on theft from places with public access and the conditions for amputation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which theft from a public-access place triggers the hadd of amputation, and distinguish when a place qualifies as a hirz.

Core Idea
A place's openness does not automatically negate hirz; time, guardian presence, and type of property determine amputation.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the rules of theft (sariqa) from places where public entry is permitted, such as bathhouses, mosques, and shops. The key concept is hirz—a secure enclosure—which is not simply about walls but about the custom and purpose of the place. Understanding how hirz operates in these contexts is critical for correctly applying the hadd penalty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the place and its purpose
Determine if the location is built for securing goods (e.g., stable, shop) or for service/ritual (e.g., bathhouse, mosque). This affects whether it is considered a hirz by default.
A bathhouse is built for service, not for securing goods, so it is not a hirz by itself.
2
Assess public permission and time
Check if public access is general and at what times. Permitted entry during daytime may disrupt hirz, but at night when entry is not allowed, the place may become a hirz.
Theft from a bathhouse at night warrants amputation per al-Tabyin.
3
Consider the presence of a guardian (hafidh)
A guardian who is present can make even a non-hirz location (like a mosque or open land) count as secure. His presence substitutes for a physical hirz.
In a mosque, if the owner is present, he acts as a guardian and amputation applies.
4
Examine the method of theft
Distinguish between a thief who picks a lock (fashshash) or one who distracts (qaffaf). These may not involve breaching a hirz if the place is open or the owner is tricked.
A qaffaf is not amputated because there is no breach of hirz; it is a trick.
5
Apply the hirz rule to the specific property
Check if the hirz is appropriate for the type of item. Al-Tahawi held that the hirz must match the item (e.g., stable for a horse, not for a pearl). The madhhab follows al-Sarakhsi that the hirz of a type covers all items in that category.
Stealing a pearl from a stable: al-Tahawi says no amputation; al-Sarakhsi says yes because a stable is a hirz for the type 'goods kept there'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إذا سرق من الحمام أو بيت أذن للناس في الدخول فيه فلاختلال الخرز بالإذن في الدخول. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا سرق من الحمام وصاحبه عنده أو المسروق تحته بخلاف ما إذا سرق من المسجد وصاحبه عنده فإنه يقطع والفرق على الظاهر أن الحمام بني للإحراز . (والمحرمية بالمصاهرة كالمحرمية بالرضاع انظر ما معنى هذا الكلام هنا فإن المحرم بالرضاع يقطع كما تقدم . ومن سرق من المسجد متاعاً وربه عنده قطع ولو سرق ضيف ممن أضافه أو سرق فكان حرزاً فلا . يعتبر الحافظ كالبيت بخلاف المسجد لأنه ما بني لإحراز الأموال فلم يكن محرزاً بالمكان فيعتبر الحافظ كالطريق والصحراء. وشمل ما إذا سرق من الحمام في وقت لم يؤذن للناس في الدخول فيها كالليل، والمنقول في التبيين أنه يقطع بخلاف المسجد لا يقطع مطلقاً. وأطلق في المأذون للناس في دخوله فشمل حوانيت التجار والخانات إلا إذا سرق منه منه ليلاً لأنها بنيت لإحراز الأموال، وإنما الإذن يختص بالنهار ؛ كذا في الهداية. وفي قوله «للناس» إشارة إلى أنه لو أذن لجماعة مخصوصين بالدخول فدخل واحد غيرهم وسرق فإنه يقطع ولم أره صريحاً، وقد قدم المصنف أنه لا بد من الإحراز بمكان أو حافظ قال الطحاوي في كتابه : حرز كل شيء معتبر بحرز مثله حتى إنه إذا سرق دابة من إصطبل يقطع، ولو سرق لؤلؤة من إصطبل لا يقطع . وذكر الكرخي في كتابه أن ما كان حرز النوع فهو حرز للأنواع كلها. قال شمس الأئمة السرخسي : وهذا هو المذهب عندنا والقفاف لا يقطع وهو الذي يعطي الدراهم لينظر إليها فيأخذ منها وصاحبها لا يعلم والفشاش وهو الذي يهيء لغلق البيت ما يفتحه به إذا فش نهاراً وليس في البيت ولا في الدار أحد وأخذ المتاع لا يقطع ، وإن كان فيها أحد من أهلها فأخذ المتاع وهو لا يعلم قطع . وفي الحاوي : إذا كان باب الدار مردوداً غير مغلق فدخلها السارق خفية وأخذ المتاع قطع، ولو كان باب الدار مفتوحاً فدخل نهاراً وسرق لا يقطع، ولو سرق من السطح ثياباً تساوي نصاباً يقطع لأنه حرز ، وإذا سرق ثوباً بسط على حائط في السكة لا يقطع، وكذلك لو سرق ثوباً بسط على خص إلى السكة وإن بسط على الحائط إلى الدار أو على الخص إلى السطح قطع ؛ كذا في الظهيرية اهـ .
As for if he steals from a bathhouse or a house where people are permitted to enter, then due to the disruption of the hirz by permission to enter. He made it absolute, so it includes if he steals from a bathhouse while its owner is with him or the stolen item is under him, unlike if he steals from a mosque while its owner is with him, then he is amputated. The apparent difference is that the bathhouse is built for providing service, not for securing goods. (And the mahramiyya by marriage is like the mahramiyya by suckling: see what the meaning of this statement is here, for the mahram by suckling is amputated, as has preceded. And whoever steals from a mosque goods with their owner present, he is amputated. Even if a guest steals from his host or steals, then it is a hirz, so no. The guardian (hafidh) is considered like a house, unlike the mosque because it was not built for securing goods, so it is not a hirz by location, so the guardian is considered, like a road or open land. And it includes if he steals from a bathhouse at a time when people are not permitted to enter, like at night; the transmitted view in al-Tabyin is that he is amputated, unlike the mosque where he is absolutely not amputated. And he made it absolute regarding what is permitted for people to enter, so it includes shops of merchants and caravanserais, except if he steals from it at night, because they are built for securing goods, and permission is only for daytime; as in al-Hidayah. And in his statement 'for people' is an indication that if he permitted a specific group to enter, and one other entered and stole, then he is amputated—I have not seen it explicitly. And the author has already stated that secure enclosure is by place or guardian. Al-Tahawi said in his book: The hirz of each thing is considered by its like, so if he steals a riding beast from a stable, he is amputated; if he steals a pearl from a stable, he is not amputated. And al-Karkhi mentioned in his book that whatever is the hirz of a type is the hirz for all types. Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi said: This is the madhhab with us. The qaffaf is not amputated—he is the one who gives dirhams to look at and then takes some while the owner does not know. And the fashshash—he is the one who prepares for closing the house what he opens it with, if he opens it during the day while there is no one in the house or courtyard, and he takes goods, he is not amputated. If there is any of the household in it and he takes goods while not knowing, he is amputated. In al-Hawi: If the door of a house is closed but not locked, and a thief enters secretly and takes goods, he is amputated. If the door is open and he enters during the day and steals, he is not amputated. If he steals clothes from a roof worth a nisab, he is amputated because it is a hirz. If he steals a cloth spread on a wall in a lane, he is not amputated. Likewise if he steals a cloth spread on a mat towards the lane; if it is spread on a wall towards the house or on a mat towards the roof, he is amputated; as in al-Zahiriyya. End.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a compilation of rulings on theft from places where public access is granted, focusing on the concept of hirz (secure enclosure). The author explains that a bathhouse (hammam) is not a hirz because it is built for service, not for securing goods; therefore, even if the owner is present, there is no amputation unless a specific guardian (hafidh) is watching. In contrast, a mosque is also not a hirz by itself, but if the owner is present with his goods, that presence acts as a guardian, and amputation applies. However, if the thief steals from a bathhouse at night when public access is not allowed, the bathhouse becomes a hirz and amputation applies. Similarly, shops and caravanserais are hirz by day (when they are open for business) but not necessarily at night. The passage then cites various authorities on the specifics of hirz: al-Tahawi states that the hirz must be appropriate for the item (a stable is hirz for an animal but not for a pearl); al-Karkhi and al-Sarakhsi adopt a general rule that the hirz of a type suffices for all items of that type. The text also discusses specialized thieves: al-qaffaf (one who distracts the owner with money) and al-fashshash (one who picks locks) — such thieves may not be amputated if they steal without breaking into a secure enclosure. The ruling also differentiates between a closed door (even if unlocked) and an open door: entering secretly through a closed door is considered a breach of hirz, but entering through an open door during the day is not. This lesson helps students understand the relativity of hirz based on custom, time, and type of property.

Key Terms
حرز (hirz): A secure place or guardian that protects property from theft; the condition for applying the hadd of theft.
حافظ (hafidh): A guardian who is present and can protect the property; his presence makes even a non-hirz location count as secure.
قفاف (qaffaf): A thief who distracts the owner by giving him coins to examine and then takes some without the owner's knowledge.
فشاش (fashshash): A thief who picks or opens a lock (or door mechanism) to enter and steal, especially when no one is home.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is theft from a bathhouse (hammam) subject to the hadd of amputation?
A) Always, because the bathhouse is a building.
B) Only if the owner is present and watching the item.
C) If it occurs at night when the public is not permitted to enter.
D) Never, because the bathhouse is not a place for securing goods.

Key Takeaway
The hadd of theft depends on the existence of a hirz; public accessibility does not automatically negate hirz; time, guardian, and the nature of the property all matter.

Theft from Mosque and the Role of the Guardian
Ruling on theft from mosque with owner present, with proof and intra-Hanafi expansion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on theft from a non-hirz place when the owner is present and explain why the owner's presence suffices as guardianship, using the hadith of Safwan.

Core Idea
Owner presence, even asleep, makes a non-hirz place effectively guarded—theft from him warrants amputation.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies a key Hudud ruling: theft from a mosque does not automatically trigger amputation, because the mosque is not a hirz. However, if the owner is present with his property, his presence substitutes for the lack of physical security. This ruling is grounded in the Prophetic action regarding Safwan's cloak. We will examine the proof, the conditions, and an intra-Hanafi difference on how close the property must be to the owner.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
State the specific scenario: theft of goods from a mosque (or any non-hirz place) when the owner is present. The ruling: the thief is amputated (hadd).
The mosque is not a hirz by itself, so the ruling depends on the owner's presence.
2
Locate the Dalil
Find the proof: the hadith of Safwan ibn Umayyah. The Prophet ﷺ amputated a thief who stole a cloak from under Safwan's head while he slept in the mosque.
The hadith is ahad, but its meaning is clear (qati' al-dalala) for this ruling.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
The Prophet's action demonstrates that the sleeping owner's presence makes the place effectively guarded. The custom ('urf) considers a sleeping person a guardian of what is near him.
This is why the hadd applies even though the mosque is not a physical hirz.
4
Note the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Some mashayikh restrict 'with him' to items under the body (under head/side). Al-Sarakhsi and al-Mujtaba expand it to any item near the sleeping person that is customarily guarded (including in front). The mufta bihi is the absolute view.
The difference hinges on whether the custom of guarding extends to items in front.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ومن سرق من المسجد متاعاً وربه عنده قطع لأنه عليه السلام قطع سارق رداء صفوان من تحت رأسه وهو نائم في المسجد وأراد بالمسجد كل موضع لم يكن حرزاً فدخل الطريق والصحراء. وأطلق في ربه» فشمل النائم واليقظان وهو الصحيح، وأراد من كونه عنده أن يكون بحيث يراه كما في المجتبى. وأطلق في كونه عنده فشمل ما إذا كان تحت رأسه أو تحت جنبه أو بين يديه حالة النوم وهو قول بعض المشايخ وإليه مال الإمام السرخسي. وفي الأصل ما يدل على خلافه فإنه قال : المسافر ينزل في الصحراء فيجمع متاعه ويبيت عليه فسرق رجل منه شيئاً قطع، فإن بعض المشايخ فهم منه أنه إذا كان موضوعاً بين يديه لا يقطع ؛ كذا في الظهيرية وصحح في المجتبى ما اختاره السرخسي من الإطلاق لأنه يعد النائم حافظاً له عادة وعلى هذا لا يضمن المودع والمستعير بمثله لأنه ليس بتضييع بخلاف ما اختاره في الفتاوى .اهـ. وأشار المصنف، إلى أنه لو سرق الغنم أو البقر أو الفرس من المرعى
His statement: (And whoever steals from the mosque goods with their owner present, he is amputated, because he (peace be upon him) amputated the thief of the cloak of Safwan from under his head while he was sleeping in the mosque. And by 'mosque' he meant every place that is not a hirz, so it includes road and open land. And he made it absolute regarding 'his owner' so it includes the sleeping and the awake, and this is the correct view. And he meant by 'with him' that he should be in a position to see it, as in al-Mujtaba. And he made it absolute regarding 'with him' so it includes if it is under his head, or under his side, or in front of him while sleeping—this is the statement of some mashayikh, and Imam al-Sarakhsi inclined towards it. And in al-Asl there is evidence to the contrary, for he said: A traveler camps in the desert and gathers his belongings and sleeps on them, then a man steals something from him, he is amputated. Some mashayikh understood from this that if it is placed in front of him, he is not amputated; as in al-Zahiriyya. And al-Mujtaba confirmed what al-Sarakhsi chose of the absolute, because a sleeping person is customarily considered a guardian. Upon this, the depositary and borrower are not liable for similar, because it is not negligence, unlike what was chosen in al-Fatawa. End. And the author indicated that if he steals sheep, cows, or horses from pasture...
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn regarding theft from a mosque. The commentator explains that the mosque itself is not a hirz (secure enclosure) because it is not built for safeguarding goods. However, if the owner is present with his property, that presence serves as a guardian (hafidh), and the thief is amputated. The proof is the hadith of Safwan: the Prophet ﷺ ordered the amputation of a thief who stole Safwan's cloak while Safwan was sleeping in the mosque with the cloak under his head. The term 'mosque' in the matn is interpreted broadly to include any place that is not a hirz, such as a road or open land. The commentator then discusses the meaning of 'with him' (عنده): it includes both the awake and the sleeping owner, as long as the owner is in a position to see or is customarily considered a guardian. The sleeping person is considered a guardian because it is customary for a person to guard what is under his head or near him. There is a difference of opinion: some scholars argue that if the item is merely placed in front (rather than under the body), it may not be considered guarded. Al-Sarakhsi and al-Mujtaba adopt the absolute view that any placement close to the sleeping person suffices. The implication is that a depositary or borrower is not liable for loss under similar circumstances because it is not considered negligence. The passage ends with an incomplete reference to theft from pasture, indicating that such theft may not warrant amputation because the animals are not in a hirz.

Key Terms
حافظ (hafidh): A person who guards property; in this context, the owner himself, even if asleep, is considered a guardian because it is customary that a person guards what is near him.
تحت رأسه (tahta ra'sihi): Under his head; a specific position indicating close physical proximity and customary guarding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did the Prophet amputate the thief of Safwan's cloak even though Safwan was sleeping in the mosque?
A) Because the mosque is a public place.
B) Because Safwan's presence made it effectively guarded.
C) Because the cloak was under Safwan's head.
D) Because the thief confessed.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school relies on the owner's presence (even asleep) as effective guardianship, thus theft from a non-hirz place with the owner present warrants amputation.

Theft from a Mosque: The Guardian Condition
Ruling on theft from mosque with condition of guardian.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for the cutting penalty in theft from a mosque and explain why the mosque is not considered a ḥirz.

Core Idea
A mosque is not a protected place; the thief’s hand is cut only if the owner is present as guardian.

In this passage, we learn a key principle: the mosque is not built for storing wealth, so it is not a ḥirz. Therefore, the cutting penalty for theft from a mosque depends on whether the owner was guarding the item. This lesson unpacks the ruling and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Theft from a mosque: the hand of the thief is cut only if the owner is with the item. The mosque itself does not confer protection.
2
Define ḥirz
A ḥirz is a place intended for safekeeping wealth, such as a house, shop, or stable. The mosque is not built for this purpose.
3
Apply the rule to the mosque
Because the mosque is not a ḥirz by location, the thief is cut only if the owner acts as a guardian (ḥāfiẓ) by being present.
4
Contrast with other places
Shops and caravanserais are built for storing wealth, so they are ḥirz. Permission to enter during the day does not negate that.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب السرقة
ومن سرق من المسجد متاعاً وربه عنده قطع ولو سرق ضيف ممن أضافه أو سرق فكان حرزاً فلا . يعتبر الحافظ كالبيت بخلاف المسجد لأنه ما بني لإحراز الأموال فلم يكن محرزاً بالمكان فيعتبر الحافظ كالطريق والصحراء. وشمل ما إذا سرق من الحمام في وقت لم يؤذن للناس في الدخول فيها كالليل، والمنقول في التبيين أنه يقطع بخلاف المسجد لا يقطع مطلقاً. وأطلق في المأذون للناس في دخوله فشمل حوانيت التجار والخانات إلا إذا سرق منه منه ليلاً لأنها بنيت لإحراز الأموال، وإنما الإذن يختص بالنهار ؛ كذا في الهداية. وفي قوله «للناس» إشارة إلى أنه لو أذن لجماعة مخصوصين بالدخول فدخل واحد غيرهم وسرق فإنه يقطع ولم أره صريحاً، وقد قدم المصنف أنه لا بد من الإحراز بمكان أو حافظ قال الطحاوي في كتابه : حرز كل شيء معتبر بحرز مثله حتى إنه إذا سرق دابة من إصطبل يقطع، ولو سرق لؤلؤة من إصطبل لا يقطع . وذكر الكرخي في كتابه أن ما كان حرز النوع فهو حرز للأنواع كلها. قال شمس الأئمة السرخسي : وهذا هو المذهب عندنا والقفاف لا يقطع وهو الذي يعطي الدراهم لينظر إليها فيأخذ منها وصاحبها لا يعلم والفشاش وهو الذي يهيء لغلق البيت ما يفتحه به إذا فش نهاراً وليس في البيت ولا في الدار أحد وأخذ المتاع لا يقطع ، وإن كان فيها أحد من أهلها فأخذ المتاع وهو لا يعلم قطع . وفي الحاوي : إذا كان باب الدار مردوداً غير مغلق فدخلها السارق خفية وأخذ المتاع قطع، ولو كان باب الدار مفتوحاً فدخل نهاراً وسرق لا يقطع، ولو سرق من السطح ثياباً تساوي نصاباً يقطع لأنه حرز ، وإذا سرق ثوباً بسط على حائط في السكة لا يقطع، وكذلك لو سرق ثوباً بسط على خص إلى السكة وإن بسط على الحائط إلى الدار أو على الخص إلى السطح قطع ؛ كذا في الظهيرية اهـ .
Book of Theft. Whoever steals from a mosque an item while its owner is with him, his hand is cut. And if a guest steals from the one who hosted him, or if he stole while it was a protected place, then no consideration is given to the guardian as in a house, unlike the mosque because it was not built for the safeguarding of wealth, so it is not a protected place by location, so the guardian is considered like the road and desert. It includes if he steals from the bathhouse at a time when people are not permitted to enter, like the night; the transmitted view in al-Tabyīn is that his hand is cut, unlike the mosque where it is not cut at all. It is applied generally to permission for people to enter, so it includes shops of merchants and caravanserais, except if he steals from it at night because they are built for safeguarding wealth, and permission is specific to daytime; thus in al-Hidāyah. In His saying 'for people' is an indication that if he permitted a specific group to enter and someone else entered and stole, then his hand is cut, but I have not seen it explicitly. The author has stated that safeguarding must be by place or guardian. Al-Ṭaḥāwī said in his book: The protected place of everything is considered by its like, so that if he steals a horse from a stable his hand is cut, but if he steals a pearl from a stable his hand is not cut. Al-Karkhī mentioned in his book that what is a protected place for the type is a protected place for all types. Shams al-A’immah al-Sarakhsī said: This is the madhhab with us. And al-qafqāf (one who takes dirhams to look at them and then takes them) is not cut, and al-fashshāsh (one who prepares something to open the lock and picks it during the day while no one is in the house or the building and takes the goods) his hand is not cut; but if there is someone from the residents inside and he takes the goods without them knowing, his hand is cut. In al-Ḥāwī: If the door of the house is closed but not locked, and the thief enters secretly and takes the goods, his hand is cut; if the door was open and he entered during the day and stole, his hand is not cut. If he steals clothes from the roof that are worth a nisāb, his hand is cut because it is a protected place. If he steals a cloth spread on a wall in the alley, his hand is not cut; similarly if he steals a cloth spread on a reed fence towards the alley; but if it is spread on the wall towards the house or on the reed fence towards the roof, his hand is cut; thus in al-Ẓahīriyyah end.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqā’iq. It states the general rule for theft from a mosque: the thief’s hand is cut only if the owner is present with the item (i.e., guarding it). The mosque itself is not considered a ḥirz (protected place) because it is not built for storing wealth; rather, it is like a road or desert. Therefore, the thief is cut only because the owner acts as a guardian (ḥāfiẓ). The same applies to a bathhouse when people are not permitted to enter, like at night. Shops and caravanserais are ḥirz because they are built for storing wealth, but permission to enter is only during the day; theft at night from them incurs cutting. The passage also discusses the concept of ḥirz by type: according to al-Ṭaḥāwī, each type of item has its own ḥirz (e.g., a horse is usually in a stable, a pearl is not), so theft of a horse from a stable cuts, but a pearl from a stable does not. Al-Karkhī holds that if the place is a ḥirz for one type, it is a ḥirz for all types, but al-Sarakhsī affirms the latter is the madhhab. The passage then gives specific cases: the qafqāf (a trickster who takes dirhams to look at) and the fashshāsh (a lock picker who acts during the day when no one is home) are not cut. However, if the lock picker enters while someone is home and takes the item, he is cut. For a house that is closed but not locked, secret entry and theft causes cutting. If the door is open and entry is during the day, no cut. The roof is a ḥirz, so theft of clothes from the roof cuts. A cloth spread on a wall or fence facing the street is not protected, but if it faces the house or the roof, it is protected.

Key Terms
حرز (ḥirz): A secure place or container where property is habitually kept and safeguarded, such as a house, shop, or stable.
سرقة (sariqah): Taking the property of another secretly from its ḥirz, with the intention of ownership, when the value reaches the nisāb.
قطع (qaṭ‘): Amputation of the right hand as the ḥadd punishment for theft that meets all conditions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What condition makes theft from a mosque subject to the cutting penalty?
A) The mosque is a place of worship
B) The thief enters at night
C) The owner is present with the item
D) The mosque is locked

Key Takeaway
The cutting penalty for theft from a mosque requires the owner's presence because the mosque itself is not a ḥirz.

Hirz by Type and Specific Cases of Theft
Intra-Hanafi dispute on ḥirz by type and application to specific theft scenarios.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi concept of ḥirz to determine whether the cutting penalty applies in various scenarios, including disputes on ḥirz by type.

Core Idea
A place that is a ḥirz for one type of item is a ḥirz for all types per the preferred Hanafi view.

The second part of the passage addresses a key dispute: does the ḥirz of a type extend to all items? Al-Ṭaḥāwī says no, al-Karkhī says yes, and al-Sarakhsī affirms the latter as the madhhab. The passage then illustrates this principle with several specific cases involving thieves like the qafqāf and fashshāsh, and scenarios of doors, roofs, and walls. This lesson unpacks these applications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute on ḥirz by type
Al-Ṭaḥāwī: each type has its own ḥirz (e.g., horse in stable, pearl not). Al-Karkhī: if a place is a ḥirz for one type, it is for all. Al-Sarakhsī declares the latter the madhhab.
This affects whether stealing a pearl from a stable incurs cutting.
2
Analyze the qafqāf and fashshāsh cases
The qafqāf (takes dirhams to examine) and fashshāsh (lock picker when no one home) are not cut because the item is not taken from a proper ḥirz or the taking is not secret enough.
But if someone is home, the fashshāsh is cut.
3
Apply the rule to door and roof scenarios
Closed door (not locked) but secret entry: cutting applies. Open door daytime entry: no cutting. Roof is a ḥirz: cutting applies. Cloth on wall/fence: depends on orientation (towards house = ḥirz; towards street = no).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب السرقة
ومن سرق من المسجد متاعاً وربه عنده قطع ولو سرق ضيف ممن أضافه أو سرق فكان حرزاً فلا . يعتبر الحافظ كالبيت بخلاف المسجد لأنه ما بني لإحراز الأموال فلم يكن محرزاً بالمكان فيعتبر الحافظ كالطريق والصحراء. وشمل ما إذا سرق من الحمام في وقت لم يؤذن للناس في الدخول فيها كالليل، والمنقول في التبيين أنه يقطع بخلاف المسجد لا يقطع مطلقاً. وأطلق في المأذون للناس في دخوله فشمل حوانيت التجار والخانات إلا إذا سرق منه منه ليلاً لأنها بنيت لإحراز الأموال، وإنما الإذن يختص بالنهار ؛ كذا في الهداية. وفي قوله «للناس» إشارة إلى أنه لو أذن لجماعة مخصوصين بالدخول فدخل واحد غيرهم وسرق فإنه يقطع ولم أره صريحاً، وقد قدم المصنف أنه لا بد من الإحراز بمكان أو حافظ قال الطحاوي في كتابه : حرز كل شيء معتبر بحرز مثله حتى إنه إذا سرق دابة من إصطبل يقطع، ولو سرق لؤلؤة من إصطبل لا يقطع . وذكر الكرخي في كتابه أن ما كان حرز النوع فهو حرز للأنواع كلها. قال شمس الأئمة السرخسي : وهذا هو المذهب عندنا والقفاف لا يقطع وهو الذي يعطي الدراهم لينظر إليها فيأخذ منها وصاحبها لا يعلم والفشاش وهو الذي يهيء لغلق البيت ما يفتحه به إذا فش نهاراً وليس في البيت ولا في الدار أحد وأخذ المتاع لا يقطع ، وإن كان فيها أحد من أهلها فأخذ المتاع وهو لا يعلم قطع . وفي الحاوي : إذا كان باب الدار مردوداً غير مغلق فدخلها السارق خفية وأخذ المتاع قطع، ولو كان باب الدار مفتوحاً فدخل نهاراً وسرق لا يقطع، ولو سرق من السطح ثياباً تساوي نصاباً يقطع لأنه حرز ، وإذا سرق ثوباً بسط على حائط في السكة لا يقطع، وكذلك لو سرق ثوباً بسط على خص إلى السكة وإن بسط على الحائط إلى الدار أو على الخص إلى السطح قطع ؛ كذا في الظهيرية اهـ .
Book of Theft. Whoever steals from a mosque an item while its owner is with him, his hand is cut. And if a guest steals from the one who hosted him, or if he stole while it was a protected place, then no consideration is given to the guardian as in a house, unlike the mosque because it was not built for the safeguarding of wealth, so it is not a protected place by location, so the guardian is considered like the road and desert. It includes if he steals from the bathhouse at a time when people are not permitted to enter, like the night; the transmitted view in al-Tabyīn is that his hand is cut, unlike the mosque where it is not cut at all. It is applied generally to permission for people to enter, so it includes shops of merchants and caravanserais, except if he steals from it at night because they are built for safeguarding wealth, and permission is specific to daytime; thus in al-Hidāyah. In His saying 'for people' is an indication that if he permitted a specific group to enter and someone else entered and stole, then his hand is cut, but I have not seen it explicitly. The author has stated that safeguarding must be by place or guardian. Al-Ṭaḥāwī said in his book: The protected place of everything is considered by its like, so that if he steals a horse from a stable his hand is cut, but if he steals a pearl from a stable his hand is not cut. Al-Karkhī mentioned in his book that what is a protected place for the type is a protected place for all types. Shams al-A’immah al-Sarakhsī said: This is the madhhab with us. And al-qafqāf (one who takes dirhams to look at them and then takes them) is not cut, and al-fashshāsh (one who prepares something to open the lock and picks it during the day while no one is in the house or the building and takes the goods) his hand is not cut; but if there is someone from the residents inside and he takes the goods without them knowing, his hand is cut. In al-Ḥāwī: If the door of the house is closed but not locked, and the thief enters secretly and takes the goods, his hand is cut; if the door was open and he entered during the day and stole, his hand is not cut. If he steals clothes from the roof that are worth a nisāb, his hand is cut because it is a protected place. If he steals a cloth spread on a wall in the alley, his hand is not cut; similarly if he steals a cloth spread on a reed fence towards the alley; but if it is spread on the wall towards the house or on the reed fence towards the roof, his hand is cut; thus in al-Ẓahīriyyah end.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqā’iq. It states the general rule for theft from a mosque: the thief’s hand is cut only if the owner is present with the item (i.e., guarding it). The mosque itself is not considered a ḥirz (protected place) because it is not built for storing wealth; rather, it is like a road or desert. Therefore, the thief is cut only because the owner acts as a guardian (ḥāfiẓ). The same applies to a bathhouse when people are not permitted to enter, like at night. Shops and caravanserais are ḥirz because they are built for storing wealth, but permission to enter is only during the day; theft at night from them incurs cutting. The passage also discusses the concept of ḥirz by type: according to al-Ṭaḥāwī, each type of item has its own ḥirz (e.g., a horse is usually in a stable, a pearl is not), so theft of a horse from a stable cuts, but a pearl from a stable does not. Al-Karkhī holds that if the place is a ḥirz for one type, it is a ḥirz for all types, but al-Sarakhsī affirms the latter is the madhhab. The passage then gives specific cases: the qafqāf (a trickster who takes dirhams to look at) and the fashshāsh (a lock picker who acts during the day when no one is home) are not cut. However, if the lock picker enters while someone is home and takes the item, he is cut. For a house that is closed but not locked, secret entry and theft causes cutting. If the door is open and entry is during the day, no cut. The roof is a ḥirz, so theft of clothes from the roof cuts. A cloth spread on a wall or fence facing the street is not protected, but if it faces the house or the roof, it is protected.

Key Terms
حرز (ḥirz): A secure place or container where property is habitually kept and safeguarded, such as a house, shop, or stable.
قفاف (qafqāf): A person who takes dirhams to look at them and then steals them without the owner's knowledge.
فشاش (fashshāsh): A person who picks a lock or prepares a tool to open a door, enters during the day when no one is home, and steals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling for the fashshāsh when no one is home?
A) His hand is cut because he broke in.
B) His hand is not cut.
C) His hand is cut only if the stolen item exceeds the nisāb.
D) He is not punished but must return the item.

Key Takeaway
The application of ḥirz depends on the specific circumstances, including the type of place, time of day, and presence of people, with the preferred view that ḥirz for one type is ḥirz for all.

Theft from a Mosque and Related Scenarios: The Hadith of Safwan
Prophetic proof and expansion on theft scenarios

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on theft from a mosque, from a sleeping person, by a guest, and within a house, and identify the conditions that trigger the hadd of cutting.

Core Idea
The hadd of cutting applies only when the item is taken from a valid ḥirz; a guest's permission nullifies the ḥirz.

This passage presents the prophetic proof for the ruling on theft from a mosque and expands to various scenarios: theft from a sleeping person, theft by a guest, and theft within different rooms. Understanding the concept of ḥirz (secure place) is key. By studying the hadith of Ṣafwān and the subsequent analysis, you will learn how Hanafi scholars determine the application of the ḥadd punishment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core proof and its application
The hadith of Ṣafwān proves that theft from a mosque with the owner present incurs the cut. Note the conditions: owner present, item under his head while sleeping.
The mosque itself is not a ḥirz; the owner's presence creates the ḥirz.
2
Analyze the scope of 'with him'
The text discusses whether items under the side or between the hands of a sleeping person are included. Al-Sarakhsī says yes; some earlier texts say no. The muftā bihī is the general view.
This intra-Hanafī ikhtilāf is resolved by considering customary guardianship.
3
Distinguish between theft from a sleeping person's body and from his vicinity
Garments worn or on the body are not considered in a ḥirz (so no cut). Items placed nearby (under head, side, or between hands) are considered guarded if the sleeper is customarily a guardian.
The distinction between 'on the body' and 'near the body' is critical.
4
Examine the guest scenario
The guest's permission to enter vitiates the ḥirz; his act is betrayal, not theft. No cut.
The guest is likened to a family member for the purpose of ḥirz.
5
Apply the rule of separate ḥirz per room
If an item is moved from one room to another within the same house, the hand is cut because each room is a separate ḥirz for its occupant.
This applies to houses with multiple compartments (maqāṣīr).

قوله: (ومن سرق من المسجد متاعاً وربه عنده قطع لأنه عليه السلام قطع سارق رداء صفوان من تحت رأسه وهو نائم في المسجد وأراد بالمسجد كل موضع لم يكن حرزاً فدخل الطريق والصحراء. وأطلق في ربه» فشمل النائم واليقظان وهو الصحيح، وأراد من كونه عنده أن يكون بحيث يراه كما في المجتبى. وأطلق في كونه عنده فشمل ما إذا كان تحت رأسه أو تحت جنبه أو بين يديه حالة النوم وهو قول بعض المشايخ وإليه مال الإمام السرخسي. وفي الأصل ما يدل على خلافه فإنه قال : المسافر ينزل في الصحراء فيجمع متاعه ويبيت عليه فسرق رجل منه شيئاً قطع، فإن بعض المشايخ فهم منه أنه إذا كان موضوعاً بين يديه لا يقطع ؛ كذا في الظهيرية وصحح في المجتبى ما اختاره السرخسي من الإطلاق لأنه يعد النائم حافظاً له عادة وعلى هذا لا يضمن المودع والمستعير بمثله لأنه ليس بتضييع بخلاف ما اختاره في الفتاوى .اهـ. وأشار المصنف، إلى أنه لو سرق الغنم أو البقر أو الفرس من المرعى
قوله: (فلو سرق من رجل ثوباً عليه إلى قوله لم يقطع) أي لأنه اختلاس كما في الزيلعي،
سارق شيئاً ولم يخرجه من الدار لا وإن أخرجه من حجرة إلى الدار أو أغار من أهل الحجرة على حجرة أخرى أو نقب فدخل وألقى شيئاً في الطريق ثم أخذه أو حمله على
ومعها حافظ فإنه يقطع، وإطلاق محمد عدم القطع محمول على ما إذا لم يكن معها حافظ لكن إن كان الحافظ الراعي ففيه اختلاف ففي البقالي لا يقطع وهكذا في المنتقى عن أبي حنيفة . وأطلق خواهر زاده ثبوت القطع إذا كان معها حافظ، ويمكن التوفيق بأن الراعي لم يقصد لحفظها من السراق بخلاف غيره ؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وفي المجتبى : لا قطع في المواشي في المرعى وإن كان معها الراعي وإن كان معها سوى الراعي من يحفظها يجب القطع، وكثير من مشايخنا أفتوا بهذا وإن كانت الغنم تأوي إلى بيت في الليل بني لها عليه باب مغلق فكسره وسرق منها شاة قطع لا يعتبر الغلق إذا كان الباب مردوداً إلا أن يكون بيتاً منفرداً في الصحراء أو المراح. وفي الحاوي: اتخذ من الحجر أو الشوك حظيرة وجمع هذه الأغنام وهو نائم عندها قطع وعن محمد يقطع سواء كان معها حافظ أو لا وعليه عامة المشايخ اهـ. وأشار المصنف بالحضرة إلى أن الثياب ليست عليه فلو سرق من رجل ثوباً عليه أو رداء أو قلنسوة أو منطقة أو سرق من امرأة نائمة حلياً عليها لم يقطع وكذا إذا سرق من رجل نائم عليه ملاءة وهو لابسها لم يقطع وقيل يقطع كالموضوع عنده ؛ كذا في المجتبى. وقيد بما ليس بحرز لما في الخلاصة جماعة نزلوا بيتاً أو خاناً فسرق بعضهم من بعض متاعاً وصاحب المتاع يحفظه أو تحت رأسه لم يقطع ولو كان في مسجد جماعة قطع قوله: (ولو سرق ضيف ممن أضافه أو سرق شيئاً ولم يخرج من الدار (لا أي لا يقطع ; أما الأول فلأن البيت لم يبق حرزاً في حقه لكونه مأذوناً في دخوله ولأنه بمنزلة أهل الدار فيكون فعله خيانة لا سرقة . أطلقه فشمل ما إذا سرق من البيت الذي أضافه فيه أو من بعض بيوت الدار، سوء كان مقفلاً أو صندوق مقفل - ذكره القدوري في شرحه - لأن الدار مع جميع بيوتها حرز واحد فبالإذن في الدار اختل الحرز في جميع بيوتها . وأما الثاني فلأن الدار كلها حرز واحد فلا بد من الإخراج منها وما فيها في يد صاحبها معنى فتتمكن شبهة عدم الأخذ قيد بالسرقة لأنه يجب الضمان على الغاصب بمجرد الأخذ وإن لم يخرجه من الدار هو الصحيح لأنه يجب مع الشبهة .
من
قوله : (وإن أخرجه من حجرة إلى الدار وأغار من أهل الحجرة على حجرة أخرى أو نقب فدخل وألقى شيئاً في الطريق ثم أخذه أو حمله على حمار فساقه وأخرجه قطع) بيان لأربع مسائل : الأولى لو كانت الدار فيها مقاصير فأخرجها من مقصورة إلى صحن الدار فإنه يقطع لأن كل مقصورة باعتبار ساكنها حرز على حدة، فالمراد بالدار الكبيرة التي فيها منازل وفي
وجزم بأنه لو سرق من رجل قلادة عليه وهو لابسها أو ملاءة له وهو لابسها أو واضعها قريباً منه يقطع فتأمل .
قوله : (فيها مقاصير) قال في معراج الدراية : المقصورة الحجرة بلسان أهل الكوفة. قوله : (ثم
His statement: (Whoever steals from a mosque an item while its owner is with him, his hand is cut) because the Prophet (peace be upon him) cut the hand of the thief of Ṣafwān's cloak from under his head while he was sleeping in the mosque. By 'mosque' he meant every place that is not a ḥirz, so the road and desert are included. He spoke generally regarding 'its owner' to include the sleeping and the awake—this is the correct view. He meant by 'being with him' that he sees it as in al-Mujtabā. He spoke generally regarding 'being with him' to include when it is under his head, under his side, or between his hands while sleeping—this is the view of some mashāyikh, and Imām al-Sarakhsī inclined towards it. In al-Aṣl there is evidence to the contrary: he said, 'A traveller descends in the desert, gathers his belongings and sleeps on them, and a man steals something from him; his hand is cut.' Some mashāyikh understood from this that if it is placed between his hands, his hand is not cut—thus in al-Ẓahīriyyah. In al-Mujtabā, he corrected what al-Sarakhsī chose of generality because the sleeper is considered a guardian over it customarily. Accordingly, the depositary and borrower are not liable for its like because it is not negligence—contrary to what he chose in al-Fatāwā, end. And the author indicated that if he steals sheep, cattle, or a horse from the pasture... His statement: (If he steals from a man a garment that is upon him until his statement: his hand is not cut) i.e., because it is snatching (ikhtilās) as in al-Zayla‘ī. ... a thief steals something and does not take it out of the house, no cut. If he takes it from a room to the house, or if the inhabitants of one room attack another room, or he breaks in and throws something into the street then takes it, or loads it on a donkey and drives it out, his hand is cut. ... and if there is a guardian with it, his hand is cut. Muhammad's mutlaq statement of no cut is interpreted as when there is no guardian. But if the guardian is a shepherd, there is disagreement: in al-Baqqālī, no cut, and likewise in al-Muntaqā from Abū Ḥanīfah. Khwāharzādah affirmed the establishment of the cut if there is a guardian with it. A reconciliation can be made: the shepherd does not intend to guard them from thieves, unlike others—thus in Fatḥ al-Qadīr. In al-Mujtabā: no cut for livestock in the pasture even if there is a shepherd, and if there is someone other than the shepherd who guards them, the cut is obligatory. Many of our mashāyikh gave fatwa on this. If the sheep return to a house at night built for them with a closed door, and he breaks it and steals a sheep, his hand is cut; the lock is not considered if the door is merely closed, unless it is an isolated house in the desert or the fold. In al-Ḥāwī: He made an enclosure of stones or thorns and gathered these sheep, and he is sleeping near them, his hand is cut. And from Muhammad: he is cut whether or not there is a guardian, and the majority of mashāyikh agree. The author indicated by 'al-ḥaḍrah' that the clothes are not upon him. So if he steals from a man a garment that is on him, or a cloak, or a cap, or a belt, or steals jewelry from a sleeping woman, his hand is not cut. Similarly, if he steals from a sleeping man a covering that he is wearing, his hand is not cut. It is said his hand is cut like that which is placed with him—thus in al-Mujtabā. And it is restricted to what is not a ḥirz. In al-Khulāṣah: A group descended in a house or a caravanserai, and one of them stole goods from another while the owner was guarding it or it was under his head, his hand is not cut. But if it was in a mosque in a congregation, his hand is cut. His statement: (If a guest steals from his host or steals something and does not take it out of the house, no), i.e., no cut. As for the first, because the house does not remain a ḥirz with respect to him due to his being permitted to enter, and because he is like the residents of the house, so his action is betrayal, not theft. He applied it generally to include if he steals from the house in which he was hosted or from some of the rooms of the house, whether it was locked or a locked chest—mentioned by al-Qudūrī in his commentary—because the house with all its rooms is one ḥirz, and by permission in the house, the ḥirz is vitiated in all its rooms. As for the second, because the entire house is one ḥirz, so extraction from it is necessary, and what is in it is in the hand of its owner in a figurative sense, so the doubt of not taking is established. It is restricted to theft because the guarantor (ḍāmin) is required to guarantee upon mere taking even if he does not take it out of the house—this is the correct view because it is required in the presence of doubt. His statement: (And if he takes it out from a room to the house, or if the inhabitants of one room attack another room, or he breaks in and throws something into the street then takes it, or loads it on a donkey and drives it out, his hand is cut) an explanation of four issues: First, if the house has compartments (maqāṣīr) and he takes it from one compartment to the courtyard of the house, his hand is cut because each compartment is a separate ḥirz with respect to its resident. The intended meaning is the large house that contains dwellings. ... And he affirmed that if he steals from a man a necklace that he is wearing, or a covering that he is wearing, or placed near him, his hand is cut—ponder. His statement: (in which there are compartments) said in Mi‘rāj al-Dirāyah: the compartment (al-maqṣūrah) is the room (al-ḥujrah) in the language of the people of Kufa. His statement: (then...).
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe providing the prophetic proof for the ruling on theft from a mosque. The hadith: the Prophet (peace be upon him) cut the hand of the thief who stole Ṣafwān's cloak while Ṣafwān was sleeping in the mosque with the cloak under his head. This proves that the mosque is not a ḥirz by itself; rather, the owner’s possession (being with the item) makes it a ḥirz. The text then discusses whether ‘with him’ includes items under his head, under his side, or between his hands. Al-Sarakhsī holds that it does, considering the sleeper a guardian customarily. There is a dispute: some earlier texts suggested that if the item is merely between his hands (not under him) it may not be considered his possession. But the preferred view (al-mu‘tamad) is that the sleeper is a guardian for items near him. The text then covers several other scenarios: (1) stealing from a sleeping person’s body—if the garment is worn, it is not considered a ḥirz because the body itself is not a place of storage; thus no cut (mere snatching). (2) Theft by a guest: the guest is permitted to enter, so the house ceases to be a ḥirz for him; his act is betrayal, not theft, so no cut. (3) Theft within the same house: if the thief takes an item from one room to another within the same house, his hand is cut because each room is a separate ḥirz for its occupant. (4) Livestock in the pasture: if there is a guardian, the cut applies even if the guardian is a shepherd, with disagreement among scholars; the preferred view is that it depends on the guardian's intent to protect from thieves.

Key Terms
تحت رأسه (taḥta ra’sih): Under the head: refers to an item placed beneath the head of a sleeping person, considered as being in his possession.
ضيف (ḍayf): A guest who is invited and given permission to enter the host’s home. The guest is like a resident for the purpose of ḥirz.
حجرة (ḥujrah): A room within a house; each room is a separate ḥirz for its occupant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a guest's hand not cut when he steals from the host's home?
A) The guest is forgiven by Islamic law
B) The guest is considered like a family member
C) The house ceases to be a ḥirz for the guest due to permission
D) The minimum nisāb is not met in such cases

Key Takeaway
The application of the ḥadd of cutting depends on whether the stolen item was in a valid ḥirz; exceptions include theft by permission (guest) and theft from a person's body (snatching).

The Essential Condition of Removal from the House for Hadd Amputation
Explains the condition of removal from the house for the hadd of theft.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that the stolen item must be removed from the entire house (dar) for the hadd of amputation to apply, and identify which actions satisfy this condition.

Core Idea
No cut unless the stolen item leaves the entire house — moving within the house is not enough.

The hadd punishment of amputation for theft is not automatic. The Hanafi school specifies that the thief must physically take the item out of the entire house or property. This lesson examines that condition and its application in various scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The passage discusses the act of stealing an item from a house. The ruling is that amputation (qat') applies only when the item is removed from the entire house (dar).
2
Determine the condition for cut
The central condition is 'ikhraj' (removal) from the dar. Moving within the house, such as from one room to another, does not count as removal.
The dar is the entire property, not just a room.
3
Apply to various scenarios
The passage lists cases: breaking in and throwing the item onto the road then retrieving it, or loading it on a donkey and driving it out—these constitute removal. Attacking from one room to another is not removal.
The road is outside the dar, so throwing onto it counts as removal.
4
Note the negative case
If the thief does not take the item out of the house, no cut applies. This reinforces the strict condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سارق شيئاً ولم يخرجه من الدار لا وإن أخرجه من حجرة إلى الدار أو أغار من أهل الحجرة على حجرة أخرى أو نقب فدخل وألقى شيئاً في الطريق ثم أخذه أو حمله على حمار فساقه وأخرجه قطع. ومعها حافظ فإنه يقطع، وإطلاق محمد عدم القطع محمول على ما إذا لم يكن معها حافظ لكن إن كان الحافظ الراعي ففيه اختلاف ففي البقالي لا يقطع وهكذا في المنتقى عن أبي حنيفة . وأطلق خواهر زاده ثبوت القطع إذا كان معها حافظ، ويمكن التوفيق بأن الراعي لم يقصد لحفظها من السراق بخلاف غيره ؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وفي المجتبى : لا قطع في المواشي في المرعى وإن كان معها الراعي وإن كان معها سوى الراعي من يحفظها يجب القطع، وكثير من مشايخنا أفتوا بهذا وإن كانت الغنم تأوي إلى بيت في الليل بني لها عليه باب مغلق فكسره وسرق منها شاة قطع لا يعتبر الغلق إذا كان الباب مردوداً إلا أن يكون بيتاً منفرداً في الصحراء أو المراح. وفي الحاوي: اتخذ من الحجر أو الشوك حظيرة وجمع هذه الأغنام وهو نائم عندها قطع وعن محمد يقطع سواء كان معها حافظ أو لا وعليه عامة المشايخ اهـ.
If someone steals something and does not take it out of the house, no [cut]. And if he takes it out from a room to the house, or attacks from one room to another, or breaks in and throws something on the road then takes it, or loads it on a donkey and drives it out, he is cut. And if there is a guard with it, he is cut. And the statement of Muhammad that there is no cut is interpreted for when there is no guard, but if the guard is a shepherd, there is disagreement. In al-Baqqali, no cut, and similarly in al-Muntaqa from Abu Hanifa. And Kharzada stated the cutting is established if there is a guard. Reconciliation is possible by saying the shepherd does not intend to guard against thieves unlike others; thus in Fath al-Qadir. In al-Mujtaba: no cut for livestock in pasture even with a shepherd, but if there is someone other than a shepherd guarding them, cutting is obligatory, and many of our scholars gave fatwa according to this. And if sheep take shelter at night in a house built with a closed door, and he breaks it and steals a sheep, he is cut. The lock is not considered if the door is closed except if it is a separate house in the desert or enclosure. In al-Hawi: if he makes a pen from stone or thorns and gathers sheep while he is sleeping near them, he is cut. And from Muhammad: he is cut whether there is a guard or not, and this is the view of most scholars.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage from the Sharh of Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses two key issues in the law of theft (sariqa). First, it restates the general rule: the hadd of amputation (qat') applies only when the stolen item is physically removed from the entire house/property (dar). Moving from one room to another within the same house does not constitute removal. Similarly, if the thief throws the item onto the road from inside and then retrieves it, that counts as removal because the road is outside the dar. Driving a loaded donkey out also counts as removal. Second, the passage discusses livestock theft in open pastures (mar'a). There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Muhammad held that amputation applies regardless of a guard, while Abu Hanifa distinguished between a shepherd (ra'i) whose presence does not make the animals a proper hirs and a dedicated guard (hafiz). Later scholars reconciled these views. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is Muhammad's view that amputation applies even without a guard, as stated by 'amma al-mashayikh.

Key Terms
حرز (hirs): A secure place of storage; the item must be in a hirs for amputation to apply.
سارق (sariq): A thief who takes property from a hirs secretly.
قطع (qat‘): The hadd punishment of amputation of the right hand.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following constitutes removal from the house (dar) for the hadd of theft?
A) Moving the item from one room to another
B) Throwing the item onto the road from inside the house
C) Hiding the item inside a cupboard
D) Handing the item to an accomplice inside the house

Key Takeaway
Theft from a house requires removal from the entire house for the hadd to apply; mere movement within the house is insufficient.

The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Livestock Theft and the Role of a Guard
Explains the intra-Hanafi dispute on livestock theft and the guard condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad regarding theft of livestock from pasture and state the relied-upon position.

Core Idea
A shepherd does not make livestock a hirs; a dedicated guard does — the later majority ruled with Muhammad: cut applies regardless.

The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement on whether the hadd of amputation applies when livestock are stolen from open pasture if a guard is present. This lesson analyzes the positions of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad and traces the preferred view that has become the mufta bihi.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule for livestock in pasture
Livestock in open pasture are not considered to be in a hirs (secure place), so normally no cut applies.
This is the baseline position.
2
Identify the dispute
Abu Hanifa holds that even with a shepherd (ra'i), no cut applies because the shepherd does not intend to protect against thieves. Muhammad holds that the presence of any guard (hafiz) makes them a hirs, so cut applies.
The dispute centers on whether a shepherd qualifies as a guard.
3
Analyze the reasoning
The passage reconciles the views by distinguishing between a shepherd (whose role is not to guard against thieves) and a dedicated guard. A shepherd is not considered to make the animals a hirs.
This reconciliation appears in Fath al-Qadir and al-Mujtaba.
4
Follow the later tarjih
Many later scholars (including those in al-Baqqali, al-Muntaqa) initially followed Abu Hanifa, but the majority eventually adopted Muhammad's view. The text states 'وعليه عامة المشايخ' (the view of most scholars).
Muhammad's view is the mufta bihi.
5
Determine the practical consequence
Under the mufta bihi, a thief who steals livestock from pasture will be subject to amputation even if only a shepherd is present, as long as there is some form of guardianship.
This is the operational ruling for fatwa.

Source Text
