Theft of Worn Items and Guest Theft
Expansion on cases where hadd is not applied due to absence of proper hirs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on stealing worn items and explain why a guest's theft does not incur hadd amputation.

Core Idea
Items worn are not a hirs, so no hadd; a guest's taking is betrayal, not theft, even from locked rooms.

This lesson examines two scenarios where the hadd of theft is not applied despite an item being taken: when the item is worn by the victim, and when a guest steals from the host. The key is the concept of hirs (secure storage) and the principle that a guest, being authorized, is like a resident whose taking is betrayal. Understanding these exceptions helps clarify the strict conditions for amputation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on worn items
Read the first part: stealing a garment, cloak, cap, belt, or jewelry worn by the victim does not incur amputation. Note the minority opinion that does cut.
The base ruling is no cut; the minority is not mufta bihi.
2
Understand the guest theft ruling
Read the second part: a guest who steals from the host is not cut. The house is not a hirs for the guest due to permission.
This applies even if the guest steals from a locked chest within the house.
3
Grasp the concept of khiyana
The guest's act is classified as betrayal (khiyana), not theft (sariqa), because the house is not a hirs for someone authorized to enter.
Khiyana incurs civil liability (daman) but not hadd.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Identify the contested point: whether stealing an item placed near (but not on) a sleeping person counts as hirs violation. The majority says no; the minority says yes.
The text relies on al-Mujtaba and al-Khulasah for the majority view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأشار المصنف بالحضرة إلى أن الثياب ليست عليه فلو سرق من رجل ثوباً عليه أو رداء أو قلنسوة أو منطقة أو سرق من امرأة نائمة حلياً عليها لم يقطع وكذا إذا سرق من رجل نائم عليه ملاءة وهو لابسها لم يقطع وقيل يقطع كالموضوع عنده ؛ كذا في المجتبى. وقيد بما ليس بحرز لما في الخلاصة جماعة نزلوا بيتاً أو خاناً فسرق بعضهم من بعض متاعاً وصاحب المتاع يحفظه أو تحت رأسه لم يقطع ولو كان في مسجد جماعة قطع قوله: (ولو سرق ضيف ممن أضافه أو سرق شيئاً ولم يخرج من الدار (لا أي لا يقطع ; أما الأول فلأن البيت لم يبق حرزاً في حقه لكونه مأذوناً في دخوله ولأنه بمنزلة أهل الدار فيكون فعله خيانة لا سرقة . أطلقه فشمل ما إذا سرق من البيت الذي أضافه فيه أو من بعض بيوت الدار، سوء كان مقفلاً أو صندوق مقفل - ذكره القدوري في شرحه - لأن الدار مع جميع بيوتها حرز واحد فبالإذن في الدار اختل الحرز في جميع بيوتها . وأما الثاني فلأن الدار كلها حرز واحد فلا بد من الإخراج منها وما فيها في يد صاحبها معنى فتتمكن شبهة عدم الأخذ قيد بالسرقة لأنه يجب الضمان على الغاصب بمجرد الأخذ وإن لم يخرجه من الدار هو الصحيح لأنه يجب مع الشبهة .
And the author pointed out by 'al-hadra' that clothes are not upon him [i.e., not a hirs]. So if he steals from a man a garment on him, or a cloak, or a cap, or a belt, or steals from a sleeping woman jewelry on her, he is not cut. Similarly, if he steals from a sleeping man a sheet while he is wearing it, he is not cut. And it is said he is cut like something placed with him; thus in al-Mujtaba. And it is restricted to what is not a hirs. In al-Khulasah: a group stayed in a house or inn, and one of them stole from another while the owner was guarding it or under his head, he is not cut. But if it was in a mosque, he is cut. His saying: (And if a guest steals from the one who hosted him, or steals something and does not take it out of the house, no) i.e., no cut. As for the first, because the house does not remain a hirs for him since he is permitted to enter, and because he is like the owner, so his act is betrayal not theft. It is unrestricted, so includes if he steals from the house he was hosted in or from some rooms of the house, whether locked or a locked chest - mentioned by al-Quduri in his commentary - because the house with all its rooms is one hirs, so permission in the house disrupts the hirs in all its rooms. As for the second, because the whole house is one hirs, so removal from it is necessary, and what is in it is in the owner's hand in meaning, so a doubt of not taking occurs. It is restricted by theft because guarantee is obligatory on the usurper merely by taking even if he does not take it out of the house; this is correct because it is obligatory with doubt.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on theft rulings. First, it clarifies that items worn by the victim (clothes, jewelry, etc.) are not considered in a hirs (secure place) because they are on the person. Therefore, stealing such items does not incur the hadd amputation, even if the victim is sleeping. There is a minority opinion ('qila') that it does, comparing it to an item placed nearby. The relied-upon position is no cut. Second, the passage addresses theft by a guest. A guest, having been given permission to enter, is like a resident. His taking of property is considered betrayal (khiyana) rather than theft, because the house is no longer a hirs relative to him. This applies even if he steals from a locked room or chest within the house, because the house as a whole is a single hirs, and the permission disrupts it. Similarly, if someone other than a guest steals but does not take the item out of the house, no cut applies because the entire house is one hirs until the item is physically removed outside.

Key Terms
حرز (hirs): A secure storage; items on the body are not hirs.
خيانة (khiyana): Betrayal; guest's taking is betrayal, not theft.
ضمان (daman): Liability for the value; the guest must guarantee the item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does a guest who steals from his host not receive the hadd amputation?
A) Because the value is too low
B) Because he did not take it out of the house
C) Because the house is not a hirs for him due to permission
D) Because he is a family member

Key Takeaway
Theft from the person (worn items) and theft by a guest do not meet the criteria for hadd amputation due to the absence of a valid hirs.

Scenarios of Theft: When Amputation Applies and When It Does Not
Provides detailed explanation of four scenarios and four cases where no cut applies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between four scenarios where amputation applies and four where it does not, and explain the Hanafi position on the throw-and-retrieve trick using the concepts of hirs and intervening hand.

Core Idea
Amputation requires complete removal from a valid hirs without an intervening valid hand; clever tricks do not avoid the hadd.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq expands on the conditions for amputation in theft. It presents four situations where the hadd is imposed and four where it is not, clarifying the role of the hirs and the concept of an intervening hand (yad mu'tabara). A key dispute with Zufar is highlighted regarding the thief who throws the item out then retrieves it, emphasizing the importance of custom ('urf) in determining the continuity of removal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four application scenarios
List the four scenarios given in the first part of the passage where amputation is applied. Note the common element: each involves removal from a hirs without an intervening hand.
These are: (1) moving from a compartment to the courtyard, (2) attacking from one room to another, (3) throwing out and retrieving, (4) loading on a donkey and driving out.
2
Identify the four non-application scenarios
List the four scenarios in the second part where amputation is not applied. Note the missing element: either the removal is incomplete due to an intervening hand, or the hirs is not violated.
These are: (1) handing to another outside, (2) inserting hand into a house without entering, (3) stealing a purse sticking out of a sleeve, (4) stealing from a train (qitar).
3
Analyze the Zufar dispute
Focus on the throw-and-retrieve scenario. Compare the Hanafi view (amputation applies) with Zufar's view (no amputation). Identify the Hanafi response: the act is a single removal because it is a common trick and no valid hand intervenes.
This dispute illustrates the use of custom ('urf) in qiyas.
4
Understand the role of custom ('urf) in hirs determination
Note that compartments in a house are considered separate hirs based on custom. Also, the train of camels (qitar) is not a hirs. Consider how custom defines 'secure place'.
Custom determines whether a place is a hirs; it is not merely physical enclosure.
5
Apply the qiyas reasoning
In the throw-and-retrieve scenario, the illa (effective cause) for amputation is removal from hirs by the thief's action without intervening valid hand. The throwing and picking up are part of a single design; thus the ruling of the asl (direct removal) applies to the far' (trick removal).
This is an example of qiyas khafi (obscure analogy) because the similarity is not immediately apparent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وإن أخرجه من حجرة إلى الدار وأغار من أهل الحجرة على حجرة أخرى أو نقب فدخل وألقى شيئاً في الطريق ثم أخذه أو حمله على حمار فساقه وأخرجه قطع) بيان لأربع مسائل : الأولى لو كانت الدار فيها مقاصير فأخرجها من مقصورة إلى صحن الدار فإنه يقطع لأن كل مقصورة باعتبار ساكنها حرز على حدة، فالمراد بالدار الكبيرة التي فيها منازل وفي وجزم بأنه لو سرق من رجل قلادة عليه وهو لابسها أو ملاءة له وهو لابسها أو واضعها قريباً منه يقطع فتأمل . قوله : (فيها مقاصير) قال في معراج الدراية : المقصورة الحجرة بلسان أهل الكوفة. قوله : (ثم حمار فساقه وأخرجه قطع وإن ناوله آخر من خارج أو أدخل يده في بيت فأخذ أو طرصرة خارجة من كم أو سرق من قطار بعيرا أو جملاً لا فهي المسألة كل منزل مكان يستغنى به أهله عن الانتفاع بصحن الدار وإنما ينتفعون به انتفاع السكة وإلا السابقة التي لا بد فيها من الإخراج من الدار الثانية لو أغار إنسان من أهل المقاصير على مقصورة فسرق منها قطع لما بينا والمراد أنه دخل مقصورة على غرة فأخذ بسرعة. يقال أغار الفرس والثعلب في العدو إذا أسرع. الثالثة اللص إذا نقب البيت فدخل وأخذ المال ثم ألقاه في الطريق ثم خرج وأخذه فإنه يقطع . وقال زفر : لا يقطع لأن الإلقاء غير موجب للقطع كما لو خرج ولم يأخذ فكذا الأخذ السكة كما لو أخذه غيره. ولنا أن من الرمي حيلة يعتادها السراق لتعذر الخروج مع المتاع أو ليتفرغ لقتال صاحب الدار وللفرار ولم تعترض عليه يد معتبرة فاعتبر الكل فعلاً واحداً قيد بقوله ثم أخذه» لأنه لو لم يأخذه فهو مضيع لا سارق، وكذا لو أخذه غيره الرابعة لو حمله على حمار وساقه وأخرجه لأن سيره مضاف إليه بسوقه. قيد بالسوق لأنه لو لم يسقه وخرج بنفسه لم يقطع . والمراد أن يكون متسبباً في إخراجه فيشمل ما إذا علقه في عنق كلب وزجره، ولو خرج بغير زاجر لم يقطع لأن للدابة اختياراً فما لم يفسد اختيارها بالحمل والسوق لا ينقطع نسبة الفعل إليها. وكذا إذا علقه على طائر فطار به إلى منزل السارق فإنه لا يقطع ويشمل ما لو ألقاه في نهر في الدار وكان الماء ضعيفاً وأخرجه بتحريك السارق لأن الإخراج مضاف إليه وإن أخرجه الماء بقوة جريه لم يقطع، وقيل يقطع وهو الأصح لأنه أخرجه بسببه؛ كذا في النهاية. قوله : (وإن ناوله آخر من خارج أو أدخل يده في بيت فأخذ أو طر صرة خارجة من كم أو سرق من قطار بعيراً أو حملاً لا أي لا يقطع في هذه المسائل الأربع. أما الأولى وهي ما إذا نقب اللص البيت فدخل وأخذ المال وناوله آخر من خارج الدار فلا قطع عليهما لأن الأول لم يوجد منه الإخراج لاعتراض يد معتبرة على المال قبل خروجه. والثاني لم يوجد منه هتك الحرز فلم تتم السرقة من كل واحد. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا أخرج الداخل يده وناولها الخارج أو أدخل يده الخارج فتناولها من يد الداخل وهو ظاهر المذهب، ولم يذكر محمد ما إذا وضع الداخل المال عند النقب ثم خرج وأخذه قيل يقطع والصحيح أنه لا يقطع؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وأما الثانية وهي ما إذا أدخل يده في بيت وأخذ فلما روي عن علي رضي الله عنه أن اللص إذا كان ظريفاً لا يقطع . قيل : وكيف ذلك؟ قال : أن ينقب البيت ويدخل يده من غير أن يدخله ولأنه لم يهتك الحرز قيد بالبيت لأنه لو أدخل يده في الصندوق والجيب ألقاه في الطريق الخ قال في الجوهرة: هذا إذا رمى به في الطريق بحيث يراه وإلا فلا قطع عليه وإن خرج وأخذه لأنه صار مستهلكاً له قبل خروجه بدليل وجوب الضمان عليه، فإذا وجب عليه الضمان باستهلاكه قبل خروجه لم يجب عليه قطع كما لو ذبح الشاة في الحرز وليس كذلك إذا رمى
His saying: (And if he takes it out from a room to the house, or attacks from the people of a room to another room, or breaks in and enters and throws something on the road then takes it, or loads it on a donkey and drives it out, he is cut.) Explanation of four issues: First, if the house has compartments and he takes it from a compartment to the courtyard of the house, he is cut because each compartment is a separate hirs with respect to its resident. So the intended meaning of the large house is one with dwellings... and he was certain that if he steals from a man a necklace on him while he is wearing it, or a sheet while wearing it, or placed near him, he is cut, so reflect. His saying: (in which compartments) says in Mi'raj al-Dirayah: al-maksurah is a room in the language of Kufans. His saying: (then a donkey and he drives it out, he is cut. And if he hands it to another from outside, or inserts his hand into a house and takes, or a purse sticking out from a sleeve, or steals from a train a camel or a load, no) i.e., no cut in these four issues. As for the first, if the thief breaks into the house and takes the money and hands it to another outside the house, there is no cut on either because the first did not effect removal due to a valid hand intervening before the money exits. The second did not violate the hirs, so theft is not complete from either. It is unrestricted, so includes if the insider extends his hand and hands it to the outsider, or the outsider inserts his hand and takes it from the insider's hand; this is the apparent school. Muhammad did not mention if the insider places the money at the breach then goes out and takes it; it is said he is cut, but the correct is that he is not cut; thus in Fath al-Qadir. As for the second, if he inserts his hand into a house and takes... based on what is narrated from Ali that the thief if he is clever, he is not cut. It was said: How is that? He said: that he breaks the house and inserts his hand without entering it, because he did not violate the hirs. Restricted by 'house' because if he inserts his hand into a chest or pocket... he throws it on the road etc. He said in al-Jawharah: This is if he throws it on the road where he can see it, otherwise there is no cut on him even if he goes out and takes it because it became consumed by him before its exit, evidenced by the obligation of guarantee. So if guarantee became obligatory due to consumption before exit, the cut is not obligatory, like if he slaughtered the sheep in the hirs. But it is not like that if he throws...
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This long passage is a detailed explanation of four scenarios where amputation applies and four where it does not. The first set: (1) moving from a compartment to the courtyard – cut because each compartment is a separate hirs. (2) attacking from one room to another – cut. (3) breaking in, throwing the item onto the street, then retrieving – cut. There is an inter-madhhab dispute with Zufar, who argued that throwing out is not sufficient without a valid hand; but the Hanafi response considers it a single act because it is a common trick of thieves. (4) loading onto a donkey and driving out – cut because the driving is attributed to the thief. The passage then discusses details: if the item is thrown into a river with weak current and the thief moves it, cut; if strong current takes it, no cut. The second set (no cut): (1) handing over to another outside – the insider did not complete removal, the outsider did not violate hirs. (2) inserting hand into a house without entering – no cut because the hirs is not breached; a narration from Ali supports this. (3) stealing a purse sticking out from a sleeve – no cut because it is not in a hirs. (4) stealing a camel or load from a train (qitar) – no cut because a train in motion is not a hirs. The passage ends with a discussion about throwing the item where the thief cannot see it – if he then retrieves it, no cut because the item is considered destroyed (mustahlak) and guarantee applies before exit.

Key Terms
حرز (hirs): A secure storage; each room in a multi-room dwelling can be a separate hirs.
يد معتبرة (yad mu'tabara): A legally recognized hand (possession/control) that interrupts the thief's control.
قطار (qitar): A train of camels tied together; not a hirs for individual animals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a thief breaks into a house, takes money, and hands it to an accomplice outside the house before exiting, what is the ruling?
A) Both are cut
B) Neither is cut
C) Only the insider is cut
D) Only the outsider is cut

Key Takeaway
Amputation for theft requires both violation of a valid hirs and complete removal by the thief without an intervening valid hand; clever tricks like throwing and retrieving are considered a single removal based on custom.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Thief Throwing Goods Then Retrieving
Intra-Hanafi dispute on continuity of removal (ikhraj) for theft hadd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school amputates a thief who throws goods onto the road and retrieves them, and evaluate Zufar's counterargument.

Core Idea
Throwing goods out of the hira is a trick, not a break in possession — the whole act is one continuous removal.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his student Zufar ibn al-Hudhayl. The case involves a thief who breaks into a house, takes goods, throws them into the road, then exits and retrieves them. At stake is the application of the fixed penalty (hadd) of hand amputation. The dispute turns on whether the throwing severs the continuity of removal from the secure place (hirz). By mastering this dispute, you will sharpen your ability to apply usul al-fiqh reasoning to nuanced fact patterns.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling held by Abu Hanifa and the majority of the Hanafi school: the thief is amputated because the throwing and retrieval are part of one continuous act.
The position is represented by 'ولنا' in the text.
2
Identify Zufar's objection
State Zufar's view: no amputation because the throwing severs possession, making the retrieval a new taking from the road, analogous to someone else taking it.
Zufar's argument is based on qiyas to a third party's intervention.
3
Follow the Hanafi response
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal: throwing is a trick (hila) commonly used by thieves, no valid hand intervenes, so the whole sequence is one act of removal.
The response emphasizes custom (urf) of thieves.
4
Analyze the subcases from al-Jawhara
Distinguish between throwing where the thief can see the goods (remaining in constructive possession) vs. out of sight (deemed mustahlik, no amputation).
Visibility preserves the continuity of possession for hadd purposes.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Conclude that the practical difference (thamarah) is that Abu Hanifa amputates; Zufar does not. The Hanafi position is mufta bihi within the school.
This dispute illustrates how differing usul principles produce different fiqh outcomes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالثة اللص إذا نقب البيت فدخل وأخذ المال ثم ألقاه في الطريق ثم خرج وأخذه فإنه يقطع . وقال زفر : لا يقطع لأن الإلقاء غير موجب للقطع كما لو خرج ولم يأخذ فكذا الأخذ السكة كما لو أخذه غيره. ولنا أن من الرمي حيلة يعتادها السراق لتعذر الخروج مع المتاع أو ليتفرغ لقتال صاحب الدار وللفرار ولم تعترض عليه يد معتبرة فاعتبر الكل فعلاً واحداً قيد بقوله ثم أخذه» لأنه لو لم يأخذه فهو مضيع لا سارق، وكذا لو أخذه غيره. ألقاه في الطريق الخ قال في الجوهرة: هذا إذا رمى به في الطريق بحيث يراه وإلا فلا قطع عليه وإن خرج وأخذه لأنه صار مستهلكاً له قبل خروجه بدليل وجوب الضمان عليه، فإذا وجب عليه الضمان باستهلاكه قبل خروجه لم يجب عليه قطع كما لو ذبح الشاة في الحرز وليس كذلك إذا رمى به بحيث يراه لأنه باق في يده فإذا خرج وأخذه صار كأنه خرج وهو معه اهـ.
The third issue: If a thief breaks into a house, enters, takes the money, then throws it into the road, then goes out and retrieves it, then he is amputated. Zufar said: He is not amputated because the throwing is not a cause for amputation, just as if he went out and did not take it, so similarly taking it from the road is like if someone else took it. Our proof: This throwing is a trick commonly used by thieves either because they cannot exit with the goods or to fight the owner or to flee, and no valid hand intervened, so the whole is considered one act. It is conditioned by 'then he retrieves it' because if he did not retrieve it, he is a loser, not a thief, and similarly if another retrieved it. The comment in al-Jawhara: This applies if he threw it into the road where he can see it; otherwise there is no amputation even if he goes out and retrieves it because he became a consumer of it before removal, as liability is established, so amputation is not required just as if he slaughtered a sheep in the hira. It is not so if he threw it where he can see it because it remains in his hand, so when he goes out and retrieves it, it is as if he went out with it.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa (whose position is represented by 'we') and Zufar ibn al-Hudhayl, a prominent student of Abu Hanifa. Zufar argues that the thief's act of throwing the stolen goods out of the house before exiting severs the continuity of his possession, so that when he later retrieves it, it is like a new taking from the road, analogous to someone else taking it. The Hanafi position responds by pointing to the common practice of thieves: they throw the goods out to facilitate their own escape or to free their hands for fighting the homeowner. Since no valid hand (like that of a guardian or owner) intervenes, the entire sequence — breaking in, taking, throwing, exiting, and retrieving — is considered one continuous act of removal from the hira. The condition 'then he retrieves it' (ثم أخذه) is crucial: if he does not retrieve it, he is merely a vandal (مضيع) not a thief. Additionally, if he throws it out of sight (لاحيث يراه), the object becomes 'consumed' (مستهلك) in his liability, similar to slaughtering the sheep inside the hira, so amputation is not applied because the taking is considered completed inside. However, if he throws it where he can see it, the object remains in his constructive possession (كأنه معه), so amputation applies upon retrieval.

Key Terms
القطع (al-qat'): Amputation of the hand as the fixed penalty (hadd) for theft.
الإلقاء (al-ilqa'): Throwing the stolen goods out of the hira (usually to the road).
الحرز (al-hirz): A secure place where property is customarily kept protected.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, why does the thief's act of throwing the stolen goods into the road before exiting not prevent amputation?
A) Because the thief is not liable for the thrown goods until he retrieves them.
B) Because the throwing is a common trick used by thieves, and no valid hand intervenes, so the whole act is one continuous removal.
C) Because Zufar's opinion is rejected by consensus.
D) Because the goods are considered consumed (mustahlik) the moment they are thrown.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a thief's act of throwing stolen goods out of the hira does not sever the continuity of removal if he retrieves them, unless the goods are thrown out of sight.

Direct Causation and Attribution in Theft (Hadd al-Sariqa)
Ruling on amputation with animal/bird/river intermediaries

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on amputation when a thief uses an animal, bird, or river to remove stolen goods, and explain how direct causation (tasabbub) determines attribution of the act.

Core Idea
A thief is amputated only if he directly causes removal; animal choice interrupts attribution.

In this passage, al-Khassaf discusses the fourth mas'ala from the matn: a thief loads goods onto a donkey and drives it out of the hira. The key ruling is that amputation applies because the animal's movement is attributed to the thief through his driving. But if the animal walks out on its own, or a bird flies with the goods, the thief is not cut because the animal acts by its own choice. This introduces the critical Hanafi principle of attribution (isnad) based on direct causation versus independent choice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its key condition
The passage states that if the thief loads goods on a donkey and drives it out, he is cut. The condition is 'driving' (sawq). Why is this condition essential? Because without it, the animal's movement is attributed to its own choice, not the thief.
Sawq is a form of direct causation (mubashara) that overrides the animal's independent choice.
2
Apply the principle of animal choice (ikhtiyar)
The text explains that if the thief does not drive the animal and it leaves on its own, no amputation. Similarly, hanging goods on a dog without incitement, or on a bird that flies by its own volition, does not lead to amputation. The legal reason is that the animal has ikhtiyar, and the thief's act does not dominate that choice.
This contrasts with cases where the thief directly leads or incites the animal, where attribution is clear.
3
Extend to the river case: weak vs strong current
If the thief throws goods into weak water and stirs it to bring them out, he is cut because the removal is attributed to him. But if the water's own powerful current carries the goods out, he is not cut according to the primary view. However, a later opinion (qila) says he is cut because he caused the removal by throwing the goods in.
The strong current case raises the question: is throwing into moving water a direct enough cause? The primary view says no because the water acts independently; the later view says yes because the thief initiated the chain.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi disagreement on the river case
Al-Nahr notes a contradiction with the bird case: why is the thief cut for throwing into strong current but not for attaching goods to a bird? The resolution: the bird flies by its own choice, but the water has no choice; it is a natural force. So the thief's act (throwing) is the sole cause, and the water's movement is not volitional. Al-Haddadi therefore ruled no cutting in the river case, aligning with the bird analogy.
This illustrates how Hanafi scholars refine the concept of causation: human choice vs. natural compulsion.
5
Conclude with the general rule
The general Hanafi rule is: for hadd to apply, the removal must be directly attributed to the thief's act. If an intermediary (animal, bird, water) acts with independent choice or natural force that breaks the chain of attribution, amputation is not imposed.
This rule is grounded in the principle that hudud are avoided on the basis of doubt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابعة لو حمله على حمار وساقه وأخرجه لأن سيره مضاف إليه بسوقه. قيد بالسوق لأنه لو لم يسقه وخرج بنفسه لم يقطع . والمراد أن يكون متسبباً في إخراجه فيشمل ما إذا علقه في عنق كلب وزجره، ولو خرج بغير زاجر لم يقطع لأن للدابة اختياراً فما لم يفسد اختيارها بالحمل والسوق لا ينقطع نسبة الفعل إليها. وكذا إذا علقه على طائر فطار به إلى منزل السارق فإنه لا يقطع ويشمل ما لو ألقاه في نهر في الدار وكان الماء ضعيفاً وأخرجه بتحريك السارق لأن الإخراج مضاف إليه وإن أخرجه الماء بقوة جريه لم يقطع، وقيل يقطع وهو الأصح لأنه أخرجه بسببه؛ كذا في النهاية. قوله: (وقيل يقطع وهو الأصح) قال في النهر : يشكل عليه ما مر من مسألة الطائر ولذا والله تعالى أعلم جزم الحدادي بأنه لا قطع ولم يحك غيره .اهـ. وقد يدفع الإشكال بأن الطائر طار باختياره فلم يضف الفعل إلى السارق لأنه عرض على فعله فعل مختار لأن للدابة اختياراً كما مر ونظيره ما قالوه في الغصب : لو حل قيد عبد غيره أو رباط دابته أو فتح باب إصطبلها أو قفص طائره فذهبت لا يضمن.
The fourth issue: If he loads it on a donkey and drives it and brings it out, then [the thief] is cut because its movement is attributed to him by his driving. It is conditioned by driving because if he did not drive it and it went out by itself, he is not cut. The meaning is that he should be the cause of its removal, so it includes if he hangs it on a dog's neck and incites it, and if it goes out without incitement, he is not cut because the animal has choice; as long as he does not override its choice by loading and driving, the attribution of action to him does not cease. Similarly, if he hangs it on a bird and it flies with it to the thief's house, he is not cut. It includes if he throws it into a river inside the house and the water is weak and he brings it out by stirring, because the removal is attributed to him; but if the water brings it out by its own force, he is not cut. It is said he is cut, and that is the correct view, because he caused its removal; as in al-Nihaya. His words: 'It is said he is cut, and it is the correct view' — in al-Nahr: There is a problem with what passed regarding the bird issue, and therefore al-Haddadi definitively said there is no cut and did not relate other views. The difficulty can be resolved by [saying] the bird flew by its own choice, so the act is not attributed to the thief because he juxtaposed his act with a voluntary act, as the animal has choice as previously mentioned. And the analogy is what they said in ghasp: if someone unties a slave's chain or a beast's rope or opens a stable door or a birdcage and they go away, he does not guarantee.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage discusses the fourth mas'ala from the matn: the case of loading stolen goods onto a donkey and driving it out of the hira. The ruling is that the thief is amputated because the removal is attributed to him through his driving (سوق). The condition 'driving' (ساقه) is emphasized: if the animal simply walks out on its own, no amputation because the animal has choice (اختيار). This principle extends to other animals: hanging goods on a dog and inciting it (زجره) attributes the removal to the thief, but if the dog leaves without incitement, or if a bird flies with the goods, no amputation because the animal acts independently. For the river case: if the water is weak and the thief stirs it to bring the goods out, it's attributed to him (cutting applies); if the water's own current brings it out, no cutting (but a later opinion — 'the correct view' — says cutting because he caused it by throwing it in). A later gloss from al-Nahr notes a contradiction with the bird case, resolved by noting that the bird's flight is voluntary, whereas the weak water has no choice, so the thief's causal act (throwing) suffices for attribution. This is analogous to ghasp (usurpation): freeing a slave or opening a cage does not make one liable for the subsequent escape because the being acts by its own choice.

Key Terms
السوق (al-sawq): Driving or leading an animal; a direct causative act that attributes the animal's movement to the driver.
اختيار (al-ikhtiyar): Volition or choice; animals have an element of choice that can interrupt attribution of an act to a human agent.
التسبب (al-tasabbub): Indirect causation; if the thief sets up a cause (like throwing goods into weak water), the removal is attributed to him.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of a thief loading stolen goods onto a donkey, when does amputation NOT apply according to the Hanafi position discussed?
A) When the donkey is driven by the thief.
B) When the thief loads the donkey but does not drive it, and it walks out on its own.
C) When the thief ties the goods to the donkey and leads it gently.
D) When the thief uses a dog to carry the goods by inciting it.

Key Takeaway
For hadd al-sariqa, amputation requires that the thief be the direct cause of removal; if an intermediary acts independently (by choice or natural force), the act is not attributed to the thief and hadd is suspended.

Four Exceptions to Amputation: When Theft Lacks Full Hira Violation
Four exceptions to amputation in theft with detailed reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four cases where amputation is not applied and apply the criterion of hira violation and complete removal to determine the ruling.

Core Idea
Amputation requires both violation of hira and complete removal; if either is missing, the hadd is not applied.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq examines four scenarios where a thief's act does not result in amputation, even though property is taken. The key is that either the hira (secure place) was not fully violated or the removal was not complete from the hira. Understanding these exceptions sharpens your ability to apply the conditions of theft correctly.

Guided Path
1
First case: Handing to another outside
Identify that the insider (who enters and takes) hands the property to an outsider before leaving the hira. The insider did not remove it from the hira (a valid hand intercepted), and the outsider did not break the hira. Thus neither condition is fully met by either.
This applies whether the insider extends his hand or the outsider inserts his hand.
2
Second case: Inserting hand only into a house
If a thief inserts only his hand into a house and takes property without entering his body, he is not amputated because he did not fully violate the hira of the house. The Prophet ﷺ said of a 'clever' thief who does this: he is not cut.
Contrast with boxes or pockets: inserting the hand does violate that hira because the container itself is the hira, and full entry is impossible.
3
Third case: Cutting a protruding purse
A purse (or sleeve-tie) protruding from the sleeve: if the tie is outside, cutting it results in taking from outside the sleeve (no hira violation). If the tie is inside, cutting leaves the purse inside, so taking is from inside (violation). If the thief unties instead of cuts, the ruling reverses accordingly.
Abu Yusuf disagrees and holds that the tarar is always cut. The relied-upon position follows the distinctions.
4
Fourth case: Stealing from a camel train
A line of camels (qitar) in transit is not a protected hira because the driver's purpose is travel, not guarding. Thus stealing a camel or load does not incur amputation. However, if there is a designated guard, it becomes a hira. Also, slitting a sack on a camel and taking contents does require amputation because the sack itself is a hira.
Stealing a tent (fustat) itself is not amputation unless it is folded and guarded.

Source Text

قوله : (وإن ناوله آخر من خارج أو أدخل يده في بيت فأخذ أو طر صرة خارجة من كم أو سرق من قطار بعيراً أو حملاً لا أي لا يقطع في هذه المسائل الأربع. أما الأولى وهي ما إذا نقب اللص البيت فدخل وأخذ المال وناوله آخر من خارج الدار فلا قطع عليهما لأن الأول لم يوجد منه الإخراج لاعتراض يد معتبرة على المال قبل خروجه. والثاني لم يوجد منه هتك الحرز فلم تتم السرقة من كل واحد. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا أخرج الداخل يده وناولها الخارج أو أدخل يده الخارج فتناولها من يد الداخل وهو ظاهر المذهب، ولم يذكر محمد ما إذا وضع الداخل المال عند النقب ثم خرج وأخذه قيل يقطع والصحيح أنه لا يقطع؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وأما الثانية وهي ما إذا أدخل يده في بيت وأخذ فلما روي عن علي رضي الله عنه أن اللص إذا كان ظريفاً لا يقطع . قيل : وكيف ذلك؟ قال : أن ينقب البيت ويدخل يده من غير أن يدخله ولأنه لم يهتك الحرز قيد بالبيت لأنه لو أدخل يده في الصندوق والجيب والكم ونحوه فإنه يقطع لأن الممكن فيها إدخال اليد لا الدخول بخلاف ما إذا شق الجولق فتبدد ما فيه من الدراهم فأخذه لا يقطع لعدم الهتك. وأما الثالثة وهي ما إذا طر صرة خارجة من كم فلان الرباط من خارج فبالطر لا تبقى الصرة داخل الكم فيتحقق الأخذ من الخارج فلم يوجد هتك الحرز . قيد بكونها خارجة لأنه إن طر صرة داخلة وأخذها قطع لأن الرباط من داخل فبالطر تبقى الصرة داخل الكم فتحقق الأخذ من الداخل فيوجد الهتك . والطر الشق. وذكر الشمني أن المراد بالصرة بعض الكم المشدود فيه الدراهم. وقيد بالطر لأنه لو كان مكانه حل الرباط انعكس الحكم لانعكاس العلة فيقطع إن كان الرباط خارج الكم لأنه يأخذ الدراهم من داخله، ولا يقطع إن كان الرباط من داخل الكم لأنه يأخذها من خارجه. وفي فتح القدير : وبما ذكر من التفصيل في الطر ظهر أن ما يطلق في الأصول من أن الطرار يقطع إنما يتأتى على قول أبي يوسف فإنه قال يقطع الطرار على كل حال اهـ. وأما الرابعة وهي ما إذا سرق من قطار بعيراً أو حملاً عليه فإنه ليس بمحرز مقصوداً فيتمكن فيه شبهة العدم أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان معها سائق أو قائد أو لم يكن لأن السائق أو الراكب يقصد قطع المسافة ونقل الأمتعة دون الحفظ حتى لو كان معها من يحفظها يقطع والقطار الإبل على نسق واحد والجمع قطر وقيد بسرقة الجمل لأنه لو شق الجولق على الجمل وهو يسير وأخذ ما فيه فإنه يقطع لأن صاحب المال اعتمد الجوالق فكان هاتكاً للحرز بخلاف ما إذا أخذ الجولق بما فيه، وكذا لو سرق من الفسطاط فإنه يقطع ولو سرق نفس الفسطاط فإنه لا يقطع لعدم إحرازه إلا إذا كان الفسطاط غير منصوب وإنما هو ملفوف عنده من يحفظه أو في فسطاط آخر فإنه يقطع ؛ كذا في فتح القدير
His statement: 'And if he hands it to another from outside, or inserts his hand into a house and takes, or cuts a purse protruding from a sleeve, or steals from a camel train a camel or a load — no [amputation]' — meaning there is no amputation in these four issues. As for the first: if a thief breaks into a house, enters, takes the money, and hands it to another outside the house, there is no amputation on either because the first did not effect removal (a valid hand intervened before exit) and the second did not break the hira, so theft is not completed by either. It is general, including if the insider extends his hand and the outsider takes, or the outsider inserts his hand and takes from the insider's hand — this is the apparent school. Muhammad did not mention the case where the insider places the money at the hole then goes out and takes it; some say he is cut, but the correct view is no cut; as in Fath al-Qadir. As for the second: inserting the hand into a house and taking — based on what is narrated from Ali (RA) that 'if the thief is clever, he is not cut.' It was asked: How is that? He said: He breaks the house and inserts his hand without entering, because he did not violate the hira. It is conditioned by 'house' because if he inserts his hand into a box, pocket, sleeve, etc., he is cut, because one can only insert the hand, not the whole body, unlike if he slits a sack and the dirhams scatter and he takes them — no cut due to lack of violation of hira. As for the third: cutting a purse protruding from a sleeve — the tie is outside, so by cutting, the purse does not remain inside the sleeve, so the taking is from outside, so no violation of hira. Conditioned by 'protruding' because if he cuts an inner purse and takes it, he is cut because the tie is inside, so by cutting the purse remains inside the sleeve, so the taking is from inside, so violation exists. 'Tar' means cutting. Al-Shumni said that 'purse' here means a part of the sleeve tied with dirhams. Conditioned by 'cutting' because if instead of cutting he untied, the ruling reverses accordingly: he is cut if the tie is outside the sleeve because he takes the dirhams from inside; he is not cut if the tie is inside the sleeve because he takes from outside. In Fath al-Qadir: from the detail on cutting, it appears that the general statement that the 'tarar' (purse-cutter) is cut only applies according to Abu Yusuf, who said the tarar is cut in all cases. As for the fourth: stealing from a camel train a camel or a load — it is not a deliberately protected hira, so there is a doubt of absence. It is general, including whether there is a driver or leader or not, because the driver or rider aims to travel and transport goods, not guard, to the extent that if there is someone guarding, he is cut. 'Qitar' is camels in a line, plural 'qutur'. Conditioned by stealing a camel because if he slits the sack on a moving camel and takes what is inside, he is cut because the owner relied on the sack, so he violated the hira, unlike if he takes the sack with its contents. Similarly, if he steals from a tent (fustat), he is cut; but if he steals the tent itself, he is not cut because it is not a hira, unless the tent is not erected but folded and with someone guarding it or inside another tent, then he is cut; as in Fath al-Qadir.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage explains four cases from the matn where the hadd of amputation is not applied despite apparent theft. The first case involves two persons: one enters the hira and takes the money, then hands it to another outside. Neither is amputated: the insider did not remove the goods from the hira since a valid hand (the outsider's) intercepted it before exit, and the outsider did not break the hira. This applies whether the insider extends his hand or the outsider inserts his hand into the house. A sub-case: if the insider places the goods at the hole, then exits and retrieves them, the correct view is no amputation (the placement is like an interruption). The second case: inserting only the hand into a house and taking. Citing a hadith from Ali (RA), if the thief is 'clever' (ظريف) meaning he inserts only his hand without entering, he is not amputated because he did not violate the hira (the house's integrity). But if it is a box, pocket, or sleeve, he is amputated because only the hand can enter; full entry is not possible, so the hira is the container. The third case: cutting a purse that protrudes from a sleeve. If the tie is outside, cutting severs the purse from inside? Actually, if the purse protrudes, cutting the tie from outside means the purse remains outside? The text details that if the tie is outside, cutting results in taking from outside (no violation); if the tie is inside, cutting leaves the purse inside, so taking is from inside (violation). This reverses if the thief unties instead of cutting. Abu Yusuf holds that the purse-cutter (الطرّار) is always cut. The fourth case: stealing from a camel train (qitar). A caravan of camels is not considered a properly guarded hira because the driver's purpose is travel, not security. But if someone is specifically guarding, then amputation applies. Similarly, if the thief slits a sack on a moving camel and takes the contents, he is cut because the owner relied on the sack as a hira. For a tent (fustat): stealing an object inside the tent is amputation, but stealing the tent itself is not unless it is folded and guarded.

Key Terms
الطر (al-tarr): Cutting a purse or part of a sleeve to steal money; the act of a 'tarar' (purse-cutter).
القطار (al-qitar): A line of camels in a caravan; considered not a proper hira due to the purpose of travel.
الحرز (al-hirz): A secure place that protects property; violation of hira is required for amputation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of a thief inserting his hand into a box (not a house) and taking money, what is the ruling according to the passage?
A) He is not amputated because he did not enter the box.
B) He is amputated because the hira of the box is violated by inserting the hand.
C) He is not amputated because a box is not a hira.
D) He is amputated only if he cuts the box open.

Key Takeaway
For the hadd of theft to apply, both violation of a valid hira and complete removal of property from it are required; missing either condition drops the amputation.

Cases Requiring Amputation: Load, Sack, Hand in Container
Ruling that amputation applies when thief violates the hira

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three cases where amputation is applied and identify the condition of violating the hira.

Core Idea
Amputation requires theft from within the hira; taking spilled goods from the ground does not violate the hira.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra’iq concludes the section on theft rulings by presenting clear-cut scenarios where the hadd (amputation) is applied. It reinforces that the key element is violation of the hira (the secure container). The commentary adds a critical nuance: if the thief merely picks up scattered dirhams that fell out of a slit sack, he is not amputated because his hand never entered the hira. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the hadd punishment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three main cases
The matn lists: (1) slitting a load and stealing from it, (2) stealing a sack that the owner is guarding or sleeping on, (3) inserting the hand into a box, pocket, or sleeve and taking money.
All three involve taking from a hira (secure place).
2
Recognize the common element: theft from hira
Each case shares the condition that the property was in a hira at the moment of taking. The load, sack, box, pocket, and sleeve are all considered hira when properly guarded.
Hira is a place where property is stored securely.
3
Apply the hatk (violation) condition
Amputation requires hatk: the thief must physically enter the hira space. For a container, this means inserting the hand or tool into it. Simply slitting and taking spilled goods does not constitute hatk.
Hatk is achieved by reaching inside the hira.
4
Distinguish the scattered dirhams scenario
The commentary clarifies: if the sack is slit and dirhams fall out, and the thief picks them up from the ground without inserting his hand, there is no amputation because the hira remains unviolated.
Taking from the ground is not taking from within the hira.
5
Connect to the moving camel example
The passage references an earlier case: slitting a sack on a moving camel and taking from inside it results in amputation, because the hand entered the sack. This is consistent with the hatk requirement.
The camel's movement does not change the hira status; the sack itself is the hira.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإن شق الحمل فسرق منه أو سرق جوالقاً فيه متاع وربه يحفظه أو نائم عليه أو أدخل يده في صندوق أو جيب غيره أو كمه فأخذ المال قطع) لوجود السرقة من الحرز وقدمنا كل ذلك والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم. قوله : (فتبدد ما فيه من الدراهم فأخذه أي أخذه من الأرض مثلاً ولم يدخل يده فيه، أما إن أدخل يده فأخذ يقطع لوجود الهتك كما صرح به الزيلعي وعليه يحمل ما يأتي من قوله «لو شق الجولق على الجمل وهو يسير وأخذ ما فيه فإنه يقطع».
His statement: 'And if he slits the load and steals from it, or steals a sack containing goods while its owner is guarding it or sleeping on it, or inserts his hand into a box or pocket of another or his sleeve and takes the money — [he] is cut,' because the theft occurred from a hira. We have already presented all that. And Allah knows best. His statement: 'So if the dirhams in it scatter and he takes them' — meaning he takes them from the ground for example, and did not insert his hand into it. But if he inserts his hand and takes, he is cut due to violation of hira, as stated by al-Zayla'i, and upon this is based what follows: 'If he slits the sack on the camel while it is moving and takes what is in it, he is cut.'
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage concludes the section on theft by listing cases where amputation is definitely applied. The matn (from Kanz al-Daqaiq) states that amputation applies in three scenarios: (1) slitting a load (حمل) and stealing from it; (2) stealing a sack (جوالق) that contains goods while the owner is either actively guarding it or sleeping on it (sleeping on it indicates it is his hira); (3) inserting the hand into a box, pocket, or sleeve and taking money. The commentary adds a clarification: if the sack is slit and the dirhams scatter, and the thief picks them up from the ground without inserting his hand into the sack, he is not amputated because he did not violate the hira (the sack itself). However, if he inserts his hand into the sack (even after slitting), he is amputated. This is consistent with the earlier principle that the hira of a sack is violated only by entering the hand. The example of slitting a sack on a moving camel (mentioned earlier in the fourth case of the prior lesson) is cited to show that taking from inside such a sack is amputation because the owner relied on the sack as a hira. Thus, the key is violation of the hira: if the thief's hand enters the container that serves as hira, amputation applies; if he merely takes spilled goods from the ground, no amputation.

Key Terms
الجوالق (al-jawaliq): A large sack or container used for storing goods; considered a hira when the owner protects it.
الهتك (al-hatk): Violation or breach of the hira; necessary for amputation.
الرباط (al-ribat): The tie or fastening of a purse; its position (inside or outside the sleeve) determines the ruling on amputation for the purse-cutter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A thief slits a sack full of dirhams, and the coins scatter on the ground. He picks them up from the ground without putting his hand into the sack. What is the ruling?
A) He is amputated because he stole from the sack.
B) He is not amputated because he did not violate the hira of the sack.
C) He is amputated only if the sack was guarded by the owner.
D) He is not amputated because the dirhams fell out by themselves.

Key Takeaway
Amputation for theft requires the thief to actually enter the hira (by hand or tool) and take property from within it; taking scattered goods from outside the hira does not meet the condition and thus no hadd is applied.

The Ruling on the Tarrār (Cutpurse): Inside vs. Outside Tie
Intra-Hanafi dispute on tarrār cutpurse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on the tarrār (cutpurse) and identify the practical difference between Abu Hanifa's and Abu Yusuf's positions.

Core Idea
Amputation for tarr depends on tie location: inside = hirz breach, outside = no breach.

The classical Hanafi texts discuss several nuanced scenarios of theft to determine when the hadd (amputation) applies. One key scenario is the tarrār—a cutpurse who slits a purse from a sleeve. The ruling hinges on whether the thief's hand enters the secure place (hirz) or not. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning behind the distinction of the tie's location, and highlights an internal disagreement between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Understanding this will sharpen your ability to apply the concept of hirz in theft cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Variation
Recognize that the passage discusses two variations: (1) the purse tie is outside the sleeve, and (2) the tie is inside the sleeve. Determine how each affects the breach of hirz.
This is a classic example of `illa` analysis: the effective cause is entering the hirz.
2
Trace the Ruling for Each Variation
For outside tie: the purse is taken from outside, no breach → no amputation. For inside tie: the purse remains inside, hand enters hirz → amputation. State the ruling clearly.
The condition 'protruding' (khārija) is crucial; if the purse is internal, the tie location inside makes it a hirz.
3
Identify the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Note that Abu Yusuf holds: the tarrār is always amputated regardless of tie location, because cutting itself is a breach. Abu Hanifa distinguishes based on tie location.
The text from Fatḥ al-Qadīr clarifies that the general rule 'tarrār is amputated' only holds according to Abu Yusuf.
4
Determine the Muftā Bihī Position
The relied-upon position is the detailed distinction (Abu Hanifa's view). Abu Yusuf's view is reported but not operative. Understand the tarjīḥ mechanism.
This is a case of legitimate ikhtilāf within the school; the later authorities chose the more precise view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو سرق جوالقاً فيه متاع وربه يحفظه أو نائم عليه أو أدخل يده في صندوق أو جيب غيره أو كمه فأخذ المال قطع. والكم ونحوه فإنه يقطع لأن الممكن فيها إدخال اليد لا الدخول بخلاف ما إذا شق الجولق فتبدد ما فيه من الدراهم فأخذه لا يقطع لعدم الهتك. وأما الثالثة وهي ما إذا طر صرة خارجة من كم فلان الرباط من خارج فبالطر لا تبقى الصرة داخل الكم فيتحقق الأخذ من الخارج فلم يوجد هتك الحرز. قيد بكونها خارجة لأنه إن طر صرة داخلة وأخذها قطع لأن الرباط من داخل فبالطر تبقى الصرة داخل الكم فتحقق الأخذ من الداخل فيوجد الهتك. والطر الشق. وذكر الشمني أن المراد بالصرة بعض الكم المشدود فيه الدراهم. وقيد بالطر لأنه لو كان مكانه حل الرباط انعكس الحكم لانعكاس العلة فيقطع إن كان الرباط خارج الكم لأنه يأخذ الدراهم من داخله، ولا يقطع إن كان الرباط من داخل الكم لأنه يأخذها من خارجه. وفي فتح القدير: وبما ذكر من التفصيل في الطر ظهر أن ما يطلق في الأصول من أن الطرار يقطع إنما يتأتى على قول أبي يوسف فإنه قال يقطع الطرار على كل حال اهـ. وأما الرابعة وهي ما إذا سرق من قطار بعيراً أو حملاً عليه فإنه ليس بمحرز مقصوداً فيتمكن فيه شبهة العدم أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان معها سائق أو قائد أو لم يكن لأن السائق أو الراكب يقصد قطع المسافة ونقل الأمتعة دون الحفظ حتى لو كان معها من يحفظها يقطع والقطار الإبل على نسق واحد والجمع قطر وقيد بسرقة الجمل لأنه لو شق الجولق على الجمل وهو وأخذ ما فيه فإنه يقطع لأن صاحب المال اعتمد الجوالق فكان هاتكاً للحرز بخلاف ما إذا أخذ الجولق بما فيه، وكذا لو سرق من الفسطاط فإنه يقطع ولو سرق نفس الفسطاط فإنه لا يقطع لعدم إحرازه إلا إذا كان الفسطاط غير منصوب وإنما هو ملفوف عنده من يحفظه أو في فسطاط آخر فإنه يقطع؛ كذا في فتح القدير قوله: (وإن شق الحمل فسرق منه أو سرق جوالقاً فيه متاع وربه يحفظه أو نائم عليه أو أدخل يده في صندوق أو جيب غيره أو كمه فأخذ المال (قطع) لوجود السرقة من الحرز وقدمنا كل ذلك والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم. به بحيث يراه لأنه باق في يده فإذا خرج وأخذه صار كأنه خرج وهو معه اهـ. قوله: (وقيل يقطع وهو الأصح) قال في النهر: يشكل عليه ما مر من مسألة الطائر ولذا والله تعالى أعلم جزم الحدادي بأنه لا قطع ولم يحك غيره.اهـ. وقد يدفع الإشكال بأن الطائر طار باختياره فلم يضف الفعل إلى السارق لأنه عرض على فعله فعل مختار لأن للدابة اختياراً كما مر ونظيره ما قالوه في الغصب: لو حل قيد عبد غيره أو رباط دابته أو فتح باب إصطبلها أو قفص طائره فذهبت لا يضمن. قوله: (فتبدد ما فيه من الدراهم فأخذه أي أخذه من الأرض مثلاً ولم يدخل يده فيه، أما إن أدخل يده فأخذ يقطع لوجود الهتك كما صرح به الزيلعي وعليه يحمل ما يأتي من قوله «لو شق الجولق على الجمل وهو يسير وأخذ ما فيه فإنه يقطع».
Or if he steals a sack containing goods while its owner is guarding it or sleeping on it, or puts his hand into a box, or another's pocket, or his sleeve, and takes the property: (the thief) is amputated. The sleeve and similar (garments) are such that he is amputated because it is possible to insert the hand but not the whole body, unlike the case where he tears the sack so that the dirhams inside scatter and then he takes them: he is not amputated due to lack of (breach of) security. As for the third (case): when he cuts a purse protruding from someone's sleeve, the tie being from outside: by cutting, the purse does not remain inside the sleeve, so the taking is verified as from outside, thus no breach of security occurred. It is conditioned that it be protruding because if he cuts an internal purse and takes it, he is amputated because the tie is from inside, so by cutting the purse remains inside the sleeve, so the taking is from inside, thus breach occurs. 'Tarr' means cutting. Al-Shumnī said that by 'purse' is meant a part of the sleeve in which dirhams are tied. It is conditioned by cutting because if instead he untied the knot, the ruling is reversed due to reversal of the cause: he is amputated if the tie was outside the sleeve because he takes the dirhams from inside, and not amputated if the tie was inside the sleeve because he takes them from outside. In Fatḥ al-Qadīr: From the detailed discussion of tarr it appears that the general principle that the tarrār is amputated only holds according to Abū Yūsuf, for he said the tarrār is amputated in all cases. As for the fourth (case): if he steals from a caravan a camel or a load on it: that is not a (properly) protected place intentionally, so there is a doubt of absence (of hirz). It is general, covering whether there is a driver or leader or not, because the driver or rider intends to cover distance and transport goods, not protection; but if there is someone guarding them, he is amputated. 'Qitār' is camels in a single line, plural 'quṭr'. It is conditioned by stealing the camel because if he tears the sack on the camel while it is moving and takes what is inside, he is amputated because the owner relied on the sack, so he breached the security, unlike if he took the sack with its contents. Similarly, if he steals from a tent, he is amputated; but if he steals the tent itself, he is not amputated due to lack of being a secured place, unless the tent is not pitched but folded with someone guarding it, or in another tent, then he is amputated. Likewise in Fatḥ al-Qadīr. He said: (If he tears the load and steals from it, or steals a sack containing goods while its owner is guarding it or sleeping on it, or puts his hand into a box or another's pocket or sleeve and takes the property, he is amputated) because the theft occurred from a secure place; we have presented all that. And Allah knows best. (With) such that he sees it because it remains in his hand; when he goes out and takes it, it is as if he went out with it. He said: (It is said he is amputated, and that is the most correct.) In al-Nahr: This is problematic with the previous issue of the bird, so al-Ḥaddādī definitively stated that there is no amputation and did not narrate otherwise. The problem may be resolved by saying that the bird flew by its own choice, so the act is not attributed to the thief because he presented to his act a chosen act (of the bird), since the animal has choice as previously. Analogy is what they said in usurpation: if one unties another's slave's shackle, or his animal's tether, or opens the door of his stable or birdcage, and it goes away, he does not guarantee. He said: (And the dirhams inside scattered and he took them) meaning he took them from the ground, for example, and did not put his hand into it. But if he put his hand in and took, he is amputated due to breach, as al-Zayla'ī explicitly stated, and upon this is based what comes: 'If he tears the sack on the camel while it is moving and takes what is inside, he is amputated.'
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage is an expanded commentary (sharh) on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, discussing several scenarios of theft and whether the hadd (amputation) is applied. The general principle is that amputation requires the thief to directly breach a secure place (hirz). The text first considers a sack: if the thief inserts his hand into the sack and takes the property, he is amputated because the sack is the hirz. But if he tears the sack, causing the coins to fall out, and then picks them up from the ground, he is not amputated because he did not directly breach the hirz—the coins are no longer inside. The third case (الثالثة) involves a purse (صرة) that has been cut (طر) from outside a sleeve. The ruling depends on where the tie is. If the tie is outside, the purse is taken from outside, so no breach occurs and no amputation. If the tie is inside, the purse remains inside the sleeve when cut, so the thief's hand enters the sleeve (a hirz), and amputation applies. This is a fine application of the concept of 'illa: the effective cause is entering the hirz. The text then notes a dispute: according to Abu Yusuf, the tarrār (cutpurse) is always amputated regardless of the tie's location, because he considers the act of cutting itself a breach. Abu Hanifa's view (implied) distinguishes the tie's location. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is detailed in Fath al-Qadir, which clarifies that the general rule about the tarrār being amputated applies only according to Abu Yusuf. The fourth case (الرابعة) concerns stealing from a caravan. A camel or its load is not considered a secure place (hirz maqsud) because the caravan is in motion; the driver aims to travel, not to secure items. However, if a specific guard is assigned, then it becomes a hirz and amputation applies. Similarly, a tent: stealing from inside a tent warrants amputation, but stealing the tent itself does not, unless it is folded and stored securely. The passage also addresses a difficult case: if a thief tears open a load on a moving camel and takes the contents, he is amputated because the sack is the hirz. But if he takes the entire sack, he is not amputated because the sack itself is not a hirz—it is simply a container. Further comments clarify that seeing the property after taking it does not affect the ruling if the taking is complete. The text also discusses a subsidiary issue: if coins scatter from a torn sack, and the thief picks them up without putting his hand inside, no amputation. But if he puts his hand inside the tear, he is amputated. Finally, the commentary resolves an apparent contradiction regarding a bird: if a thief opens a cage and the bird flies away, he is not liable for the bird (in ghasp) because the bird chose to fly. This is analogized to the thief's act: if he only opens the cage and the bird flies on its own, no theft. The overall lesson teaches that amputation in Hanafi fiqh is strictly limited to direct, forceful breach of a secure repository.

Key Terms
الطر (al-tarr): The act of cutting or slitting a purse or sleeve to steal its contents.
الصرة (al-surra): A purse or a tied portion of a sleeve containing coins.
الحرز (al-hirz): A secure place where property is kept; breach of hijz is required for amputation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of a tarrār (cutpurse) where the purse is protruding from the sleeve and the tie is outside, what is the relied-upon (muftā bihī) Hanafi ruling?
A) Amputation is applied because cutting itself is a breach of hirz.
B) No amputation, because the purse is taken from outside the sleeve, so no breach of hirz.
C) Amputation is applied only if the thief also takes the sleeve.
D) The case is referred to the judge's discretion.

Key Takeaway
Amputation in theft requires the thief to directly enter a secure place (hirz); the location of the tie on a cutpurse determines whether that entry occurred.

Manner of Amputation: Hand, Foot, and Cauterization
Explains the method of amputating the right hand and left foot, and the rule of cauterization.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the amputation of a thief's hand and foot, identify the proof for the right hand and the wrist, and apply the rule of cauterization.

Core Idea
The thief's right hand is cut from the wrist and cauterized; the left foot on second offense.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq details the physical procedure of the hadd for theft. It specifies not only which limbs are cut, but the exact anatomical location and the requirement of cauterization to prevent death. Understanding these details is crucial for correctly applying the hadd.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? What is the hukm level (fard/wajib) for each part?
The amputation of the right hand is fard; cauterization is wajib.
2
Locate the dalil
What proof does the author cite for the amputation and for the specificities?
Quran 5:38 for obligation; reading of Ibn Mas'ud for right hand; hadith for cauterization.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How exactly does the dalil prove the ruling? What linguistic or usul principle is used?
The imperative 'faqta'u' indicates obligation; the mashhur report of Ibn Mas'ud restricts the absolute to the right hand.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Does any school disagree on the place of cutting or the treatment of the hand?
Shafi'i and Hanbali recommend hanging the hand; Abu Thawr/Rafida cut the foot from the foot joint.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Which position is relied upon and why?
Hanafi relies on inherited practice (mutawarith) and the practice of Umar; hanging is discretionary for the imam.

وتقطع يمين السارق من الزند وتحسم ورجله اليسرى إن عاد فإن سرق ثالثاً
فصل في كيفية القطع وإثباته
لما كان القطع حكم السرقة ذكره عقبه لأن حكم الشيء يعقبه قوله: (ونقطع يمين السارق من الزند) لقوله تعالى: {والسارق والسارقة فاقطعوا أيديهما} [المائدة: 38] والمعنى يديهما وحكم اللغة أن ما أضيف من الخلق إلى اثنين لكل واحد واحد أن يجمع مثل قوله تعالى: {فقد صغت قلوبكما} [التحريم: 4] وقد يثنى والأفصح الجمع. وأما كونها اليمين فبقراءة ابن مسعود رضي الله عنه "فاقطعوا أيمانهما" وهي مشهورة فكان خبراً مشهوراً فيقيد إطلاق النص فهذا من تقييد المطلق لا من بيان المجمل لأن الصحيح أنه لا إجمال في الآية وقد قطع عليه السلام اليمين والصحابة رضي الله عنهم. وأما كونه من الزند ـ وهو مفصل الرسغ ويقال الكوع وهو مذكر كما في المغرب - فلأنه المتوارث ومثله لا يطلب له سند بخصوصه كالمتواتر ولا يبالي فيه بكفر الناقلين فضلاً عن فسقهم أو ضعفهم قوله: (وتحسم) أي تكوى كي ينقطع الدم لقوله عليه السلام "فاقطعوه واحسموه" ولأنه لو لم يحسم يفضي إلى التلف والحد زاجر لا متلف - كذا في الهداية - وهو يقتضي وجوبه. وفي المغرب: الحسم أن يغمس في الدهن الذي أغلى وفي فتح القدير: وثمن الزيت وكلفة الحسم على السارق عندنا. والمنقول عن الشافعي وأحمد أنه يسن تعليق يده في عنقه لأنه عليه السلام أمر رواه أبو داود وابن ماجه وعندنا ذلك مطلق للإمام إن رآه ولم يثبت عنه عليه السلام في كل قطعه ليكون سنة قوله: ( ورجله اليسرى إن عاد لقوله عليه السلام «فإن عاد فاقطعوه» وعليه إجماع المسلمين. ولم يذكر المصنف نهاية القطع من الرجل لأنه يقطع من الكعب عند أكثر العلماء وفعل عمر رضي الله عنه ذلك. وقال أبو ثور والروافض: يقطع من القدم من معقد الشراك لأن علياً كان يفعل كذلك ويدع له عقباً يمشي عليها اهـ قوله: (فإن حبس حتى يتوب ولم يقطع) لقول علي رضي الله عنه فيه: إني لأستحي من الله أن لا أدع له يداً يأكل بها ويستنجي بها ورجلاً يمشي عليها فلهذا حاج بقية الصحابة رضي الله عنهم فحجهم فانعقد إجماعاً، ولأنه إهلاك معنى لما فيه من تفويت جنس المنفعة والحد زاجر ولأنه نادر الوجود والزجر فيما يغلب بخلاف القصاص لأنه حق العبد فيستوفي ما أمكن جبراً لحقه. وما ورد من الحديث من قطع يده اليسرى في الثالثة والرجل اليمنى في الرابعة من سرق ثالثاً. فصل في كيفية القطع وإثباته به. نصف قوله: (للإمام أن يقتله سياسة أي إن سرق بعد القطع مرتين لا ابتداء. كذا ذكره بعضهم.
Chapter on the manner of amputation and its proof. And the right hand of the thief is cut from the wrist, and it is cauterized, and his left foot if he returns (to theft), then if he steals a third time... Chapter on the manner of amputation and its proof. Since amputation is the penalty for theft, he mentioned it after it because the ruling of a thing follows it. He said: (And we cut the thief's right hand from the wrist) due to His statement: 'As for the thief, male and female, amputate their hands' (Quran 5:38), meaning their hands. The linguistic rule is that when a created thing is attributed to two (individuals), for each one (the word) is singular, like His statement: 'Your hearts have inclined' (Quran 66:4); it can also be dual, but the plural is more eloquent. As for it being the right hand, it is due to the reading of Ibn Mas‘ūd: 'amputate their right hands,' which is well-known, so it is a mashhūr report, thus it qualifies the absolute text; this is restricting the unrestricted, not clarifying the ambiguous, because the correct view is that there is no ambiguity in the verse. The Prophet (peace be upon him) amputated the right hand, and the Companions did likewise. As for it being from the wrist (zand) – which is the joint of the forearm, also called al-kū‘, and it is masculine as in al-Mughrib – because it is the inherited (practice); such a thing does not require a specific chain like mutawātir, and no attention is paid to the disbelief of the transmitters, let alone their sin or weakness. He said: (and it is cauterized) i.e., it is cauterized so that the blood stops, due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'Amputate it and cauterize it,' and because if it is not cauterized, it leads to death, and the hadd is a deterrent, not a destroyer – thus in al-Hidāyah – and it requires its obligation. In al-Mughrib: 'al-hasm' is to dip it in boiled oil. In Fatḥ al-Qadīr: The price of the oil and the cost of cauterization are upon the thief according to us. It is reported from al-Shāfi‘ī and Aḥmad that it is sunnah to hang his hand from his neck because the Prophet (peace be upon him) ordered it, narrated by Abū Dāwūd and Ibn Mājah; but according to us, that is discretionary for the imam if he sees fit, and it is not established that the Prophet did it in every amputation to be a sunnah. He said: (and his left foot if he returns) due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'If he returns, then amputate him,' and there is consensus of the Muslims upon it. The author did not mention the limit of cutting the foot because it is cut from the ankle according to most scholars, and ‘Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) did that. Abū Thawr and the Rāfiḍa said: it is cut from the foot at the knot of the sandal strap because ‘Alī used to do that and leave a heel for him to walk on. He said: (If he is imprisoned until he repents and is not amputated) due to the statement of ‘Alī about it: 'I am ashamed before Allah to leave him no hand to eat and clean himself with, and no foot to walk on.' Therefore he argued with the rest of the Companions and convinced them, and consensus was formed. Because it is destruction of a limb in meaning due to loss of the type of benefit, and the hadd is a deterrent, and because it is rare in occurrence, and deterrence is for what is predominant, unlike qiṣāṣ because it is the right of the servant, so it is carried out as far as possible to compensate his right. And what is narrated in the hadith about cutting his left hand in the third (offense) and the right foot in the fourth, regarding one who steals a third time. Chapter on the manner of amputation and its proof. He said: (The imam may kill him as policy) i.e., if he steals after two amputations, not initially. Some mentioned this.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage begins a new chapter (fasl) on the manner of amputating a thief and the evidence for it. The author of the matn (Kanz al-Daqa’iq) starts by stating the ruling: the right hand is cut from the wrist (zand), cauterized, and if the thief repeats, the left foot is cut. The commentator (Ibn Nujaym) then expands. First, the proof for cutting the hand is the Quranic verse (al-Ma'idah 5:38) 'amputate their hands.' The linguistically preferred reading is 'a'yudihima' (dual) but the plural 'aydiyahuma' is also valid; the author explains that the verse uses plural because each person has one hand attributed to them. The right hand specifically is proven by the famous reading of Ibn Mas'ud 'amputate their right hands' (aimanahuma). This is a restriction (taqyid) of the absolute, not clarification of ambiguous (tabyin al-mujmal), because the correct position is that the verse is not ambiguous. The practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him) and the Companions confirms this. The wrist (zand) is specified as the place of cutting because this is the inherited practice (mutawarith), which does not need a specific chain like mutawatir; it is accepted even if reported by disbelievers. The second part rules that the stump must be cauterized (hasm) – either by cautery iron or by dipping in boiling oil – to stop bleeding. This is wajib according to al-Hidayah because without it the thief may die, and the hadd is a deterrent, not a destroyer. The cost of oil and cauterization is borne by the thief according to the Hanafi school. The commentator contrasts with Shafi'i and Hanbali views that it is sunnah to hang the severed hand from the thief's neck; Hanafis say that is discretionary for the imam. For the second offense, the left foot is cut. The proof is the hadith 'if he returns, then amputate him,' and there is consensus. The limit of cutting the foot is from the ankle (ka'b), based on the practice of Umar, contrary to Abu Thawr and the Rafidah who cut from the foot joint, leaving the heel. The text then addresses a scenario: if the thief is imprisoned until he repents, he should not be amputated because Ali argued that leaving a hand for eating and cleaning and a foot for walking is more merciful; this became consensus. The final note: if a thief steals a third time after two amputations, the imam may kill him as a deterrent policy (siyasa), not as a fixed hadd. This is because after the hands and feet are cut, further amputation is impossible, and killing is for prevention.

Key Terms
الزند (al-zand): The wrist joint (mufsal al-rusgh); also called al-ku'.
الحسم (al-hasm): Cauterization of the severed limb to stop bleeding.
الكعب (al-ka'b): Ankle bone; the point from which the left foot is cut according to Hanafis.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, from where is the right hand of the thief amputated?
A) From the elbow (mirfaq)
B) From the wrist (zand)
C) From the fingers
D) From the shoulder

Key Takeaway
The hadd for theft in the Hanafi school is specifically the amputation of the right hand from the wrist (on first offense) and the left foot from the ankle (on second offense), with mandatory cauterization to prevent death.
