Hadd for a Thief with a Pre-Existing Amputation: The Principle of Functional Benefit
Ruling with commentary expanding on the principle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a thief with a pre-existing missing or paralyzed limb and explain the underlying principle of tafwīt jins al-manfaʿah (loss of the type of benefit).

Core Idea
A thief whose limb is already non-functional is not cut – the hadd removes benefit, not a dead limb.

This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqā'iq addresses a specific scenario: what if the thief's hand or foot is already missing or paralyzed? The Hanafi school answers that the hadd of cutting is not applied because cutting would not remove any remaining function. Instead, the thief is imprisoned until repentance. The reasoning hinges on the principle of 'loss of the type of benefit' (tafwīt jins al-manfaʿah).

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling and the specific exceptions
The matn states that a thief with a missing or paralyzed left thumb, two missing fingers of the left hand, or a missing or paralyzed right foot is not cut, but imprisoned until repentance.
The exceptions are explicitly named; the ruling applies only to these specific cases.
2
Extract the underlying legal principle
The commentator provides the cause (ʿilla): tafwīt jins al-manfaʿah. The hadd cutting is intended to remove the criminal's ability to commit theft through that limb. If the limb is already non-functional, cutting it would not remove any additional benefit.
The principle is derived from the purpose of the punishment (maqṣid al-sharʿ).
3
Analyze the restrictions on the left hand
Only the left thumb (primary gripper) is considered essential for gripping. Missing a finger other than the thumb does not significantly impair gripping, so the thief is still cut. Similarly, if the left thumb is paralyzed (shalāʾ), the same ruling applies.
The left hand is the non-cut hand; the right hand is the one amputated.
4
Analyze the restrictions on the right foot
If the right foot is missing or paralyzed such that the thief cannot stand or walk, cutting his hand would be redundant because he already lacks the benefit of walking. If he can still stand and walk despite missing toes, the hand is cut.
The foot's functionality affects the hand ruling through the same principle of tafwīt al-manfaʿah.
5
Note the siyasa and repentance discussion
The passage also mentions that the imam may execute the thief as siyasa (policy), and that his repentance is accepted when signs of righteousness appear. These are supplementary rulings beyond the main hadd.
This is a separate discussion not essential to the core ruling but useful for comprehensive understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حبس حتى يتوب ولم يقطع كمن سرق وإبهامه اليسرى مقطوعة أو شلاء أو أصبعان منها سواها أو رجله اليمني مقطوعة. طعن فيه الطحاوي أو نحمله على السياسة وتمامه في الأصول من بحث الأمر. وفي الفتاوي السراجية : للإمام أن يقتله سياسة ؛ كذا في شرح مسكين. ولم يذكر المصنف ضربه مع الحبس وأثبته في المجتبى ولم يذكروا متى تقبل توبته وتظهر. وفي غاية البيان معزياً إلى النافع أنه يحبس حتى يتوب أو تظهر عليه سيما رجل صالح. قوله : (كمن سرق وإبهامه اليسرى مقطوعة أو شلاء أو أصبعان منها سواها أو رجله اليمني مقطوعة) يعني لا يقطع في هذه المسائل لما فيه من تفويت جنس المنفعة بطشاً أو مشياً، وكذا إذا كانت رجله اليمنى شلاء لما قلنا وقوام البطش بالإبهام قيد بالإبهام لأنه لو كان المقطوع أصبعاً غير الإبهام أو أشل فإنه يقطع لأنه فوتها لا يوجب خللاً في البطش ظاهراً. وقيد باليد اليسرى لأنه لو كانت يده اليمنى شلاء أو ناقصة الأصابع تقطع في ظاهر الرواية لأن المستحق بالنص قطع اليمنى واستيفاء الناقص عند تعذر الكامل جائز. وقيد بقطع الرجل اليمنى لأنه لو كانت رجله اليمنى مقطوعة الأصابع فإن كان يستطيع القيام والمشي عليها قطعت يده، وإن كان لا يستطيع القيام والمشي لم تقطع يده كذا في غاية البيان.
He is imprisoned until he repents and is not cut, like one who stole while his left thumb is cut or paralyzed, or two fingers of it other than it, or his right foot is cut. Al-Tahawi objected to it [?] or we carry it on siyasa, and its completion is in usul from the discussion of command. In al-Fatawa al-Sirajiyya: The imam may kill him as siyasa; likewise in Sharh Miskin. The author did not mention striking him with imprisonment, and it is affirmed in al-Mujtaba, and they did not mention when his repentance is accepted and becomes apparent. In Ghayat al-Bayan attributed to al-Nafi‘: He is imprisoned until he repents or there appears on him the sign of a righteous man. He said: (like one who stole while his left thumb is cut or paralyzed, or two fingers of it other than it, or his right foot is cut) means: he is not cut in these cases because of what entails loss of the type of benefit, either gripping or walking. Likewise if his right foot is paralyzed for what we said, and the essence of gripping is with the thumb. He restricted it to the thumb because if the cut finger is other than the thumb or paralyzed, he is cut because its loss does not cause an apparent defect in gripping. And he restricted it to the left hand because if his right hand is paralyzed or has deficient fingers, it is cut in the apparent narration because the prescribed right hand is what is due by text, and executing the deficient when the perfect is impossible is permissible. And he restricted it to the cutting of the right foot because if his right foot has its toes cut, if he can stand and walk on it, his hand is cut; if he cannot stand and walk, his hand is not cut. Likewise in Ghayat al-Bayan.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is a sharh (commentary) on a matn from Kanz al-Daqa'iq presenting a ruling about a thief who already has a pre-existing amputation or paralysis. The ruling states that such a thief is not subject to the hadd punishment of hand-cutting; instead, he is imprisoned until repentance. The commentary then explains the legal reasoning (ta'lil): the hadd cutting is intended to remove the criminal's ability to commit theft through the limb. If the limb is already non-functional (e.g., left thumb missing or paralyzed, two fingers of left hand missing, right foot missing or paralyzed), then cutting it would not remove any remaining benefit. The key principle is 'tafwit jins al-manfa'ah' (loss of the type of benefit). The commentator specifies that only the left thumb is considered because the right hand is the one to be cut; loss of a finger other than the thumb does not significantly impair gripping. Similarly, if the right foot is missing and the thief cannot walk, cutting his hand would be redundant. However, if only the toes of the right foot are missing but he can still stand and walk, the hand is cut. This shows the careful linking of the punishment's purpose to the functional utility of the limb. The passage also includes a side discussion about siyasa (execution as policy) and the conditions for accepting repentance, citing various sources.

Key Terms
تفويت جنس المنفعة (tafwīt jins al-manfaʿah): The loss of the entire category of benefit provided by a limb (e.g., gripping for hand, walking for foot).
شلاء (shalāʾ): Paralyzed, useless, without function.
البطش (al-baṭsh): Gripping, grasping; the primary function of the hand.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A thief has his left thumb paralyzed from birth. According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, what is the consequence?
A) His right hand is cut.
B) He is imprisoned until repentance and his hand is not cut.
C) His left foot is cut instead.
D) He is executed as siyasa.

Key Takeaway
A thief with a pre-existing non-functional left thumb, two left fingers, or right foot is not subject to hand-cutting because the hadd aims to remove functional benefit, not a useless limb.

Liability for Cutting the Wrong Hand: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on executioner's liability for wrong limb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on the executioner's liability and explain the basis for each.

Core Idea
An executioner who cuts the left hand by order is not liable according to Abu Hanifa, but liable according to the Sahibayn and Zufar—depending on intent and ijtihad.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute: when a judge orders the executioner to cut the thief's right hand, and the executioner cuts the left instead, does he owe financial compensation? The answer splits among the three great Hanafi imams—Abu Hanifa, the two Sahibayn, and Zufar—each with distinct reasoning rooted in principles of ijtihad, the nature of the limb, and the executioner's obedience. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to trace how a single legal question yields multiple valid positions within the madhhab.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core scenario and the ruling of Abu Hanifa
The judge orders the executioner to cut the thief's right hand. The executioner intentionally cuts the left. Abu Hanifa holds: no financial liability (daman) at all. Why? Because the executioner acted under judicial order and his error is in ijtihad (legal reasoning), which is excused. Also, the left hand is of the same 'kind' as the right, so it is not a true destruction.
Note the phrase 'أَتْلَفَ وَأَخْلَفَ مِنْ جِنْسِهِ مَا مِنْهُ' (destroyed and replaced from its kind) – a key argument.
2
Contrast the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say: if the error was accidental (khata'), no liability; but if intentional (amdan), full liability (daman). Their reasoning: cutting a protected limb (ṭaraf ma‘ṣūm) without right or valid interpretation (ta’wīl) deserves compensation.
They distinguish based on intent, whereas Abu Hanifa does not.
3
Note Zufar's stricter position
Zufar holds liability even in accidental error. He considers the error to be in action, not in ijtihad, and the limb is absolutely protected. Thus, any cutting requires compensation.
Zufar's view is that the executioner's obedience does not excuse a mistaken action.
4
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The practical difference: If the executioner acts intentionally, Abu Hanifa says no payment; the Sahibayn say payment; Zufar says payment even if mistaken.
This affects whether the executioner must compensate the thief or the state.
5
Analyze the supporting proofs
Abu Hanifa uses analogy (qiyas) with a person who testifies about a sale and then retracts: no liability because the testimony is akin to ijtihad. He also argues that the left hand is not a separate destruction. The Sahibayn and Zufar rely on the general principle that injury to a protected limb entails liability.
The dispute is rooted in whether the executioner's act is considered ijtihad or a mere action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الكافي : وإذا حبس السارق ليسأل عن الشهود فقطع رجل يده اليمنى عمداً فعليه القصاص وقد بطل الحد عن السارق، وكذلك إن كان قطع يده اليسرى، وإن حكم عليه بالقطع في السرقة فقطع رجل يده اليمنى من غير أن يؤمر بذلك فلا شيء عليه اهـ قوله : (ولا يضمن بقطع اليسرى من أمر بخلافه) أي إذا قال الحاكم للجلاد اقطع يمين هذا في سرقة سرقها فقطع يساره عمداً فلا شيء عليه عند أبي حنيفة. وقالا : لا شيء عليه في الخطأ ويضمن في العمد وقال زفر يضمن في الخطأ أيضاً الخطأ القياس والمراد هو وهو في الاجتهاد، وأما الخطأ هو خير رفة اليمين واليسار لا يجعل عفواً وقيل : يجعل عذراً أيضاً. له أنه قطع يداه في معرا معصومة والخطأ في حق العباد غير مضمون فيضمنها . قلنا إنه أخطأ في اجتهاده إذ ليس في النص تعيين اليمين والخطأ في الاجتهاد موضوع ولهما أنه قطع طرفاً معصوماً بغير حق ولا تأويل. له لأنه تعمد الظلم فلا يعفى وإن كان في المجتهدات، وكان ينبغي أن يجب القصاص إلا أنه امتنع القصاص للشبهة . ولأبي حنيفة أنه أتلف وأخلف من جنسه ما منه فلا يعد إتلافاً كمن شهد على غيره ببيع ماله بمثل قيمته ثم رجع، وعلى هذا لو قطعه غير الجلاد لا أيضاً هو الصحيح. قيد بالأمر لأنه لو قطعه أحد قبل الأمر والقضاء وجب يضمن القصاص في العمد والدية في الخطأ اتفاقاً وسقط القطع عن السارق لأن مقطوع اليد لا يجب عليه القطع حداً وقضاء القاضي بالحد كالأمر على الصحيح فلا يرد على المصنف. وقيد بقوله اتفاقاً بخلافه لأن الحاكم لو أطلق وقال اقطع يده ولم يعين اليمني فلا ضمان على القاطع لعدم المخالفة إذ اليد تطلق عليهما، وكذلك لو أخرج السارق يده فقال هذه لأنه قطعه بأمره. وقيد بعدم الضمان لأنه يعزر إذا كان عمداً كما في فتح القدير. ولم يذكر المصنف أن هذا القطع وقع حداً أو لا قالوا فعلى طريقة أنه وقع حداً فلا ضمان على السارق لو كان استهلك العين لأن القطع والضمان لا يجتمعان وعلى طريقة عدم وقوعه حداً فهو ضامن في العمد والخطأ.
And in al-Kafi: If the thief is imprisoned to ask about witnesses, and a man cuts his right hand intentionally, then qisas is due and the hadd is void for the thief; similarly if he cut his left hand. And if he was sentenced to cutting for theft, and a man cuts his right hand without being ordered, there is no liability. End quote. He said: (And there is no liability for cutting the left when ordered contrary) meaning if the judge says to the executioner: Cut the right of this one for a theft he committed, and he cuts his left intentionally, then there is no liability according to Abu Hanifa. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say: no liability in error, and liability in intentional. And Zufar says: liability even in error; error is qiyas, and the intended is that he is in ijtihad. And as for error in the right and left, it does not make it excused; and it is said it makes an excuse also. He has the argument that he cut the hand in a protected state and error regarding the rights of people is not guaranteed, so he is liable. We said: he erred in his ijtihad because there is no specification of the right in the text, and error in ijtihad is removed. And the two have that he cut a protected limb without right and without interpretation. He has the argument that he intentionally committed injustice, so it is not excused even if it is among ijtihad matters. And it would be necessary for qisas to be due, but it is prevented due to doubt. And according to Abu Hanifa, he destroyed and replaced from its kind what comes from it, so it is not considered destruction, like one who testifies against another that he sold his property for its value and then retracts. And upon this, if a non-executioner cut him, it is also the correct view. It is restricted by the order because if someone cuts him before the order and judgment, he is liable for qisas in intentional and diya in error by agreement, and the cutting drops from the thief because the one with a cut hand is not subject to hadd cutting. And the judge's ruling on hadd is like the order, so there is no objection to the author. And it is restricted by his saying 'by agreement' because if the judge says generally: Cut his hand, and does not specify the right, there is no liability on the cutter because there is no violation, since 'hand' applies to both. Likewise if the thief presents his hand and says 'this', because he cuts it by his order. And it is restricted by no liability because he is punished with tazir if intentional, as in Fath al-Qadir. The author did not mention whether this cutting occurred as hadd or not. They said: On the basis that it occurred as hadd, there is no liability on the thief if he had consumed the stolen property, because cutting and guarantee do not combine. And on the basis that it did not occur as hadd, he is liable in intentional and error.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage addresses two interconnected issues. First, from al-Kafi, a scenario where a thief imprisoned for questioning has his hand cut by someone else—this voids the hadd and requires qisas if intentional. Second, the commentary on the matn 'ولا يضمن بقطع اليسرى من أمر بخلافه' which states that if a judge orders the executioner to cut the right hand and he mistakenly or intentionally cuts the left, the ruling differs. The intra-Hanafi dispute is presented: Imam Abu Hanifa holds that no financial liability (daman) applies because the executioner acted under judicial order and his error is in ijtihad, which is excused; additionally, the left hand is of the same type as the right, so it is not considered a destruction (إتلاف) as he 'replaced from its kind'. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad) distinguish: no liability if the error was accidental (khata'), but full liability if intentional (amdan). Imam Zufar holds liability even in accidental error, based on qiyas (analogy) that any injury to a protected limb requires compensation. The passage then provides detailed reasoning for each position, including the principle that error in ijtihad is excused, and that the executioner's obedience creates a valid exculpation. It also discusses edge cases: if the judge did not specify which hand, or if the thief himself presents his left hand, the executioner is not liable. Finally, it notes a subsidiary issue: whether the cutting counts as a valid hadd or not, which affects the thief's liability for the stolen property—if it counts as hadd, the thief is not liable for the property; if not, he is liable.

Key Terms
الاجتهاد (al-ijtihād): Legal reasoning by a qualified scholar; error in ijtihad is excused if the matter is not explicitly determined by text.
الضمان (aḍ-ḍamān): Financial liability, compensation for damages.
القصاص (al-qiṣāṣ): Retributive punishment (eye for eye) for intentional injury, unless waived or mitigated by doubt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A judge orders the executioner to cut the thief's right hand, but the executioner mistakenly cuts the left hand. According to Abu Hanifa, what is the ruling?
A) The executioner must pay diya for the left hand.
B) The executioner is not liable for damages.
C) The executioner must be punished with tazir but no financial liability.
D) The thief's right hand must still be cut later.

Key Takeaway
In an intra-Hanafi dispute, Abu Hanifa's ruling that an executioner who cuts the wrong hand under judicial order is not financially liable prevails as the operative position, based on excusing error in ijtihad.

Victim's Demand as Condition for Hand-Cutting
Ruling that the victim's demand is a condition for applying the hadd of cutting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for applying the hadd of cutting and identify the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the victim's demand.

Core Idea
The victim's demand is a condition for hand-cutting; without it, the hadd is suspended even after confession.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra’iq, the author clarifies a crucial procedural safeguard in hudud: the victim must personally demand his property before the hand of the thief is cut. This condition applies even when the thief has confessed. We will explore the reasoning behind this rule and the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad versus Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its condition
Read the first sentence: 'And the demand of the victim is a condition for cutting.' Recognize that this is a procedural requirement.
The condition applies whether the thief confesses or evidence is established.
2
Understand the rationale (talil)
The passage explains: because litigation is a condition for manifestation, and there is a possibility the thief acknowledges ownership, dropping the hadd.
This removes uncertainty (shubha) before applying punishment.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Yusuf holds that the victim's demand is not a condition in confession; the hand is cut immediately by istihsan. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad require the victim's presence.
The commentator corrects the attribution of al-Badā'i‘.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, as the commentator favors them and rejects Abu Yusuf's isolated narration.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وطلب المسروق منه شرط القطع) أي وطلبه المال فلا قطع بدونه لأن الخصومة شرط لظهورها. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا أقر أو أقيمت عليه البينة لاحتمال أن يقر له بالملك فيسقط القطع فلا بد من حضوره عند الأداء والقطع لتنتفي تلك الشبهة. وبما ذكرناه ظهر أن ما في التبيين معزياً إلى البدائع من أنه إذا أقر أنه سرق من فلان الغائب قطع استحساناً ولا ينتظر حضور الغائب وتصديقه فإنما هو رواية عن أبي يوسف وليست هذه عبارة البدائع فإن عبارته قال أبو حنيفة ومحمد الدعوى في الإقرار شرط حتى لو أقر السارق أنه سرق مال فلان الغائب لم يقطع ما لم يحضر المسروق منه ويخاصم عندهما . وقال أبو يوسف : الدعوى في الإقرار ليست بشرط إلى آخره. وفي البدائع أيضاً قال محمد: لو قال سرقت هذه الدراهم للقطع عندنا، وأما ما سواهما فلتفويت المنفعة إما بطشاً أو مشياً كما ذكر هنا.
He said: (And the demand of the victim is a condition for cutting) meaning his demand for the property, so there is no cutting without it because litigation is a condition for its manifestation. He stated it absolutely, so it includes whether he confessed or evidence was established, because it is possible that he acknowledges ownership, so cutting is dropped. Therefore his presence at the time of payment and cutting is necessary to remove that uncertainty. And by what we mentioned, it is clear that what is in al-Tabyīn attributed to al-Badā'i‘ that if he confesses that he stole from an absent person, the hand is cut as istihsān and the presence of the absent person and his confirmation is not waited for, is only a narration from Abu Yusuf and is not the statement of al-Badā'i‘, for its statement says: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say that the claim (da‘wā) is a condition in confession, so if the thief confesses that he stole the property of an absent person, he is not cut until the victim appears and litigates according to them. And Abu Yusuf says: the claim is not a condition, etc. And in al-Badā'i‘ also, Muhammad said: If he says 'I stole these dirhams', then for cutting according to us, and as for other than them, then it is due to loss of benefit, either gripping or walking, as mentioned here.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage discusses a procedural condition for the hadd of hand-cutting: the victim of theft must personally demand his property before the judge. The reason is that if the thief confesses, he might later claim ownership, which would void the hadd due to doubt. So the victim's presence ensures the claim is genuine. The commentary then addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad hold that even if the thief confesses to stealing from an absent person, the hand is not cut until the absent victim appears and litigates. Imam Abu Yusuf, however, holds that the demand is not a condition and the hand is cut immediately based on istihsān (juristic preference). The commentator criticizes the attribution of Abu Yusuf's view to al-Badā'i‘, stating it is merely a narration from him. The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. The passage also reiterates the earlier principle that if the thief confesses to stealing a specific type of property, the hand is cut only if it involves gripping or walking benefits, as previously discussed.

Key Terms
الخصومة (al-khuṣūmah): Litigation, the formal claim between the victim and the thief before the judge.
الاستحسان (al-istiḥsān): Juristic preference; a method used by Abu Yusuf to give a ruling that avoids strict analogy for a better outcome.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The thief's hand is cut immediately.
B) The ruling is postponed until the victim appears and demands his property.
C) The thief is imprisoned until the victim returns.
D) The thief is only required to return the property.

Key Takeaway
The victim's demand is a necessary condition for hand-cutting, even after confession, to eliminate the doubt of ownership.

Conditions for Cutting in Theft: The Victim and the Owner's Demand
States that the victim's status and the owner's demand are conditions for the cutting penalty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify who qualifies as a valid victim (المسروق منه) for the cutting penalty and state the requirement of the owner's demand (الخصومة), and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the party to riba.

Core Idea
The cutting penalty requires a victim with a valid hand, not necessarily the owner; the owner's demand is essential, including even the giver of riba.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies that the cutting penalty in theft depends on two conditions: the existence of a valid victim and the owner's demand. Understanding who qualifies as a victim is crucial because the Hanafi school includes not only the owner but also depositees, usurpers, and parties to riba—anyone with a valid hand over the property. Moreover, the penalty is applied only if the victim (or the owner) demands it. A key internal dispute arises over whether the giver of riba retains litigation rights, which has practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
From the passage, the two conditions for cutting are: (1) the stolen property must be taken from a valid victim (المسروق منه), and (2) the cutting is executed only with the demand of the owner (بطلب المالك).
The second condition is also known as الخصومة (litigation).
2
List the categories of valid victims
The passage explicitly includes the owner, the depositee (مودع), the usurper (غاصب), and the party to riba (صاحب الربا). The common factor is having a valid hand (يد صحيحة).
Also mentioned: borrower, mudarib, seller, mortgagee, etc. They all have a right to litigate.
3
Understand the rationale for non-owners
The principle is that anyone with a valid hand can act as a victim because the cutting penalty is tied to the protection of property in one's custody. A deposit is a trust hand; a usurper's hand is valid for claiming restitution.
This is broader than Shafi'i, which requires ownership.
4
Analyze the riba dispute
Some Hanafi scholars (e.g., al-Shumnuni) argue that the giver of riba (المعطي) loses ownership and hand upon delivery, so he has no litigation right. The author counters that the riba contract is void, so the giver retains ownership of the original object as long as it exists; thus he can demand restitution and cutting.
The two views lead to different outcomes for theft from the giver.
5
Apply to the finder of a lost item
The passage discusses whether a finder (ملتقط) can be a victim. The author argues that the finder has a valid trust hand, so he can litigate if the found item is stolen from him; however, some earlier opinions suggest otherwise.
This illustrates how the concept of 'valid hand' is applied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسروق منه شرط القطع ولو مودعاً أو غاصباً أو صاحب الربا ويقطع بطلب المالك لو سرق منهم أي ولو كان المسروق منه والأصل فيه أن كل من كان له يد صحيحة يملك لا فلا فللمالك أن يخاصم السارق إذا سرق منه، وكذا المودع - بفتح الدال - اهـ. فقد صرح الخصومة ومن والمستعير والمضارب والمبضع والغاصب والقابض على سوم الشراء والمرتهن ومتولي المسجد والأب والوصي، فتعتبر خصومتهم في ثبوت ولاية الاسترداد وفي حق القطع. وأراد بصاحب الربا أن يبيع عشرة بعشرين وقبض العشرين فسرق منه العشرون فيقطع السارق بخصومته عندنا لأن هذا المال في يده بمنزلة المغصوب إذ الشراء فاسد بمنزلته وأما العاقد الآخر من عاقدي الربا فإنه بالتسليم لم يبق له ملك ولا يد فلا يكون له ولاية الخصومة؛ ذكره الشمني. وفي فتاوى قاضيخان من اللقطة : رجل التقط لقطة فضاعت منه فوجدها في يد غيره فلا خصومة بينه وبين ذلك الرجل بخلاف الوديعة فإن في الوديعة يكون للمودع أن يأخذها من الثاني لأن في اللقطة الثاني كالأول في ولاية أخذ اللقطة وليس الثاني كالأول في ولاية إثبات اليد على الوديعة .هـ فينبغي أن لا يقطع بطلب الملتقط كما لا يخفى قوله : (ويقطع بطلب المالك لو سرق منهم أي من هؤلاء الثلاثة لأن الخصومة إنما شرطت ليعلم عدم القطع والضمان لا يجتمعان قوله فينبغي أن لا يقطع بطلب الملتقط) فيه نظر لأن الملتقط الأول للثاني إنما هو لزوال يد الأول بإثبات يد مثل يده كما أشار إليه قول الخانية أن الثاني كالأول في ولاية أخذ اللقطة، ولا يخفى أن هذا لا يدل على أنه لا بد للأول قبل ضياعها منه إذ لا شك أن يده يد أمانة حتى لا يتمكن أحد أخذها منه ، ولو وصف أحد علامتها ولم يصدقه الملتقط لا يجبر على دفعها إليه، ولو دفعها إلى أحد له أن يستردها منه فهذا يدل على أن له يداً صحيحة فله مخاصمة من سرقها منه قوله : أي) من هؤلاء الثلاثة هذا مخالف لما قدمه عن الشمني اتفاقاً لا خصومة لمعطي الربا. ثم رأيت في النهر ما نصه : واعلم أن ظاهر كلامه ـ أي المصنف ـ يفيد أنه من من من أنه
The victim is a condition for cutting, even if he is a depositee, a usurper, or a party to riba. And cutting is done with the demand of the owner if stolen from them. Meaning: even if the victim is the one from whom it was stolen, the principle is that anyone who has a valid hand of ownership owns... so the owner may litigate the thief if stolen from him, and likewise the depositee (with fatha on dal) ... Thus, the litigation is affirmed from... and the borrower, the mudarib, the seller, the usurper, the one who takes on the basis of purchase, the mortgagee, the trustee of the mosque, the father, and the executor. Their litigation is considered for establishing the right of recovery and for the right of cutting. He intended by 'party to riba' that he sells ten for twenty and takes the twenty, then the twenty are stolen from him; then the thief is cut by his litigation in our school because this wealth in his hand is like that of a usurper, since the purchase is void and similar to it. As for the other party to the riba transaction, upon delivery, he has neither ownership nor hand, so he has no right of litigation; al-Shumnuni mentioned it. And in the Fatawa of Qadikhan from the chapter of found property: a man found a lost item, then it was lost from him, then he found it in the hand of another; there is no litigation between him and that man, unlike the case of a deposit, because in a deposit, the depositor may take it from the second, since in the found item, the second is like the first in the right of taking the found item, but the second is not like the first in the right of establishing hand over the deposit. Therefore, cutting should not be done with the demand of the finder, as is not hidden. His saying: 'And cutting is done with the demand of the owner if stolen from them,' i.e., from these three, because litigation is only required to know the absence of the combination of cutting and liability. His saying: 'Therefore, cutting should not be done with the demand of the finder' is questionable because the first finder [litigating against] the second is only due to the removal of the first's hand by the establishment of a hand like his own, as indicated by the statement of al-Khaniyah that the second is like the first in the right of taking the found item. It is not hidden that this does not indicate that it is not necessary for the first before its loss from him, since there is no doubt that his hand is a trust hand, so no one can take it from him. And if someone describes its mark and the finder does not believe him, he is not forced to give it to him. And if he gives it to someone, he has the right to reclaim it from him. This indicates that he has a valid hand, so he has the right to litigate against one who steals it from him. His saying: 'i.e., from these three' contradicts what he previously mentioned from al-Shumnuni that there is no litigation for the giver of riba. Then I saw in al-Nahr its text: Know that the apparent meaning of his words—i.e., the author—indicates that he from from from that
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage expounds the conditions for the cutting penalty in theft. The first condition is that the stolen property must be taken from a valid victim, meaning someone who has a recognized hand (يد صحيحة) over the property. The text clarifies that this includes not only the owner but also depositees (مودع), usurpers (غاصب), and even a party to a riba transaction (صاحب الربا) – because they have a valid hand, even if their ownership is defective. This is because the cutting penalty is a right of Allah, but it depends on the existence of a valid claim from the victim, which requires the victim to have a valid hand. The text then addresses the requirement of the owner's demand: the cutting is only carried out if the owner demands it. There is a discussion about whether the demand must be for the property or for the penalty itself. The preferred view is that the demand for the property is sufficient, as the penalty is a right of Allah and does not require the victim's request for it specifically. The text also discusses the case of a finder of a lost item (ملتقط): if the lost item is stolen from him, he has a valid claim to litigate because his hand is a trust hand (يد أمانة). However, there is an opinion that a finder has a different status. The passage then delves into a dispute regarding the party to riba: according to some, the recipient of the riba (المعطي) loses all rights, but the author argues that both parties should have litigation rights because the property in the recipient's hand is like a usurped property, and the one who gave it still retains some ownership. The commentary also quotes from earlier authorities such as al-Shumnuni and al-Nahr to establish the Hanafi position.

Key Terms
المسروق منه (al-masruq minhu): The person from whom property is stolen, i.e., the victim who has a valid hand over the property.
الخصومة (al-khusumah): The right to bring a legal claim or litigation against the thief.
صاحب الربا (sahib al-riba): The party involved in a riba transaction, specifically the one who gives riba (المعطي) or the one who receives it (الآخذ).
يد صحيحة (yad sahiha): A valid hand or custody over property recognized by Sharia, whether ownership-based or trust-based.
ملتقط (multaqit): A finder of a lost item (لقطة).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following can be considered a valid victim (المسروق منه) for the cutting penalty?
A) Only the actual owner of the property
B) The owner and the depositee, but not a usurper
C) The owner, depositee, usurper, and the recipient of riba
D) Only the owner and the recipient of riba

Key Takeaway
The cutting penalty in theft requires a victim with a valid hand, which includes depositees, usurpers, and parties to riba; the owner's demand is necessary, but the definition of victim is broader than ownership.

Stealing from a Thief After the Hand is Cut
Fiqh ruling with proof on cutting a second thief

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the conditions under which a second thief is subject to the hadd penalty when stealing from a first thief whose hand has been cut, and apply the Hanafi differentiation based on the timing of the cutting.

Core Idea
After the cutting penalty, property loses its legal value (taqawwum) — a subsequent theft does not trigger cutting.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explores a nuanced scenario: if a thief's hand has been cut for theft, can someone else be cut for stealing the same property from that thief? The ruling depends on whether the property retains its 'taqawwum' (legal value) in the hands of the first thief. The Hanafi school distinguishes between before and after the cutting, and also addresses the role of the judge in returning the property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine the specific scenario: stealing from a thief after his hand has been cut. State the ruling: the second thief is not subject to the cutting penalty.
The act is taking property that is in the possession of a thief who has already been penalized.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The proof is the legal principle that property loses its value (taqawwum) in the hands of the first thief after the cutting, as he is no longer liable for compensation. This is stated directly in the text.
The text says: 'لأن المال غير متقوم في حق السارق حتى لا يجب عليه الضمان بالهلاك' (because the property is not valuable in respect to the thief, so he is not liable for compensation if it perishes).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the loss of taqawwum prevents cutting. One condition for the hadd of theft is that the stolen property be mutaqawwim (legally valuable). Since after cutting it is not, the second theft does not meet the threshold for cutting.
The condition of mutaqawwim is essential; without it, the hadd cannot be applied.
4
Note the ikhtilaf (dispute)
Identify the opposing view: al-Karkhi and al-Tahawi hold that no cutting applies at all when stealing from a thief, regardless of timing. Their proof is that the first thief's hand is like lost property (ضائع).
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute, resolved by the detailed view from al-Hidayah.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The author prefers the detailed view from al-Hidayah: if the second theft occurs after cutting, no cutting; if before or after a doubt, cutting applies. This is chosen in Fath al-Qadir and is the mufta bihi.
The tarjih is based on the soundness of the analogy to a usurper before cutting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا بطلب المالك أو السارق لو سرق من سارق بعد القطع
قوله : (لا بطلب المالك أو السارق لو سرق من سارق بعد القطع) يعني لو قطع سارق بسرقة فسرقت منه لم يكن له ولا لمالك العين المسروقة أن يقطع السارق الثاني لأن المال غير متقوم في حق السارق حتى لا يجب عليه الضمان بالهلاك فلم تنعقد موجبة في نفسها وللأول ولاية الخصومة في الاسترداد لحاجته إذ الرد واجب عليه قيد بقوله «بعد القطع» لأنه لو سرق الثاني قبل أن يقطع الأول أو بعد ما درئ القطع بشبهة يقطع بخصومة الأول لأن سقوط التقوم ضرورة القطع ولم يوجد فصار كالغاصب ؛ كذا في الهداية. وأطلق الكرخي والطحاوي عدم قطع السارق من السارق لأن يده ليست يد أمانة ولا ملك فكان ضائعاً ولا قطع في أخذ مال ضائع. قلنا : بقي أن يكون يد غصب والسارق منه يقطع فالحق ما في الهداية من التفصيل واختاره في فتح القدير في مسألة ولاية الاسترداد أن الوجه أنه إذا ظهر هذا الحال للقاضي لا يرده إلى الأول ولا إلى الثاني إذا رده لظهور خيانة كل منهما، بل يرده من يد الثاني إلى المالك إن كان حاضراً وإلا حفظه كما يحفظ أموال الغيب
Not with the demand of the owner or the thief if stolen from a thief after cutting. His saying: (Not with the demand of the owner or the thief if stolen from a thief after cutting) means: if a thief was cut for a theft, and then it was stolen from him, neither he nor the owner of the original stolen article may have the second thief cut, because the property is not value-able (mutqawwim) in respect to the thief, so that he is not liable for compensation if it perishes, so no obligation arises in itself. The first thief has the right of litigation for recovery due to his need, since returning (the property) is obligatory upon him. He restricted it with his saying 'after cutting', because if the second steals before the first is cut or after the cutting is averted due to a doubt, the first's litigation brings cutting, because the fall of value is due to necessity of cutting and did not occur, so he becomes like a usurper; thus in al-Hidayah. Al-Karkhi and al-Tahawi made absolute the non-cutting of the thief from a thief, because his hand is neither a trust hand nor ownership, so it is lost property, and there is no cutting for taking lost property. We say: it remains that it is a hand of usurpation, and the thief from whom it is stolen is cut. So the truth is the detail in al-Hidayah, and it is chosen in Fath al-Qadir in the issue of the right of recovery that the proper course is that when this situation appears to the judge, he should not return it to the first nor to the second if he returns it, due to the manifestation of treachery from each, but rather he returns it from the hand of the second to the owner if present, otherwise he preserves it as the property of absent persons is preserved.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific scenario: if a thief's hand has been cut for stealing, and then someone else steals the stolen property from that thief, is the second thief cut? The ruling is that neither the original owner nor the first thief can have the second thief cut, because the property in the first thief's hand loses its legal value (taqawwum) after the cutting penalty is executed. Since the first thief is not liable for compensation if the property is destroyed, the property is effectively worthless in his hands, so stealing it does not meet the nisab threshold for cutting. However, if the second theft occurs before the first thief is cut (or if the cutting was averted due to a doubt), then the first thief still has a valid hand (like a usurper), so the second thief is cut. The author prefers the detailed view from al-Hidayah over the absolute view of al-Karkhi and al-Tahawi. Additionally, the judge should not return the property to either thief but to the original owner or preserve it.

Key Terms
غير متقوم (ghayr mutaqawwim): Property that is not legally considered valuable for the purposes of liability or cutting, because the holder is not liable for its destruction.
يد غصب (yad ghasb): A hand of usurpation; a possessor who had obtained property wrongfully but is still liable for compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a first thief's hand is cut, and then a second thief steals the same property from him, what is the ruling?
A) The second thief is definitely cut.
B) The second thief is not cut because the property is no longer valuable in the first thief's hand.
C) The second thief is cut only if the original owner demands it.
D) The first thief is cut again for losing the property.

Key Takeaway
After a thief's hand is cut, the stolen property loses its legal value, so a subsequent theft of that property does not trigger the cutting penalty; however, before the cutting, the property retains its value and the second thief is cut.

Four Cases Where the Hadd of Cutting Is Not Applied
Lists and explains four exemptions from the cutting penalty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four cases where the cutting penalty for theft is not applied and explain why each case cancels the hadd.

Core Idea
Cutting is not applied if the theft dispute is resolved or the conditions for cutting are no longer met.

In this lesson, we explore four specific scenarios in which a thief escapes the cutting penalty despite having taken property. These cases highlight that the hadd is not automatic—it depends on the continued existence of certain conditions. Understanding these exceptions is crucial for applying the penalty correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four cases
From the text, list the four scenarios where cutting is not applied: (1) returning property before litigation, (2) acquiring ownership after judgment, (3) claiming the property is his own, (4) value drops below nisab.
The fourth case is about the nisab threshold.
2
Determine the missing condition for each case
For each case, identify which condition for cutting is absent. For case 1, litigation is missing. For case 4, the nisab is missing. For cases 2 and 3, the ownership element is disputed.
Litigation (khusumah) is a key condition.
3
Recognize the role of litigation
The text explains that litigation is a condition for cutting because evidence is needed only to resolve a dispute. If the property is returned before litigation, no dispute exists, so cutting is dropped.
Return after litigation still triggers cutting.
4
Apply the rule to a new scenario
Consider: if the thief returns the property after the victim has approached the judge, cutting is still applied. The dispute has already been initiated.
Timing is critical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ومن سرق شيئاً ورده قبل الخصومة إلى مالكه أو ملكه بعد القضاء أو ادعى أنه ملكه أو نقصت قيمته عن النصاب لم يقطع) بيان لأربع مسائل لا قطع فيها: الأولى لو سرق شيئاً ورده قبل الخصومة إلى مالكه فلا قطع لأن الخصومة شرط لظهور السرقة لأن البينة إنما جعلت حجة ضرورة قطع المنازعة وقد انقطعت الخصومة، وقيد بالرد بما قبل الخصومة أي قبل المرافعة إلى القاضي لأنه لو رده بعد المرافعة إلى القاضي قطع لانتهاء الخصومة لحصول يقطع بخصومة معطي الربا دون صاحب الربا لأن المال في يده بمنزلة المغصوب كما مر . قال في الفتح : : للمغصوب منه الخصومة إلا أن المسطور في السراج أنه لا يقطع بخصومة صاحب الربا لأنه لا ملك له فيه ولا بد وتبعه الشمني ولم أر من نبه عليه فتدبره .اهـ. أقول : قد صرح في الأشباه عن القنية أن الربا لا يملك فيجب عليه رد عينه ما دام قائماً حتى لو أبرأه صاحبه لا يبرأ منه لأن رد عينه القائمة حق الشرع، وعلى هذا فلصاحبه ملك قائم فيه وللآخر يد لأنه إذا قبضه برضا صاحبه ضار كالمودع لا كالغاصب فينبغي أن تثبت الخصومة لكل منهما وهو المفهوم من المتن حيث قال : ولو مودعاً أو غاصباً أو صاحب ربا . فإن التعبير بـ«لو» يدل على أن المالك كذلك بالأولى. وصرح به الماتن بعده بقوله : ويقطع بطلب المالك لو سرق منهم. فهذا يعارض قول السراج والشمني فتدبر . قوله: (وللأول ولاية الخصومة في (الاسترداد هذه إحدى الروايتين والرواية ليس له وسيأتي بحث
His saying: (And whoever steals something and returns it before litigation to its owner, or owns it after judgment, or claims that it is his property, or its value decreases below the nisab, is not cut.) An explanation of four cases in which there is no cutting: First: if he steals something and returns it before litigation to its owner, then no cutting, because litigation is a condition for the prosecution of theft, since evidence is made a proof only due to necessity of ending the dispute, and the dispute has been cut off. It is restricted with return before litigation, i.e., before referral to the judge, because if he returns it after referral to the judge, he is cut, because the dispute is ended. There is cutting with the litigation of the giver of riba but not with the litigation of the recipient of riba, because the property in his hand is like that of a usurper as previously mentioned. He said in al-Fath: The victim of usurpation has the right of litigation, except that what is written in al-Siraj is that cutting is not done with the litigation of the recipient of riba because he has no ownership in it and it is necessary, and al-Shumnuni followed him, and I have not seen anyone who warned about it, so think about it. I say: It is explained in al-Ashbah from al-Qunyah that riba cannot be owned, so he must return its essence as long as it remains, even if the owner absolves him, he is not absolved because returning its existing essence is a right of the Sharia. On this basis, the owner has existing ownership in it, and the other has a hand, because when he takes it with the owner's consent, he is like a depositee, not like a usurper. So litigation should be established for each of them, and this is the understanding from the text where he said: even if a depositee, a usurper, or a party to riba. The expression 'even if' indicates that the owner is also included by priority. And the author explicitly said after it: And cutting is done with the demand of the owner if stolen from them. This contradicts the statement of al-Siraj and al-Shumnuni, so reflect. His saying: (And the first has the right of litigation in recovery) is one of two narrations; the other narration is that he does not have it, and the discussion will come later.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage lays out four cases where the cutting penalty for theft is not applied despite the act of taking property. The four cases are: (1) If the thief returns the stolen property to the owner before any litigation is initiated; (2) If the thief acquires ownership of the property after the judgment (e.g., by purchase); (3) If the thief claims that the property is his own; (4) If the value of the stolen property falls below the nisab (minimum threshold) at the time of the claim. The first case is explained: the cutting is dropped because the condition of litigation (الخصومة) is no longer fulfilled; the conflict is resolved. However, if the return occurs after the case has been brought to the judge, cutting still applies because the dispute has already been established. The passage then digresses into a discussion about the giver and recipient of riba, reiterating that both should have the right to litigate according to the author, contrary to some earlier scholars. This indicates that the author favors a broad interpretation of who can be a victim for cutting purposes.

Key Terms
الخصومة (al-khusumah): The legal dispute or litigation between the victim and the thief before a judge.
النصاب (al-nisab): The minimum value of stolen property required for the cutting penalty, set at 10 dirhams in Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following does NOT exempt a thief from the cutting penalty according to the lesson?
A) Returning the property before litigation
B) The value of the stolen property decreasing below nisab
C) The thief purchasing the stolen property after judgment
D) The thief returning the property after the judge has been approached

Key Takeaway
The cutting penalty for theft is not absolute; it is cancelled if the property is returned before litigation, ownership changes, a claim is made, or value drops below nisab.

Stealing from a Thief After Amputation
Ruling on whether a second thief is cut when stealing from a thief after his amputation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on cutting a second thief who steals from an amputated thief, explain the role of taqawwum in the daleel, and distinguish the rulings for theft before and after the first thief's amputation.

Core Idea
A stolen property loses legal value after the first thief's amputation, so a second theft does not trigger cutting.

This lesson examines a specific scenario within the law of theft (sariqa): if one thief has already been amputated for stealing, and then a second thief steals the same property from him, does the hadd of cutting apply to the second thief? The answer hinges on the concept of taqawwum — the legal recognition of property value — and involves a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute. By working through this ruling, you will sharpen your ability to apply conditions for hadd and understand how timing affects liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
State whether the second thief is subject to hand-cutting according to the relied-upon Hanafi position.
The ruling distinguishes between before and after the first thief's amputation.
2
Understand the daleel based on taqawwum
Explain why the property ceases to be mutaqawwim in the hands of the amputated thief, and how this negates the hadd.
Qiyas: just as compensation is not due when the property is destroyed, the hadd is not triggered.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Contrast the unconditional view of al-Karkhi and al-Tahawi with the detailed view in al-Hidayah.
The dispute centers on whether the first thief's hand is a hand of betrayal or merely lost property.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical difference in rulings: cutting applies before amputation but not after.
The judge must verify the timing of the second theft.
5
Apply the ruling to a recovery scenario
Explain who should receive the stolen property according to the text.
The judge does not return it to either thief but to the original owner or the state.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لا بطلب المالك أو السارق لو سرق من سارق بعد القطع) يعني لو قطع سارق بسرقة فسرقت منه لم يكن له ولا لمالك العين المسروقة أن يقطع السارق الثاني لأن المال غير متقوم في حق السارق حتى لا يجب عليه الضمان بالهلاك فلم تنعقد موجبة في نفسها وللأول ولاية الخصومة في الاسترداد لحاجته إذ الرد واجب عليه قيد بقوله «بعد القطع» لأنه لو سرق الثاني قبل أن يقطع الأول أو بعد ما درئ القطع بشبهة يقطع بخصومة الأول لأن سقوط التقوم ضرورة القطع ولم يوجد فصار كالغاصب؛ كذا في الهداية. وأطلق الكرخي والطحاوي عدم قطع السارق من السارق لأن يده ليست يد أمانة ولا ملك فكان ضائعاً ولا قطع في أخذ مال ضائع. قلنا: بقي أن يكون يد غصب والسارق منه يقطع فالحق ما في الهداية من التفصيل واختاره في فتح القدير. في مسألة ولاية الاسترداد أن الوجه أنه إذا ظهر هذا الحال للقاضي لا يرده إلى الأول ولا إلى الثاني إذا رده لظهور خيانة كل منهما، بل يرده من يد الثاني إلى المالك إن كان حاضراً وإلا حفظه كما يحفظ أموال الغيب.
His statement: (Not by the request of the owner or the thief if he steals from a thief after amputation) means: if a thief was amputated for theft and then something was stolen from him, neither he nor the owner of the stolen property may have the second thief amputated, because the property is not considered valuable with respect to the thief, so he is not liable for compensation if it is destroyed, so its value is not established. And the first [thief] has the right of litigation for recovery due to his need, since returning [the property] is obligatory upon him. He restricted with his statement 'after amputation' because if the second steals before the first is amputated, or after the amputation is averted due to a doubt, then the second is amputated by the litigation of the first, because the loss of value is necessitated by the amputation and it did not occur, so he becomes like a usurper. This is from al-Hidayah. Al-Karkhi and al-Tahawi unconditionally stated no amputation for the thief who steals from a thief, because his hand is not a hand of trust nor ownership, so it is lost property, and there is no amputation for taking lost property. We respond: it remains that it is a hand of usurpation, and the one from whom it was stolen is amputated, so the truth is the detail in al-Hidayah, and it was chosen in Fath al-Qadir. Regarding the issue of the right of recovery, the correct view is that when this situation appears to the judge, he should not return it to the first nor to the second, due to the manifest betrayal of both, but rather he returns it from the hand of the second to the owner if present, otherwise he safeguards it as he safeguards the property of absentees.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding a thief who steals from another thief after the first thief's hand has been amputated. The central ruling: neither the original owner nor the first thief (the one amputated) can demand the amputation of the second thief. The reason is that the stolen property loses its monetary value (taqawwum) in the hands of the first thief after amputation; it becomes like a non-valuable item, so the second theft does not trigger the hadd. The passage also discusses a dispute: al-Karkhi and al-Tahawi held an absolute view that no amputation occurs because the first thief's hand is neither a trust nor ownership, hence it's like lost property. The author prefers the Hidayah's detailed distinction: if the second theft occurs before the first's amputation or after a doubt-driven dismissal of amputation, then the second thief is amputated because the property retains its value. Additionally, regarding the right of recovery (wilayat al-istirdad), the judge should not return the stolen item to either thief due to their betrayal; instead, it should go to the original owner or be preserved.

Key Terms
تقوم (taqawwum): The legal recognition of property as having monetary value; a condition for establishing liability and hadd.
ولاية الخصومة (wilayat al-khusumah): The right to initiate litigation regarding the stolen property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) B's hand is cut off because he committed theft.
B) B's hand is not cut off because the phone has lost its value in A's hands.
C) B is cut only if A requests it.
D) B is not cut because the phone is now lost property.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school does not cut a second thief who steals from an amputated thief because the property loses its legal value after amputation, but if the second theft occurs before the first's amputation, cutting is applied.

Four Cases Where Hadd of Cutting Is Not Applied
Four scenarios where the thief is not subject to hand amputation despite theft

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four cases where hand amputation is not applied and identify the legal impediment (māniʿ) in each case.

Core Idea
Four legal impediments prevent the hadd of cutting: return before litigation, ownership after judgment, claim of ownership, and value drop below nisab.

The Hanafi school, following the Quranic verse on cutting the thief's hand (al-Ma'idah 5:38), has defined precise conditions for applying this hadd. Among them are requirements of value, proof, and litigation. However, even when all conditions are met, certain impediments (mawāniʿ) can prevent the penalty. In this passage from al-Bahr al-Rā'iq, the commentator lists four such scenarios and explains their legal reasoning. Understanding these exceptions is crucial for correctly applying Islamic criminal law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four scenarios
Recognize the four cases mentioned in the matn: return before litigation, ownership after judgment, claim of ownership, and decrease in value below nisab.
2
Analyze the legal impediment in each case
For each scenario, identify the specific impediment (māniʿ) that prevents the hadd. For example, return before litigation eliminates the condition of litigation; ownership after judgment creates a doubt; claim of ownership creates a shubha; decrease in value removes the condition of nisab.
The impediments are based on the principle that hadd penalties are repelled by doubts.
3
Distinguish actual and constructive return
Understand that return can be actual (physical return to owner) or constructive (return to someone with a semblance of ownership, e.g., ascendants). Constructive return is limited to those with direct family ties or dependents.
Return to a third party like a descendant's dependent is not sufficient.
4
Understand the timing of litigation
Litigation (khusumah) begins when the case is brought to the judge with a claim and evidence. Return after litigation has begun (e.g., after testimony) does not prevent the hadd. Return before litigation is effective.
The key is whether the theft has been legally manifested before the return.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ومن سرق شيئاً ورده قبل الخصومة إلى مالكه أو ملكه بعد القضاء أو ادعى أنه ملكه أو نقصت قيمته عن النصاب لم يقطع) بيان لأربع مسائل لا قطع فيها: الأولى لو سرق شيئاً ورده قبل الخصومة إلى مالكه فلا قطع لأن الخصومة شرط لظهور السرقة لأن البينة إنما جعلت حجة ضرورة قطع المنازعة وقد انقطعت الخصومة، وقيد بالرد بما قبل الخصومة أي قبل المرافعة إلى القاضي لأنه لو رده بعد المرافعة إلى القاضي قطع لانتهاء الخصومة لحصول مقصودها فتبقى تقديراً؛ كذا في الهداية وهو شامل لما إذا رده بعد القضاء بالقطع وما إذا رده بعد ما شهد الشهود ولم يقض القاضي استحساناً لأن السرقة قد ظهرت عند القاضي بما هو حجة بناء على خصومة معتبرة؛ كذا في التبيين. فالمراد بالخصومة الدعوى والشهادة أو الإقرار، فلو ادعى ولم يثبت ثم رده ينبغي أن لا قطع لعدم ظهورها عند القاضي فهي رباعية لأن الرد إما أن يكون بعد الترافع إلى القاضي قبل الدعوى أو بعدها قبل الثبوت أو بعدهما قبل القضاء أو بعد الثلاثة، فلا قطع في الأوليين ويقطع في الأخرين. وأطلق في الرد فشمل الرد حقيقة والرد حكماً كما إذا رده إلى أصوله وإن علا كوالده وجده ووالدته وجدته، سواء كانوا في عيال المالك أو لا، لأن لهؤلاء شبهة الملك فيثبت به شبهة الرد بخلاف ما إذا رده إلى عيال أصوله فإنه يقطع لأنه شبهة الشبهة وهي غير معتبرة. ومن الرد الحكمي إليه الرد إلى فرعه وكل ذي رحم محرم منه بشرط أن يكون في عياله وإلا فليس برد ومنه الرد إلى مكاتبه وعبده، ومنه الرد إلى مولاه لو كان مكاتباً لأن ماله له رقبة ومنه إذا سرق من العيال ورد إلى من يعولهم لأن يده عليهم فوق أيديهم في ماله. الثانية لو ملكه بعد القضاء بالقطع فلان الإمضاء من القضاء في هذا الباب لوقوع الاستغناء عنه بالاستيفاء إذ القضاء للإظهار والقطع حق الله تعالى وهو ظاهر عنده، وإذا كان كذلك يشترط قيام الخصومة عند الاستيفاء وصار كما إذا ملكها منه قبل القضاء أطلقه فشمل البيع والهبة لكن يشترط القبض فيها ليحصل الملك كما في الهداية الثالثة لو ادعى السارق أن المسروق ملكه بعد ما ثبتت السرقة عليه بالبينة أو بالإقرار فلا قطع سواء أقام بينة أو لم يقم، لأن الشبهة دارئة للحد فتتحقق بمجرد الدعوى بدليل صحة الرجوع بعد الإقرار الرابعة إذا سرق شيئاً قيمته نصاب ثم نقصت قيمته بعد القضاء لم يقطع لأن كمال النصاب لما كان شرطاً يشترط قيامه عند الإمضاء لما ذكرنا. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا تغير السعر في بلد أو بلدين حتى إذا سرق ما قيمته نصاب في بلد وأخذ في بلد آخر القيمة فيه أنقص لم يقطع كما في شرح الطحاوي. وقيد بنقصان القيمة لأن العين لو نقصت فإنه يقطع لأنه مضمون عليه فكمل النصاب عيناً وديناً كما إذا استهلكه كله، أما نقصان السعر فغير مضمون فافترقا.
His statement: (And whoever steals something and returns it before litigation to its owner, or comes to own it after judgment, or claims that it is his property, or its value decreases below the nisab, he is not cut) is an explanation of four issues in which there is no cutting: First: if he steals something and returns it before litigation to its owner, then no cutting because litigation is a condition for the manifestation of theft, since the evidence is made a proof out of necessity to end the dispute, and the litigation has ended. He restricted return to before litigation, i.e., before going to the judge, because if he returns it after going to the judge, he is cut because the litigation has ended due to attaining its purpose, so it remains estimated. This is from al-Hidayah, and it includes the case where he returns it after the judgment of amputation and the case where he returns it after the witnesses testified but the judge has not yet judged, by istihsan, because the theft has become evident to the judge through proof based on valid litigation. This is from al-Tabyin. So by litigation is meant the claim, testimony, or confession. So if he claimed but did not prove, then returned, it should be that there is no cutting because [the theft] has not become evident to the judge. So it is fourfold: the return is either after going to the judge before the claim, or after it before proof, or after both before judgment, or after all three. There is no cutting in the first two, and cutting in the last two. He made the return absolute, including actual return and constructive return, such as returning to his ascendants even if high, like his father, grandfather, mother, grandmother, whether they are dependents of the owner or not, because these have a semblance of ownership, so a semblance of return is established, unlike if he returns to the dependents of his ascendants, then he is cut because it is a semblance of a semblance, which is not considered. Among constructive return is returning to his descendant and every unmarriageable relative (mahram) provided that he is in his care, otherwise it is not [constructive] return. Also, returning to his mukatab or his slave, and returning to his master if he is a mukatab because his property belongs to the master. Also, if he steals from dependents and returns to the one who supports them, because his authority over them is above their authority over his property. Second: if he comes to own it after judgment of amputation, because the implementation of the judgment in this chapter is for the purpose of dispensing with it by execution, since the judgment is for manifestation, and amputation is a right of Allah which is evident to him. When this is the case, the existence of litigation at the time of execution is required, and it becomes like the case where he came to own it before judgment. He made it absolute, including sale and gift, but possession is required therein for ownership to occur, as in al-Hidayah. Third: if the thief claims that the stolen item is his property after theft has been established against him by evidence or confession, then there is no cutting, whether he produces evidence or not, because doubt repels the hadd, and it is realized by mere claim, as evidenced by the validity of retraction after confession. Fourth: if he steals something worth the nisab, then its value decreases after the judgment, he is not cut, because the completeness of the nisab is a condition and its existence at the time of execution is required for what we mentioned. He made it absolute, including if the price changes in one country or two, so if he steals something worth nisab in a country and is taken in another country where the value is less, he is not cut, as in Sharh al-Tahawi. He restricted it to decrease in value because if the item itself decreases, he is cut because he is liable for it, so the nisab is complete in kind and value, as if he consumed it all. As for decrease in price, it is not liable, so they differ.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement enumerating four cases where the thief is not subject to hand amputation. The first case: returning the stolen item before litigation (khusumah). Litigation here means the formal claim and evidence before a judge; return after the judge has heard evidence or passed judgment results in amputation. The second case: if the thief comes to own the item after the judge has ruled for amputation—ownership can be through purchase, gift, etc., but requires possession. The third case: if the thief claims the item is his own after the theft is established; the mere claim creates a doubt that averts the hadd, even without evidence. The fourth case: if the value of the stolen item drops below the nisab after the judge's ruling but before execution; amputation is not carried out because the condition of nisab must exist at execution. The passage also details constructive return (radd hukmi), such as returning to one's parents or children in one's care, which suffices to avoid amputation.

Key Terms
الخصومة (al-khusumah): Legal litigation before a judge, including the claim and presentation of evidence.
الرد الحكمي (al-radd al-hukmi): Constructive return of stolen property to someone with a semblance of ownership, such as ascendants or dependents.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the four cases where cutting is not applied?
A) Return before litigation
B) Ownership after judgment
C) Confession of theft after proof
D) Value drop below nisab

Key Takeaway
The hadd of cutting is not applied in four specific situations; the most critical nuance is the timing of return relative to litigation.

Two Thieves Confess, Then One Retracts and Claims Ownership
Ruling on two thieves who confess, then one claims item is his

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when one thief retracts after joint confession and explain why the doubt (shubha) averts the hadd for both thieves.

Core Idea
One thief's retraction creates a doubt that saves both from the cut.

In Hanafi fiqh, the principle that 'doubts avert hudud' is applied broadly. This lesson examines a specific scenario: two thieves confess to a theft, then one retracts and claims the stolen item is his own property. The Hanafi ruling is that neither thief is punished by amputation of the hand, because the retraction creates a legal doubt that extends to both. Understanding this case illuminates how the Hanafi school prioritizes certainty in evidence for capital and corporal punishments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their statements
Note that two thieves both confess to the theft. Then one later says 'it is my property.' This retraction (ruju') and claim of ownership is the key event.
2
Recognize the ruling
The ruling is that neither thief is cut (amputation of hand is not applied). This is the clear statement from the text.
The ruling applies whether the retraction occurs before or after the judge's verdict.
3
Understand the legal reasoning
The retraction is effective for the retractor because he denies the theft. Additionally, it creates a legal doubt (shubha) for the other thief because the original confession established joint theft, and now the ownership is uncertain.
The principle 'al-hudud tudra' bi al-shubuhat' (hudud are averted by doubts) is invoked.
4
Compare with the boundary case
If only one person confesses and names an accomplice who denies, then only the confessor is cut. This is because there is no joint confession, so no doubt extends to the confessor.
This distinction shows the importance of joint confession for the doubt to spread.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولو أقرا بسرقة ثم قال أحدهما هو مالي لم يقطعا) أي السارقان المقران لأن الرجوع عامل في حق الراجع ومورث للشبهة في حق الآخر لأن السرقة قد ثبتت بإقرارهما على الشركة أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان قبل القضاء أو بعده وقيد بإقرارهما لأنه لو أقر أنه سرق هو وفلان كذا فأنكر فلان فإنه يقطع المقر لعدم الشركة بتكذيبه بقوله قتلت أنا وفلان الفتح.
His statement: (And if both confess to a theft, then one of them says 'it is my property,' they are not cut) meaning the two thieves who confessed, because the retraction is effective regarding the one who retracts and creates a doubt regarding the other, because the theft has been established by their joint confession. He made it absolute, including whether [the retraction] is before or after the judgment. And he restricted it to their confession, because if he confessed that he and so-and-so stole such-and-such, but so-and-so denied it, then the confessor is cut because there is no partnership due to the other's denial, as in the saying 'I and so-and-so killed' [the confessor alone is punished]. From al-Fath.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement about two thieves who both confess to a theft. If after the confession one of them says 'the stolen item is my property,' then neither thief is to have his hand cut. The reason is that the retraction (ruju') by one creates a legal doubt (shubha) that prevents the hadd for both. The retraction is effective for the one who retracts because he denies the theft, and it creates doubt for the other because the original confession established a joint theft, but now the status of the property is ambiguous. This applies whether the retraction occurs before or after the judge's verdict. However, if only one person confesses and names another who denies, then only the confessor is cut, because the denial of the other negates the partnership. This is analogous to confession of murder: one saying 'I and so-and-so killed' while the other denies results in punishment only for the confessor.

Key Terms
الرجوع (al-ruju'): Retraction of a confession or claim by a thief after initial confession.
الشبهة (al-shubhah): A legal doubt that prevents the application of a hadd punishment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Two thieves, A and B, both confess to stealing a laptop. Later, before the judgment, A says 'the laptop is actually mine.' What is the Hanafi ruling?
A) Both A and B have their hands cut.
B) Only A's hand is cut because he retracted.
C) Only B's hand is cut because A's claim creates doubt for A, but B is still guilty.
D) Neither A nor B has his hand cut.

Key Takeaway
A retraction by one joint thief claiming ownership creates a legal doubt that prevents the hadd punishment for both thieves in Hanafi law.

Hadd and Guarantee Do Not Combine
Principle that amputation and financial liability cannot coexist for theft.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that hadd amputation and financial liability (daman) are mutually exclusive for theft, and distinguish between returning the stolen item (return of the same) and paying its value (daman).

Core Idea
Hadd amputation cancels financial liability; only the physical item must be returned if it still exists.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq presents a key rule in Hanafi hudud: after a thief's hand is amputated, he is not required to pay the value of the stolen property. However, if the stolen item itself remains intact, it must be returned to its owner. The ruling is grounded in a specific hadith and rational reasoning that shows the incompatibility of hadd and daman. Understanding this principle is essential for correctly applying the law of theft.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle and its scope
The principle is that hadd (amputation) and daman (financial guarantee) cannot be combined for theft. The thief either suffers amputation or pays; they are mutually exclusive. The text also adds that if the stolen item (al-ayn) is still in existence (qā’ima), it must be returned.
This principle applies only after the hadd has been executed.
2
Examine the primary proof (dalīl naqlī)
The proof is the hadith: 'There is no liability on the thief after his right hand is cut off.' This directly negates financial liability after amputation.
The hadith is reported by al-Nasa’i and others; its transmission is ahad (zannī al-thubūt).
3
Analyze the rational reasoning (ta‘līl)
The commentary explains that requiring payment would imply that the thief owned the item through payment (by attributing the payment back to the time of taking). This would mean the taking was from his own property, negating the theft and thus the amputation. Therefore, what leads to the negation of hadd is itself negated.
Alternative reasoning: the hand (the locus of the hadd) is not protected as a property right of the thief, so no compensation is due.
4
Understand the exception: return of the physical item
The text explicitly states 'and the physical item is returned if it remains extant.' This is not daman but simply restoring the same property. It does not involve paying a price, so it is allowed even after amputation.
If the item has been consumed or destroyed, no liability arises because that would be daman.
5
Compare with opposing view (Shāfi‘ī)
The Shāfi‘ī school holds that the thief must pay damages in addition to amputation. The Hanafi position is based on the hadith and the logical contradiction between hadd and daman.
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilāf) is clear: in Shāfi‘ī law, the victim receives compensation; in Hanafi law, only the item itself is returned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولا يجتمع قطع وضمان وترد العين لو قائمة) لقوله عليه السلام لا غرم على السارق بعد ما قطعت يمينه»(۱) ولأن وجوب الضمان ينافي القطع لأنه يتملكه بأداء الضمان مسنداً إلى وقت الأخذ فتبين أنه ورد على ملكه فينتفي القطع، وما يؤدي إلى انتفائه فهو المنتفي أو لأن المحل لا يبقى معصوماً حقاً للعبد إذ ليخاصم فليتأمل اهـ.
His saying: 'And amputation and guarantee do not combine, and the physical item is returned if it remains extant' — because of his saying, peace be upon him: 'There is no liability on the thief after his right hand is cut off.' And because the obligation of guarantee contradicts amputation, because he (the thief) owns it by paying the guarantee attributed back to the time of taking, so it becomes clear that he took it on his own ownership, so the amputation is negated. And that which leads to its negation is itself negated. Or because the locus (the hand) does not remain protected as a right of the slave (the thief), since it does not litigate. So let one ponder.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage establishes the principle that the hadd of amputation and financial guarantee (damages) cannot be combined in theft. The proof is a hadith: 'There is no liability on the thief after his hand is cut off.' The commentary explains that if the thief were required to pay the value of the stolen item, that would imply that he owned it (by paying consideration), which would negate the fact that he stole, and thus the amputation would be unfounded. Therefore, if the amputation is carried out, the financial liability drops. However, if the stolen item is still in existence (qaima), it must be returned to the owner because it does not involve paying a price but merely restoring the property. This distinction is crucial: return of the same item is allowed, but liability for value (dam) is not combined with the hadd.

Key Terms
الضمان (al-daman): Financial guarantee or liability to pay the value of the stolen item.
القطع (al-qat‘): Amputation of the hand as a hadd punishment for theft.
العين (al-‘ayn): The stolen item itself, as opposed to its value or a substitute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, after a thief's hand is amputated for theft, what is the ruling on the stolen item?
A) The thief must pay its value in addition to amputation
B) The thief is free from all liability and keeps the item
C) The thief must return the same item if it still exists, but is not liable for its value
D) The thief owes nothing unless the owner demands compensation

Key Takeaway
Hadd amputation eliminates financial liability for theft; only the stolen item itself is returned if still extant.
