Hadd of Theft: Effect of Shubha and Absence of a Thief
Matn ruling and commentary on cutting for two thieves with shubha or absence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on cutting the hand of a thief when a co-thief claims ownership or is absent, and explain the role of shubha and absence as bars to the hadd.

Core Idea
A thief's claim of ownership creates shubha that suspends the hadd for all involved; absence of one thief does not create shubha for the present one.

The hadd of theft is a severe penalty, but it is suspended when there is any doubt (shubha) regarding the ownership of the stolen property. This lesson examines two scenarios from the Hanafi text: when one thief claims the property is his, and when one thief is absent at trial. The commentary clarifies that only actual shubha, not hypothetical possibilities, can prevent the cutting.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the matn ruling: first scenario where one thief says 'it is my property' after confession; second where two thieves steal but one is absent.
These are two distinct cases with different outcomes.
2
Understand the principle of shubha
The commentator explains that any claim creating doubt (shubha) about the stolen property drops the cutting from both thieves. This is because the hadd is prevented by uncertainty.
Shubha can arise from any claim, not just ownership.
3
Contrast with absence
In the absence case, the present thief is cut because the absent thief's non-existence does not generate shubha for the present one. The mere possibility that the absent one might later claim is 'shubha of a shubha' and not considered.
Actual shubha must exist, not be hypothetical.
4
Apply the reasoning
Consider the logic: shubha from a claim is real and bars the hadd for both; absence is procedural and only affects the absent thief's case, not the present one's.
The ruling highlights Hanafi strictness: only concrete doubts matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب السرقة. النصاب لم يقطع ولو أقر بسرقة ثم قال أحدهما هو مالي لم يقطعا ولو سرقا وغاب أحدهما وشهدا على سرقتهما قطع الآخر. قوله: قال أحدهما هو مالي تمثيل وإلا فالمراد أن أحدهما إذا ادعى شبهة أي شبهة كانت فإنه يسقط القطع عنهما كما في شرح الطحاوي قوله: (ولو سرقا وغاب أحدهما وشهد على سرقتهما قطع الآخر) أي الحاضر لأن الغيبة تمنع ثبوت السرقة على الغائب فيبقى معدوماً والعدم لا يورث الشبهة، ولا معتبر بتوهم حدوث الشبهة لأنه شبهة الشبهة وبيانه أن الغائب لو حضر وادعى كان شبهة للحاضر واحتمال دعوى الغائب شبهة الشبهة فلا تعتبر.
The book of theft. The nisab is not cut (if not reached). And if he confessed to theft then one of them said 'it is my property', they are not cut. And if two stole and one of them was absent, and they gave testimony regarding their theft, the other is cut. His statement: 'one of them said it is my property' is an example, otherwise the meaning is that if one of them claims shubha (doubt), any shubha, then the cutting is dropped from both, as in Sharh al-Tahawi. His statement: 'and if two stole and one of them was absent, and testimony was given regarding their theft, the other is cut' meaning the present one, because absence prevents the establishment of theft against the absent one, so he remains non-existent, and non-existence does not create shubha. And not considered is the possibility of shubha arising, because it is shubha of a shubha. Its clarification is that if the absent one were present and claimed, it would be a shubha for the present one, and the probability of the absent one's claim is shubha of a shubha, so it is not considered.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq regarding the application of the hadd of theft when multiple thieves are involved. The first part states that if the stolen property does not reach the nisab (the minimum threshold), the hand is not cut. Then it addresses a scenario where a thief confesses to theft but later claims the property was his own: in that case, both thieves are not cut because the claim creates a shubha (doubt) that prevents the hadd. The commentator clarifies that this is not limited to a claim of ownership but any type of shubha suffices to drop the penalty from both thieves, as established in Sharh al-Tahawi. The second part deals with a situation where two thieves stole, but one is absent. Testimony is given against both, but only the present thief's hand is cut. The reason is that absence prevents the establishment of theft against the absent one, and his non-existence does not generate shubha. The mere possibility that the absent thief might later claim shubha (if he were present) is considered 'shubha of a shubha' and is therefore not taken into account. This ruling applies strictly Hanafi reasoning, emphasizing that only actual, not hypothetical, shubha can suspend the hadd.

Key Terms
شبهة (shubha): A claim or doubt that creates ambiguity regarding the stolen property, which suspends the hadd penalty.
الغيبة (al-ghayba): The absence of a thief, which prevents the court from establishing the theft against that person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi text, if two thieves steal and one of them is absent, what is the ruling on cutting the hand of the present thief?
A) Both are cut
B) Neither is cut
C) The present thief is cut
D) The absent thief is cut if he returns

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, only actual shubha (doubt) bars the hadd of theft; absence of a co-thief is not shubha, and hypothetical future claims are disregarded.
